


KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Mai v. Gonzales, 5th Cir., December 13, 2006
2004 WL 2943580 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAI THUY-XUAN A.K.A. THUY XUAN MAI
File: AXX XX6 959 - San Antonio
November 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martha E. Garza, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Allen Reid Tilson,
Deputy Chief Counsel
*1 Now before the Board of Immigration Appeals is the respondent’s motion filed with us on September 29, 2004. We will address the motion, which is captioned as a motion to reconsider, as for both reconsideration and reopening. The motion will be denied.
 
This case was before the Board last on August 30, 2004, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen filed with us on May 19, 2004. In our prior decision dated February 26, 2004, we summarily affirmed an Immigration Judge’s November 1, 2002, decision, which found the respondent removable; denied his applications for waivers of inadmissibility under sections 212(c) and 212(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1182(c) and 1182(i); denied his motion for a continuance and his motion to withdraw an admission of an allegation concerning his false claim to United States citizenship; and ordered him removed from the United States to Vietnam.
 
With respect to reconsideration, we find that this motion must be denied because the respondent has not demonstrated any error in our decision of August 30, 2004, on the record then before us, upon consideration of the contentions in the motion. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). In our August 2004 decision, we made the following determinations. The respondent did not establish prejudice by his former attorney, with regard to his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent also did not establish eligibility for a section 212(c) waiver or cancellation of removal, and he could not couple both to waive his inadmissibility.
 
With respect to reopening, we find that this motion must be denied because it is an impermissible multiple motion to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (2004) (limiting motions to reopen to a single such motion, whether filed before the Board or the Immigration Judge). As we already have stated, we denied the respondenT’s previous motion to reopen in our decision of August 30, 2004. We also find that the respondent has not demonstrated eligibility for any of the regulatory exceptions to the time and number restrictions on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3) (2004). In addition, we do not find that sua sponte reopening is warranted for any reason. See Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997); see also Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999).
 
Even if we were to reach the merits of the present motion to reopen, we would deny it, based on the following rationale. The respondent has not demonstrated prima facie eligibility for the newly requested relief. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988); INS v. Jong Ha Wang, 450 U.S. 139 (1981) (per curiam). Consistent with the DHS’s position in its opposition memorandum, the respondent, who is already a lawful permanent resident, does not qualify to apply for adjustment of status in conjunction with a waiver under section 209(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1159. Cf. Matter of K-A-, 23 I&N Dec. 661 (BIA 2004) (confirming an Immigration Judge’s jurisdiction to adjudicate an asylee’s application for adjustment of status in conjunction with a waiver under section 209(c)).
 
*2 Accordingly, we will deny the motion to consider and reopen.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied, and the motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2943553 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROSA ELVIA ZAMUNDIO-LOPEZ FERNANDO LEGIS-ZAMUNDIO MARIA DE LOS ANGELES LEGIS-ZAMUNDIO
Files: A76 610 962 - Los Angeles
A76 610 963
A76 610 964
November 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luis Carlos Ayala, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This matter was last before Board on April 23, 2004, when we concluded that the respondents’ decision to waive appeal was knowingly and intelligently made, and thus, the Board did not have jurisdiction to consider their appeal.1 The respondents have filed a timely motion to reconsider and reopen to present new evidence regarding their claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not filed a response to the motion. The motion will be denied.2
 
In our prior decision, the Board concluded that the respondents’ decision to waive appeal was knowingly and intelligently made. Moreover, the prior decision addressed the respondents attempt to present an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. In this regard, it was first determined that the respondent had not complied with any of the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and that the record before the Board did not establish that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
The respondents, through new counsel, assert in their motion that the Board erred in requiring them to comply with the requirements set forth in with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra, since the attorney who represented them before the Immigration Judge was not licensed to practice law. The respondents, for the first time, have submitted evidence that their prior attorney had resigned on September 15, 1996, from the State Bar of California. The respondents asserts that they were not given a full and fair hearing and were prejudiced by the lack of preparation of former counsel. In this regard, the respondents, relying upon the advice of the attorney, requested voluntary departure prior to the conclusion of proceedings which then precluded them from any relief.
 
In considering this issue, we note that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such.3 See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). The Ninth Circuit has also held that if an alien is ineffectively represented by a non-lawyer who purports to be a licensed attorney, the alien is not barred from proving an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. See Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999); Varela v. INS, supra. Further, the alien must show that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. Id.
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondents have pointed to their prior representatives advice to seek and accept a grant of voluntary departure prior to the conclusion of their proceedings as provided under section 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), as evidence of ineffective assistance. However, based upon the record before the Board, voluntary departure was and is the only form of relief from removal available to the respondents. The respondents do not qualify for cancellation of removal as provided by section 240A(b) of the Act since they do not have the requisite physical presence prior to service of the Notice to Appear. See section 240A(d)(1) of the Act. The respondents have not indicated a fear of returning to Mexico pursuant to the provisions of section 208 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1158, or presented that they are eligible for any other forms of relief from removal. Thus, since voluntary departure was the only form of relief the respondents were eligible to pursue before the Immigration Judge, their representative’s advice to pursue voluntary departure was proper.
 
In sum, although the respondents were represented by someone who was not licensed to practice law at the time he represented them before the Immigration Judge, his representation of the respondents was not ineffective as to affect the outcome. Accordingly, the motion will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

A separate decision has been issued relating to the respondents’ husband/father (Aurelian Legis-Alvarez, A92 810 793).

2

The respondents indicate in their motion that the Immigration Judge failed to make a determination regarding an apparent request to withdraw representation made by the former attorney. However, the transcript reveals that, upon the former attorney’s indication that he may need to withdraw his representation, the Immigration Judge directed the attorney to consult and follow the procedures set forth in the court’s local rules (Tr. at 9-10). It appears that the former attorney never filed a motion to withdraw with the court.

3

In Lozada, supra, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved applicant setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the applicant in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640.

2004 WL 2952194 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ESPITIA, ISMAEL
File: AXX-XX5-909 - San Diego
November 5, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pollack, Linda G., Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy J. Cody,
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen and remand proceedings for consideration of an application for cancellation of removal. The Board will reopen these removal proceedings on our motion, and the record will be remanded so that the respondent can apply for cancellation of removal.
 
In a decision dated October 2, 2002, the respondent was found removable as charged, and to have abandoned any potential applications for relief, and was ordered removed from the United States. The decision was issued after a hearing held in absentia due to the respondent’s failure to appear. See section 240(b)(5)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(A).
 
On October 22, 2002, the respondent filed a motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge. The motion was denied on December 5, 2002. The Immigration Judge found that the respondent had proper notice of his hearing, and did not establish that exceptional circumstances existed which should excuse his failure to appear. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. The respondent appealed to the Board. The appeal was dismissed on November 19, 2003.
 
On February 5, 2004, the respondent filed the motion to reopen which is pending. The respondent wants the record reopened and remanded, due to exceptional circumstances based on ineffective assistance of counsel, so that his application for cancellation of removal can be considered. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has submitted a reply brief, stating its non-opposition to the respondent’s motion.
 
As previously indicated, we will reopen the respondent’s proceedings sua sponte. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). This eliminates any concern that the respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. Accordingly, we will reopen the proceedings and remand the record to the Immigration Judge for consideration of the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal.
 
ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pollack, Linda G., Esquire

Tracy J. Cody,

*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen and remand proceedings for consideration of an application for cancellation of removal. The Board will reopen these removal proceedings on our motion, and the record will be remanded so that the respondent can apply for cancellation of removal.
 
In a decision dated October 2, 2002, the respondent was found removable as charged, and to have abandoned any potential applications for relief, and was ordered removed from the United States. 2004 WL 2952190 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SHAUN GWINNETT BOMPAS
File: AXX XX7 019 - Baltimore
November 4, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nelson A. Vargas-Padilla
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(A), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(A)º - Inadmissible at time of entry or adjustment of status under section 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
 
Sec. 237(a)(1)(D)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(D)(i)º - Conditional resident status terminated
 
  
APPLICATION: Voluntary departure
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge dated July 16, 2003, denying the respondent’s application for voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b), and ordering him removed to the United Kingdom. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
Although the respondent indicated in his Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-27) that he would submit a separate brief on appeal, he has failed to do so. Thus, we are limited to the arguments raised on appeal.
 
The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his removal hearing because his prior attorney did not submit documents within the Immigration Court’s guidelines. However, the respondent has failed to establish what documents he would have submitted and how the consideration of these documents would have warranted a grant of his application for voluntary departure. The Immigration Judge properly relied on the respondent’s immigration history and criminal record in the United States to deny the respondent’s application for voluntary departure in the exercise of discretion. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b); Matter of Arguelles, 22 I&N Dec. 811 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Cordova, 22 I&N Dec. 966 (BIA 1999).
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2943531 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE SALUD CISNEROS-CEBALLOS
File: A95 592 299 - San Francisco
November 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Rhoda Wilkinson Domingo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sherry A. Nohara
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Motion to reopen
 
The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s request for cancellation of removal and granted the respondent voluntary departure on July 24, 2003. On February 10, 2004, the Board summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal of that decision. Currently, the respondent requests that his case be reopened and remanded in light of new evidence that he claims establishes that exceptional and extremely unusual hardship would befall his lawful permanent resident parents upon his removal and further claims that his case should be reopened because he received ineffective assistance of counsel. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion.
 
We will first review the portion of the respondent’s motion that is based upon ineffective assistance of counsel. We note that the respondent’s argument rests upon two alleged failures of his prior attorney. As each count stems from independent and separate aspects of the respondent’s claim, we will review each count in turn.
 
A motion to reopen based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel requires (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond, and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). An alien must also show that the proceeding was so fundamentally unfair that the he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case and that he was prejudiced by his representative’s performance. Id. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, the jurisdiction under which this case arises, has found, however, that aliens need not strictly adhere to the requirements of Matter of Lozada, especially when there is a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance.”1 See, e.g., Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002).
 
*2 First, the respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance when his prior attorney failed to timely submit evidence concerning his daughter’s physical health. The respondent argues that this failure resulted in prejudice because the Immigration Judge’s denial was based in part upon the fact that the “Court could not determine how serious the condition is, and nothing in the record suggests that the respondent’s daughter could not get the necessary medications in Mexico for her condition.” (I.J. at 6). Although the respondent is correct in that his prior attorney failed to submit this highly relevant evidence, he has nonetheless failed to establish prejudice because the documents his attorney failed to submit did not remedy the Immigration Court’s concerns, that were affirmed by this Board, regarding the lack of evidence proving that his daughter would not receive medication in Mexico. Accordingly, we cannot find that the respondent received ineffective assistance under these facts.
 
The respondent additionally argues that his prior attorney provided ineffective assistance when he failed to submit evidence to the Board, while his appeal was pending, pertaining to his parents’ recent change in status to lawful permanent residents. He argues that his parents are now qualifying relatives that he can establish exceptional and extremely exceptional hardship through. Again, the respondent must prove that he was prejudiced by his attorney’s performance. Matter of Lozada, surpa. Inasmuch as the respondent’s current attorney was able to timely file a motion to reopen before the Board containing the evidence the respondent would like considered, we cannot find that the respondent was prejudiced.
 
Finally, the respondent was granted voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). If an alien fails to voluntarily depart in the time period specified in the order, the alien will be subject to the civil penalties provided in section 240B(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d). See Shaar v. INS, 141 F.3d 953 (9th Cir. 1998); Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541, 544-46 (BIA 1996). These penalties include becoming ineligible for relief under section 240A for a period of 10 years. We note that the respondent was provided adequate notice of these penalties in our March 10, 2004, decision. 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b) (requiring notice to alien of penalties in order). The respondent has failed to submit evidence establishing that an extension was requested and granted by the district director. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(f) (2002) (providing that the authority to extend a voluntary departure period “is within the sole jurisdiction of the district director”). Without evidence of an extension, we must find that the respondent is not prima facie eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Act. Given our finding in this regard, we do not find it appropriate to reopen and remand proceedings based upon the respondent’s request for a hearing on the merits of his application for cancellation of removal.
 
*3 Based on the foregoing, we will deny the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that the respondent’s motion to reopen states that he provided his prior attorney notice of the allegations against him and that a copy of such notice was provided with the motion. Upon review of the motion, we note that the respondent has failed to include this evidence. However, considering that the Ninth Circuit does not require strict adherence, but rather substantial compliance with Matter of Lozada, we will consider the merits of the respondent’s motion. Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002) (finding compliance where alien provided affidavit explaining agreement and filed a complaint).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

IN RE: CARLOS PACHECO DEL ANGEL

November 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lisa S. Brodyaga, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I) º - Crime involving moral turpitude
  
APPLICATION: Reopening; cancellation of removal
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This record was last before us on November 6, 2003, when we summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal from the decision of an Immigration Judge, dated May 25, 2000, which denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(a). We summarily dismissed the respondent’s appeal under the provisions of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(2)(i)(E), because the respondent had indicated his intention to file a separate written brief or statement in support of his appeal, but failed to do so within the time set for filing. The respondent has filed a timely motion to reopen his proceedings, asserting that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his proceedings before the Immigration Court and on appeal.
 
Subsequently, by motion filed on October 7, 2004, the respondent has informed this Board that, on October 4, 2004, the United States District Court, Southern District of Texas, granted the respondent’s petition for writ of habeas corpus and determined that the respondent should be allowed to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(a) of the Act. The respondent requests that these proceedings be reopened and remanded to the Immigration Court for further consideration of his eligibility, for relief. The motion to reopen is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings to consider the respondent’s eligibility for cancellation of removal.

2004 WL 2943526 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PATRICIA CARREON SALVADOR CARREON
Files: AXX XX1 413 - San Francisco
AXX XX7 794
October 29, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Lina M. Alta, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Deborah K. Goodwin
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Arrived at a time or place not designated by the Attorney General (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; remand
 
An Immigration Judge found the respondents removable as charged and denied their application for cancellation of removal, but granted their request for voluntary departure under sections 240A(b), and 240B of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229b(a), and 1229c. On appeal the respondents argue that the Immigration Judge erred in finding they had failed to demonstrate the requisite hardship for cancellation of removal. The respondents have also filed a motion to remand the record based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel at the hearing below. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion denied.
 
Section 240A(b) of the Act provides that the Attorney General may cancel the removal of an alien who is inadmissible or deportable if the alien:
(A) has been physically present in the United States for a continuous period of not less than 10 years immediately preceding the date of such application;
 
(B) has been a person of good moral character during such period;
 
(C) has not been convicted of an offense under section 212(a)(2), 237(a)(2), or 237(a)(3); and
 
(D) establishes that removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to the alien’s spouse, parent, or child, who is a citizen of the United States or an alien lawfully admitted for permanent residence.
 
Section 240A(b) of the Act.
 
The respondents have three United States citizen children, born in 1990, 1995, and 1997. The lead respondent testified that they were all three in good health and were doing well in school (Tr. at 95-97). At the time of the hearing the lead respondent was receiving worker’s compensation for an injury and his spouse, the second respondent, was employed. Reviewing both the evidence presented to the Immigration Judge and, as discussed below, the evidence submitted with the motion to remand, we agree that the respondents have failed to demonstrate that their removal would result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to their children. As we have held, this standard is substantially beyond that which would ordinarily be expected to result from removal. The respondents have not demonstrated this level of hardship. Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001); see also Matter of Recinas, 23 I&N Dec. 467 (BIA 2002).
 
*2 In the motion to remand, the respondents argue that their previous attorney did not properly present the evidence in their case. They allege that the attorney should have submitted information regarding the lead respondent’s health, evidence of the children’s difficulty in school, and the respondent’s tax returns. With the motion, the respondents have submitted a letter from a doctor indicating that the respondent is recovering from back surgery and his activities are currently limited. They have also submitted school letters indicating that their Mexican born child and the oldest United States citizen child are in special education classes. They have not submitted any additional tax documentation.
 
We set forth the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In addition to other requirements, it is incumbent on the claimant to establish that he or she suffered prejudice as a result of the attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. To satisfy the requirements of due process of law, an alien must be provided a fair hearing. United States v. Torres-Sanchez, 68 F.3d 227 (8th Cir. 1995); Gandarillas-Zambrana v. BIA, 44 F.3d 1251 (4th Cir. 1995); United States v. Mendoza-Lopez, 7 F.3d 1483 (10th Cir. 1993); Michelson v. INS, 897 F.2d 465 (10th Cir. 1990); Nicholas v. INS, 590 F.2d 802 (9th Cir. 1979); Matter of D-, 20 I&N Dec. 827 (BIA 1994); Matter of Tomas, 19 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1987); Matter of Exilus, 18 I&N Dec. 276 (BIA 1982). In order to show denial of a fair hearing, an alien must demonstrate that he or she has suffered prejudice. United States v. Torres-Sanchez, supra; Gandarillas-Zambrana v. BIA, supra; Michelson v. INS, supra; United States v. Mendoza-Lopez, supra; Nicholas v. INS, supra; Matter of D-, supra; Matter of Exilus, supra. Considering the evidence below and the minimal documentation submitted with the motion, we find that the United States citizen children would not face hardship that rises to the level of exceptional and extremely unusual. Accordingly, the motion will be denied, the decision of the Immigration Judge affirmed and the appeal dismissed.
 
*3 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the decision of the Immigration Judge affirmed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event a respondent fails to so depart, that respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If a respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, that respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CHEN, SI
File: AXX-XX9-434 - Philadelphia
October 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Pignone III, Marco, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ira L. Mazer,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated April 30, 2003. On appeal, the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge improperly entered an order of removal instead of continuing proceedings to allow her to file a Form I-751 (petition to remove conditions on permanent residence). The respondent also moves the Board to remand proceedings for consideration of the I-751, as well as her application for adjustment of status based on her marriage to Yun Guang Zhang, who was granted adjustment of status under the Chinese Student Protection Act on November 22, 2002. See Motion to Remand, Exh. 1; see also Respondent’s Brief at Exh. B. The respondent correctly points out that as a spouse of one who has been granted status under the CSPA, she is presumptively eligible for adjustment of status. In her motion, the respondent argues that her former counsel was ineffective in failing to file the I-751 within the time frame set by the Immigration Judge; this prevented her from defending herself against charges of a fraudulent first marriage and from timely filing her I-751 and application for adjustment, both based upon her current marriage to Mr. Zhang. The respondent provided an affidavit setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with prior counsel. See Motion to Remand at Exh. 3. The respondent also indicates in her motion that she has notified prior counsel of her grievance and that she has filed a complaint with the Pennsylvania Office of Disciplinary Counsel. See Respondent’s Motion to Remand; Supplement to Motion to Remand. Accordingly, we find that the respondent has substantially complied with the procedural requirements for raising an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). To advance a successful claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate prejudice, i.e., she “must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting ¿herº case.” Uspango v. Ashcroft, 289 F.3d 226, 231 (3d Cir. 2002). Because it appears that prior counsel’s actions prevented the respondent from defending against the allegations of marriage fraud, and prevented her from presenting her application for adjustment of status, we will remand this matter for consideration of the respondent’s Form I-751 and her application for adjustment of status. Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

2004 WL 2943573 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SONIA DORENIAN
File: AXX XX3 162 - Los Angeles
October 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
*1 This case was last before the Board on June 25, 2004, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen removal proceedings. She has filed a timely motion to reconsider that denial. The motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
In a decision dated March 11, 2004, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen her removal proceedings after entry of an in absentia order of removal. On June 9, 2004, the respondent, acting pro se, filed with the Board a motion to reopen based upon the ineffective assistance of her former counsel. We denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for failure to substantiate her claim with any evidentiary materials.
 
On July 22, 2004, the respondent filed this motion to reconsider, claiming that the Board failed to address the fact that she had indicated that she would be supplementing her motion to reopen with materials in support of her claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Furthermore, the respondent claims that she has now satisfied those requirements. With her motion to reconsider, she has submitted a declaration setting forth the agreement she entered into with her former counsel, a copy of a bar complaint filed with the State Bar of California regarding her former counsel’s conduct, and a copy of a cover letter which accompanied a copy of the State Bar complaint informing her former counsel of the allegations.
 
A motion to reconsider asserts that at the time of the Board’s previous decision, an error was made. It is a “request that the Board reexamine its decision in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or perhaps an argument or aspect of the case which was overlooked.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, at 402 n.2 (BIA 1991) (quoting Hurwitz, Motions Practice Before the Board of Immigration Appeals, 20 San Diego L. Rev. 79, 90 (1992)). The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard. Id. at 402. The regulations require that a motion to reconsider shall state the reasons for the motion by specifying the errors of fact or law in the prior Board decision, and shall be supported by pertinent authority. 8 C.F.R § 1003.2(b)(1) (2004).
 
We find no error in our previous decision to deny the respondent’s motion to reopen. Although we did not explicitly acknowledge the respondent’s assertion that she was in the process of supplementing her motion with documentation in support of her ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we find that consideration of this factor does not change our original analysis or ultimate conclusion. A motion to reopen must be accompanied by the requisite supporting evidence. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). A motion which merely states that evidence that might support reopening will be provided does not provide an adequate basis for the reopening of proceedings. Thus, the respondent’s motion to reopen, at the time it was filed, did not meet the regulatory or substantive requirements for a motion to reopen based upon ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Finally, we note the respondent’s argument that pursuant to the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit decision in Fajardo v. INS, 300 F.3d 1018 (9th Cir. 2000). the period for filing her motion to reopen should be tolled because of the alleged negligence of her formal counsel. In Fajardo, the court held that the limitations period on motions to reopen based upon exceptional circumstances can be equitably tolled until the alien becomes aware of the harm resulting from the attorney’s misconduct. In the instant matter, however, the respondent has met the regulatory time limitation for a motion to reopen. In addition, according to the respondent’s motion to reopen, she was aware of former counsel’s alleged negligence at the time of the filing of the motion to reopen. We do not find that the Court’s decision in Fajardo v. INS, supra, compels the equitable tolling of the substantive requirements of a motion to reopen.
 
Therefore, we decline to reconsider our denial of the respondent’s motion to reopen, as we find no error in our decision as based upon the record before the Board at that time. Accordingly, the motion to reconsider will be denied.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is denied.

2004 WL 2943579 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KRIKOR ATAMIAN
File: AXX XX0 480 - Las Vegas
October 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On August 20, 2004, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal because he had waived the right to appeal. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). The respondent filed a motion to reconsider this decision on September 17, 2004. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondent argues that the decision to waive appeal was not a knowing and intelligent one. Matter of Patino, 23 I&N Dec. 74 (BIA 2001). He claims that an attorney, Rita Mahdessian, represented him, but sent another attorney in her stead for the March 11, 2004, removal hearing. This attorney, Seth Reszko, agreed to a lengthy period of voluntary departure under 240B(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(a), for which the right to appeal must be waived. Matter of Ocampo, 22 I&N Dec. 1301 (BIA 2000) (grant of voluntary departure prior to completion of removal proceeding, under section 240B(a) of the Act, requires an express waiver of the right to appeal).
 
The respondent argues that attorney Mahdessian had agreed to seek a continuance of proceedings, with an appeal filed if such was denied, in order to “keep his case alive” pending the resolution of an employment visa filed on his behalf. Reszko, according to the respondent, failed to explain why he was seeking voluntary departure, and declined to represent the respondent unless there would be no appeal.
 
The respondent has filed a complaint against attorney Mahdessian. However, the alleged actions about which he complains were taken by attorney Reszko. Therefore, the respondent has failed to comply with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003). As for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, those cases require that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1226-27 (9th Cir. 2002). Reszko has not been informed of the charges, so his version of events has not been sought. This is particularly significant, where the pursuit of a lengthy voluntary departure period appears to have been a reasonable strategic action on the part of counsel in this case. Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309, 311 (BIA 1998). Where the respondent claims that he did not agree to, or understand, this strategy, attorney Reszko should have been provided the chance to refute the respondent’s assertions.
 
*2 The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

IN RE: PAMELA FLORENCE OWOUR
File: AXX XX6 001 - Los Angeles

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS

ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Doreen A. Emenike, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(C)(i)º - Fraud or willful misrepresentation of a material fact
Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I)º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
  
APPLICATION: Reconsider; reopen; remand.
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a native and citizen of Kenya. This case was last before us on June 30, 2004, when we denied the respondent’s previous motion to reopen proceedings. Subsequent to the date on which we issued our decision, the respondent filed a motion to reconsider and a second motion to reopen proceedings. The respondent’s second motion to reopen is number barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (parties are limited to one motion to reopen). We further note that the Immigration Judge granted the respondent 120 days in which to voluntarily depart. The respondent has presented no evidence showing that she received an extension of that period from the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). The record shows that she was provided oral and written notice of the consequences for failing to voluntarily depart within the time allowed. Therefore, the respondent’s motion to reconsider and motion to reopen must be denied, because the respondent has remained in the United States beyond the expiration of her voluntary departure period. See Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996), aff’d, Shaar v. INS, 141 F.3d 953 (9th Cir. 1998); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b); section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
With regard to the respondent’s motion to reconsider, we note that, in addition to our denial of such motion based on the respondent’s failure to voluntarily depart, the motion is otherwise deniable as it fails to establish error in our prior decision. As we indicated in our June 30, 2004, decision, the respondent was not eligible for relief from removal at the time that we issued such decision. Specifically, the respondent indicates that her husband became a United States citizen in January of 2004. However, the respondent’s period of voluntary departure expired on July 15, 2003. Thus, the respondent would not have been eligible to adjust her status after that date, as she remained in the United States beyond the expiration of her voluntary departure period. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(a). Hence, the respondent has failed to establish error in our prior decision, and she has failed to establish that her prior attorney rendered ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to apply for a form of relief from which she was barred. See Munoz v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 950, 954-55 (9th Cir 2003) (finding that the applicant failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel, as he failed to demonstrate either deficient performance or the required prejudice).
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reconsider and motion to reopen are denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CECILIA SALAS DE RODRIGUEZ
File: AXX XX8 075 - San Francisco
October 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Lila Alcarez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Phillip Hatfield
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen with the Board of Immigration Appeals (“Board”). Therein, he presented additional evidence in support of his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. The motion is denied.
 
We have also considered the respondent’s argument that she was ineffectively represented by her former attorney in these proceedings. According to the respondent, her former counsel failed to present evidence to prove that her United States citizen children would suffer exceptionally and extremely unusual hardship if forced to return with the respondent to Mexico.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In addition, we have considered that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000). However, the Ninth Circuit stated that “the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and under ordinary circumstances the BIA does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Lozada.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, supra, at 525.
 
*2 In the instant case, the respondent indicated that her former attorney failed to inform the Immigration Judge that her United States citizen child suffers from cognitive problems potentially caused by a 1996 automobile accident. Submitted was a 2004 report from a Licensed Clinical Social Worker, medical and educational records, and the respondent’s affidavit. The Board need not resolve whether the respondent satisfied the procedural requirements for proving ineffective assistance of counsel because we cannot find that the respondent established that she was prejudiced. Although we do not dispute that the respondent’s child’s health condition should have been disclosed to the Immigration Judge, we note no evidence that the respondent’s child will be unable to obtain the medical and educational services that he may require in Mexico. The clinical social worker makes a general assertion in her report that the respondent’s son will be unable to receive special education in Mexico, but no evidence in support of that conclusion was provided. It is also unclear from the report whether the respondent’s child will be approved by the school system for special education. The clinical social worker recommends that the child receive therapy in the school district, but it is unclear whether the respondent’s child was approved for such. Based upon the evidence provided, we cannot conclude that the respondent was prejudiced in these proceedings, or is prima facie eligible for cancellation of removal if the proceedings were remanded.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2943343 (BIA)
End of Document

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated August 27, 2002. The respondent asserts that his former representative, James Robert Valinoti, rendered ineffective assistance of counsel in prior proceedings before the Immigration Judge. Specifically, he asserts that former counsel motioned to withdraw his application for cancellation of removal in error. Moreover, he asserts that additional evidence demonstrates that he accumulated the requisite period of continuous physical presence to apply for cancellation of removal.
 
Upon review, we note that James R. Valinoti was suspended from practice before this Board for three years beginning on July 24, 2003. In addition, new documents submitted with the motion to reopen demonstrate the respondent’s general compliance with the requirements we detailed in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (laying out the requirements for a motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000). We find that the record is unclear with regard to the respondent’s actual period of residence in the United States prior to the issuance of the Notice to Appear. We will thus remand this matter to the Immigration Judge for further review.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2004 WL 2943344 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MANUEL ENRIQUE CONCEPCION SANTOS
File: AXX XX8 700 - New York
October 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jack Sachs, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF SERVICE:
 
Lauren T. Farber
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Waiver pursuant to section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act
 
*1 The respondent has appealed the May 27, 2003, decision of the Immigration Judge ordering his removal to the Dominican Republic based on the charges set out in his Notice to Appear (Form I-862) (“NTA”). The respondent had wished to apply for relief pursuant to section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, but the application was pretermitted by the Immigration Judge. We find no error in the Immigration Judge’s decision. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
Through counsel, the respondent admitted the factual allegations contained in the NTA and conceded removability (Tr. at 7). The respondent’s attorney also conceded that no relief was available to the respondent (Tr. at 8). When a respondent admits factual allegations and concedes removability, and the Immigration Judge is satisfied that no errors of fact or law remain, the Immigration Judge may properly determine that removability has been established based on the respondent’s admissions and concessions. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.10(c).
 
In addition, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent is ineligible for relief from removal. As a lawful permanent resident convicted of an aggravated felony after April 24, 1996, the effective date of the Antiterrorism and Effective Death Penalty Act of 1996 (AEDPA), Pub. L. No. 104-132, 110 Stat. 1214, the respondent is ineligible for any form of discretionary relief from removal. As the respondent has expressed no fear of persecution or torture upon return to the Dominican Republic, the Immigration Judge properly determined that the respondent also did not have a valid claim for any form of non-discretionary relief from removal.
 
The respondent states on appeal that the file should be remanded because his conviction print-out was not a certified copy. A review of the record, however, indicates that the criminal conviction information was certified by the Superior Court of the State of Connecticut (Exh. 2). The respondent also claims on appeal that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel, stating that his attorney appeared to have little or no knowledge of the case or immigration law terminology.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such claims require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
*2 It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 3367 (BIA 1998). Ineffective assistance of counsel only amounts to a denial of due process if the proceedings were so fundamentally unfair that the alien was prevented from reasonably presenting his case and that he was prejudiced by the attorney’s performance. See Lozada, supra, at 638. This respondent was not prevented from presenting his claims. The proceedings were not fundamentally unfair.
 
The respondent’s present attorney did not comply with any of the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Moreover, the respondent would not have succeeded in obtaining relief from removal, even if his attorney had acted differently. His status as aggravated felon does not change. Different counsel more experienced in immigration proceedings would not have been able to change the outcome in these proceedings. The respondent was thus not prejudiced by his counsel’s actions. The result arrived at by the Immigration Judge is correct. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

2004 WL 2943339 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMED MOBEEN OOSMAN
File: AXX XX7 591 - New York
October 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
This case was last before the Board on June 30, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of the Immigration Judge’s October 7, 2003, decision denying his motion to reopen his proceedings and rescind the order of removal entered against him in absentia on May 5, 2003. The respondent has now filed a motion to reconsider our June 30, 2004, decision and a motion to reopen his proceedings in order to apply for adjustment of status.
 
Through his motion to reconsider, the respondent claims that the Board erred in dismissing his appeal and in failing to find that he was the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BLA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such claims require: (1) that the claim be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the claim reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
In the instant case, the respondent has admittedly not submitted an affidavit or filed a complaint with the bar association against his former attorneys. The respondent has also failed to articulate how his former attorneys failed to represent him. In fact, contrary to the respondent’s assertions, the record reflects that, although his former attorneys formally withdrew their representation of the respondent, they actually came to the respondent’s hearing in May 2003, to discuss the situation with the Immigration Judge. Unfortunately, the respondent himself was not present at the hearing. As a result, we are unable to find that the respondent has satisfied the procedural requirements for proving that he was ineffectively represented by his former attorneys in these proceedings.
 
Through his motion to reopen, the respondent has requested consideration for adjustment of status based upon his marriage to a United States citizen. Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998). However, the Notice to Appear reflects that the respondent was orally given the warnings for failure to appear. Consequently, he is ineligible for adjustment of status consideration for a 10-year period following the date of the entry of the final order of removal. See section 240(b)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §1229a(b)(7).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reconsider and the motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion to reconsider and the motion to reopen are denied.

*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated July 26, 2004. This case was last before us on July 26, 2004, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s April 24, 2003, decision finding that the respondent had waived his right apply for relief under former section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, because he failed to timely file the required application. We also noted that we would not consider ineffective assistance of counsel claims raised for the first time on appeal. The respondent has asked us to reconsider our prior order. The unopposed motion is granted. On reconsideration, we find that the respondent substantially complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), and he has established that his prior counsel’s behavior prejudiced his case. Gamez, admits that he failed to timely file the respondent’s 212(c) application thereby precluding the respondent from applying for such relief. On reconsideration, these proceedings will be reopened and remanded to afford the respondent the opportunity to apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Act.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2004 WL 2943332 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LEONARDO BRAVO MADRIZ
File: AXX XX6 468 - San Francisco
October 25, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marc Van Der Hout, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Patrick T. Barry
Assistant Chief Counsel
*1 The respondent’s motion to reopen has been filed out of time. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on September 4, 2002. The motion to reopen was filed late, on June 9, 2004. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
The respondent argues that the deadline for filing his motion to reopen should be tolled due to alleged ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent was represented by attorney Walter Pineda at the removal hearing, and on appeal to this Board. He argues that Pineda represented him in a petition for review filed with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals, and on May 3, 2004, learned from Pineda that the case had been dismissed. On May 18, 2004, he engaged current counsel, and the pending motion was filed on June 9, 2004. Guided by Ninth Circuit precedent, we find that the respondent exercised diligence in submitting the pending motion, and the deadline for such motion should be tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003) (court found that to the extent equitable tolling applies, based on actions of prior counsel, limitations period begins when respondent meets with new counsel and learns of fraud).
 
As noted, the respondent claims that Pineda provided ineffective assistance concerning his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). Specifically, the respondent claims that Pineda erred by failing to present evidence concerning the hardship the respondent’s mother would face in the event of the respondent’s removal. The respondent’s mother became a lawful permanent resident while the respondent’s appeal was pending at the Board; the respondent asserts that Pineda should have sought either a remand to the Immigration Judge (while the appeal was pending at the Board), or should have filed a timely motion to reopen after our September 4, 2002, decision. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act (hardship to lawful permanent resident parent relevant to whether cancellation of removal should be granted). The respondent has presented evidence concerning his mother’s financial and emotional dependence on him, as well as evidence concerning her numerous medical and emotional problems. The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62; (BIA 2003), Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988).
 
*2 The respondent asserts that, when he met with representatives of Pineda’s office in 1997, he was not told that hardship to a lawful permanent resident parent could be relevant (Respondent’s Affidavit). He further argues that Pineda spent little time with him concerning his claim. Id. The respondent admits that he did not inform Pineda that his mother had become a lawful permanent resident in 2001, but states that he did not realize that this might be important. Id. The respondent states that, prior to his mother becoming a lawful permanent resident, he spoke with Pineda and asked him if his mother could submit a petition on his behalf. Id. According to the respondent, this did not cause Pineda to ask whether his mother had a residence petition pending. Id. We acknowledge that Pineda submitted a letter arguing that the respondent was at fault for not informing him about the change in his mother’s status. The DHS similarly argues that, given his inaction, the respondent did not suffer ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent’s assertions that he was not fully advised concerning the requirements for cancellation of removal are not directly refuted by Pineda in his letter.
 
Further, we agree with the respondent that his situation is similar to that of the respondent in Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, 381 F.3d 855 (9th Cir. 2004). In that case, the court considered an argument that an attorney, who has been expelled from practice before the Board and the Ninth Circuit, failed to present evidence concerning the respondent’s mother, who became a lawful permanent resident while the appeal was pending before this Board. As evidence concerning the mother “may have affected the proceedings”, id. at 858-59, the Ninth Circuit found, the Board required too stringent a test for showing prejudice resulting from the alleged ineffective assistance of counsel, and remanded the case to the Board for further consideration. Given the evidence presented concerning the respondent’s mother, and guided by Maravilla Maravilla v. Ashcroft, supra, we find that evidence concerning her hardship “may have affected the proceedings,” had it been presented by Pineda.
 
We therefore find that, based on the respondent’s submissions, a remand to the Immigration Judge is appropriate. On remand, the respondent will have an opportunity to further pursue his application for cancellation of removal. The DHS shall have the opportunity to examine and verify the validity of the respondent’s submissions and present evidence.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2004 WL 2943462 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RUBEN VARGAS-ROMERO CANDELARIA NAVA DE VARGAS
Files: AXX XX8 570 - San Francisco
AXX XX8 571
October 21, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Helen B. Zebel, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evan R. Franke
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal 240A(b); remand
 
The respondents, natives and citizens of Mexico, appeal the Immigration Judge’s June 17, 2003, decision denying their application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). In addition, the respondents allege ineffective assistance of counsel and seek to remand the record in order to have the Immigration Judge reconsider their cancellation application. The motion to remand is denied and the appeal is dismissed.
 
The respondents claim that their attorney failed to adequately prepare their case. They further assert that their attorney failed to submit evidence of their son’s psychological illness. See respondents’ affidavit. Last, the respondents assert that the Immigration Judge erred by failing to give full evidentiary weight to their testimony concerning their children’s medical issues.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred, Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
*2 In addition, we have considered that the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence). See also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
We do not find sufficient evidence to establish that the respondents were prejudiced as a result of their attorney’s ineffectiveness. The respondents’ attorney questioned them extensively about their children’s past medical history (Tr. at 15-23). The record is replete with supporting documents showing past appointments with medical doctors (Exh. 7-8). However, there are no documents to show that any of the respondents’ children have been diagnosed with an illness for which they would not be able to receive treatment in Mexico. The respondents allege in their affidavit that their son’s doctor had “faxed” a letter to the immigration consultant which corroborated their claim about their son’s psychological illness. The respondents have not submitted a copy of the letter with their appeal or a statement from the child’s doctor attesting to the fact that she had faxed the letter and that the child suffers from an illness. The respondents also failed to provide evidence with their appeal which proves that they would have been granted cancellation of removal at the hearing had their attorney then submitted it to the Immigration Court. Hence, we are unable to find that the respondents were prejudiced by their former attorney in these proceedings.
 
We also affirm the Immigration Judge’s decision denying cancellation of removal as the respondents failed to establish exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to their United States citizen children if they are returned to Mexico. The respondents failed to demonstrate that their removal would cause hardship to their United States citizen children that is substantially different from, or beyond, that which would normally be expected from the deportation of an alien with close family members in the United States. See Matter of Andazola, 23 I&N Dec. 319 (BIA, 2002); Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001). It is well settled that economic detriment and loss of educational opportunities alone are insufficient to support a finding of extreme hardship under former section 244(a) of the Act which is a lower burden of proof standard than the exceptional and extremely unusual hardship standard under section 240A(b) of the Act. See Matter of Pilch, 21 I&N Dec. 627 (BIA 1996). While we acknowledge that the respondents’ children would suffer some initial hardship if the respondents had to resettle in Mexico, we do not find that their hardship would be substantially different from American children of other deportees. Although the respondents claim that their son Alfredo, suffers from some behavioral problems, the medical evidence of record does not show that any of the children suffer from any life threatening or chronic illnesses (Tr. at 15-23). Moreover, there is no evidence that they would be deprived of medical services and educational opportunities in Mexico. The children, who are young, can readily assimilate in Mexican society because of their tender age. In light of the factors in this case, we find that the respondents’ United States citizen children would not suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if the respondents are returned to Mexico. Matter of Monreal, supra.
 
*3 Accordingly, the following orders will be entered:
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed and the motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f).
 
NOTICE: If the respondents fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Buligan v. Gonzales, 9th Cir., June 13, 2007
2004 WL 2943435 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARION LUZON BULIGAN
File: AXX XX6 500 - San Francisco
October 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ashwani Bhakhri, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jo Ellen Ardinger
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on November 14, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. As the Board did not receive the motion until August 23, 2004, it was therefore filed out of time.
 
We do not find that the respondent’s case falls within the Ninth Circuit’s equitable tolling policy for aliens who have filed with the Board otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). The Ninth Circuit has stated that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with clue diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 897. The respondent admittedly personally received the Board’s adverse decision, which contains a footnote advising her that her former attorney, Mr. Gadda, had been “suspended from practice before the Board.” Yet the respondent asserts that she nonetheless then contacted that same attorney for assistance in filing a federal appeal of the Board’s decision.
 
We initially note that the respondent’s motion is defective as she has not provided an affidavit outlining in detail the agreement she asserts she had with Mr. Gadda, nor a letter informing him of the complaint against him. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The respondent claims she last communicated with Mr. Gadda in July of 2003, when she was told the appeal was pending. She has not identified what, if any, efforts she has undertaken to contact Mr. Gadda and inform him of her contentions against him despite having previously contacted him. Her counsel’s general assertion that Mr. Gadda’s “present whereabouts are unknown” does not constitute competent evidence of the claimed fact and such a statement, even coming from the respondent, fails to detail what, if any, action she took to contact Mr. Gadda. See Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3) (a motion to reopen must be supported by “affidavits or other evidentiary material,” and not mere legal assertions). Moreover, the respondent waited more than 21 months to file this motion following the Board’s adverse decision, and more than a year after she last allegedly contacted Mr. Gadda with regard to an appeal of our decision. The respondent has not stated what efforts she undertook in the interim period since July 2003 to ascertain the status of her case. In light of this record, we find that the respondent has failed to establish that her motion is subject to equitable tolling because she has not demonstrated due diligence. Id.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 2943394 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
