IN RE: AROUTIOUN VOSKANIAN
File: A76 286 855 - Los Angeles
October 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Artem M. Sarian, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has filed a motion to reopen and remand proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel.1 New counsel for the respondent claims that prior counsel misled him regarding the status of his case. The Department of Homeland Security has not responded to the motion.
 
We have held that to satisfy a claim based upon ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must support his claim by a detailed affidavit, inform his previous attorney of the allegations of misconduct and provide former counsel with an opportunity to respond, and state whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to the attorney’s alleged violation of ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Here, the respondent has submitted a declaration supporting the claim of ineffective assistance, and the respondent has notified his former counsel and filed a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority. The respondent indicated that he was advised by counsel at the place of his hearing to leave the hearing location because an outstanding arrest warrant in the respondent’s name was to be executed.2 The respondent stated that he was not subsequently advised by counsel as to the status of his case and therefore was unable to timely appeal.3 We find that the respondent has satisfied a showing of ineffective assistance. Moreover, the respondent has demonstrated that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance. Matter of Aassad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Based upon our finding that the respondent was accorded ineffective assistance of counsel, the record is ordered remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings. Accordingly, the motion is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

2004 WL 2943408 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARTINEZ MALDONADO, MANUEL
File: AXX-XX3-187 - Los Angeles
October 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated July 21, 2004. The respondents (AXX XXX XXX/XXX/XXX/XX XXX XXX) allege ineffective assistance of their prior counsel. Statements made by the respondents in a motion are not evidence and are thus not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of S-M-, 22I&N Dec. 49,51 (BIA 1998); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). Moreover, in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), the Board required that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he or she was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. It is crucial that the respondents’ prior attorney be given “the opportunity to respond to the allegation¿sº of ineffectiveness,” due to the charges made by the respondent. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondents have failed to comply with any of the above requirements. The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has indicated that rigid adherence to the Lozada requirements is not always required, see Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir.2002) (Lozada “factors are not rigidly applied, especially when the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance”). However, in this case the record does not show a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance. The need for notification and response from former counsel is critical to our meaningful assessment of this claim. Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2943402 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MALHI, GURMEET SINGH
File: AXX XX0 325 - San Francisco
October 15, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marc Van Der Hout, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Michelle Guardino,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen these proceedings. On May 10, 2002, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied his motion to remand proceedings based on a second marriage to a United States citizen that occurred on November 2, 2001. On January 23, 2003, we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen, pointing out that it was supported by essentially the same documents that we found, in our May 10, 2002, decision, to be inadequate to meet the “clear and convincing” test of Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 254 (BIA 2002). Thus, the respondent was on notice, at least by the time of our January 23, 2003, decision that his former attorney submitted the same documents. A second motion to reopen was denied by the Board on July 23, 2003, because it was number barred. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).
 
The respondent has now filed a third motion to reopen, raising for the first time a claim of ineffective assistance from his prior counsel. The motion is both number barred and untimely. We reject the respondent’s argument that the limitations on motions should be equitably tolled. Our January 23, 2003, decision placed the respondent on notice that the same documents had been submitted, and our July 23, 2003, notice advised him that he had exhausted his opportunity to reopen under the motions regulation. Yet, the respondent waited until June 28, 2004, to file a motion alleging ineffective assistance of counsel. After our prior decisions, the respondent cannot be found to have reasonably relied on his former counsel to cure the deficiencies in his prior motions. We find that he has failed to demonstrate that the time limitation should be equitably tolled. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (if petitioner acts with due diligence upon its discovery, equitable tolling is available if the petitioner was prevented from timely filing because of deception, fraud, or error); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir 2000); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2943411 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BAUDELIO RODRIGUEZ-FRANQUEZ MARIA ISABEL RODRIGUEZ PRADO-MEZA
Files: AXX XX6 474 - Los Angeles
AXX XX6 473
October 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondents have appealed from the Immigration Judge’s December 21, 2002, decision denying their application for cancellation of removal under the Immigration and Nationality Act. See 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). While the appeal was pending, the respondents filed a pro se brief, with supporting documents, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, and requesting that we remand this case to the Immigration Judge for reconsideration of whether their removal to Mexico would result in “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship” to either of their United States citizen children. We share the Immigration Judges’ concern regarding the quality of the representation afforded the respondents and given the record before us and the respondent’s arguments on appeal question the fairness of the of the hearing below. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). We conclude, therefore, that the respondents should be afforded the opportunity to seek new counsel and to have the merits of their application for relief reconsidered at a new hearing before the Immigration Judge. Accordingly, we issue the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondents’ appeal is sustained and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and the entry of a new decision.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BERNARDO DE LA ROSA
File: AXX XX1 924 - New York
October 15, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ana M. Franco, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. In a March 25, 2004, decision, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. With his Notice of Appeal, the respondent filed a brief. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the respondent’s appeal. The respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
An Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia after he failed to appear for a March 5, 2004, immigration court hearing. On March 22, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen his proceedings. The Immigration Judge denied the motion to reopen on March 25, 2004, finding that the respondent had been provided notice of his hearing date. See section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b)(5)(C).
 
The Immigration Judge correctly denied the motion to reopen. Counsel argued in the motion to reopen, and on appeal, that she “lost contact” with the respondent, due to an office move, and did not ensure that the respondent knew of the March 5, 2004, hearing date. Counsel implicitly argues that she provided ineffective assistance to the respondent by not making him aware of the hearing date. However, the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Most critically, there is no detailed affidavit from the respondent explaining his version of events and the alleged confusion over the hearing date.
 
The appeal is, therefore, dismissed.

2004 WL 2943367 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMED SHAWKAT HAIDER
File: A96 128 471 - Minneapolis
October 14, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria K. Woroby, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the June 10, 2004, decision and order of the Immigration Judge denying the respondent’s motion to reconsider her May 20, 2004, order denying a second motion to reopen removal proceedings to rescind an in absentia order based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The first motion to reopen to rescind the in absentia order was denied on March 30, 2004. The Immigration Judge had previously ordered the respondent removed in absentia on August 1, 2003. The appeal will be dismissed, and the request to remand will be denied.
 
The procedural posture of the appeal before this Board is a limited one.1 This appeal is limited to whether the Immigration Judge erred in her June 10, 2004, decision to deny the motion to reconsider her May 20, 2004, order. As noted in that order, the Immigration Judge found that the respondent essentially was seeking a third motion to reopen based on new evidence with respect to compliance with the requirements under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), in pursuing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. He also failed to challenge or address her prior determination that he can be constructively charged with having received notice of the hearing based on his failure to inform the Immigration Court of his change of address.
 
On appeal the respondent argues that the Immigration Judge abused her discretion in denying the motion to reconsider. For the first time on appeal the respondent has submitted a copy of his record before both the DHS and the Executive Office for Immigration Review which he received pursuant to a Freedom Of Information Act request. This information was not submitted to the Immigration Judge, and this Board may not consider such new evidence for the first time on appeal. See Matter of Fedorenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 73-74 (BIA 1984). Additionally, the respondent raises new arguments which are not supported by affidavit or other evidentiary material and were previously not raised before the Immigration Judge. Specifically, counsel for respondent argues for the first time that the respondent never received a copy of the Notice to Appear from his former counsel and as a result he never knew of his duty to inform DHS and the Immigration Court of his change of address. Finally, the respondent refers to a recent decision, Ghounem v. Ashcroft, 378 F.3d 740 (8th Cir. 2004), in the United States Court of Appeals for the Eighth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises. He argues that Ghounem requires that this Board remand the record for an evidentiary hearing as to whether the respondent can be constructively charged with having received notice of the hearing date and time.
 
*2 We find no reversible error in the Immigration Judge’s decision to deny the motion to reconsider. See 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.23(b)(1), (4)(ii)(2004); see also Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (alien is statutorily barred from rescinding the order of removal based on “exceptional circumstances” regardless of whether he can demonstrate that his failure to timely file the motion is attributable to ineffective assistance of counsel). See also sections 240(b)(5)(C) and 240(e)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§1229a(b)(5)(C) and 1229a(e)(1) (in absentia order of removal may be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen filed within 180 days after the date of the order of removal, if the alien demonstrates that the failure to appear was because of “exceptional circumstances,” such as the serious illness of a child).
 
We also find no error in the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent personally received the Notice to Appear and that he could be constructively charged with receiving adequate notice of the time and place of his hearing. See Matter of M-D-, 23 I&N Dec. 540 (BIA 2002). The respondent does not dispute that he was personally served with the Notice to Appear (“NTA,” Exh. 1) which informed him of the charges against him, the consequences of his failure to appear before the Immigration Judge, and his duty to inform the Immigration Court of his change of address. See Immigration Judge Order dated March 30, 2004. The respondent also does not contest that the address on the NTA was the last known address given to the DHS. The respondent admittedly moved from his apartment after the issuance of the NTA but he did not promptly inform the DHS, the Immigration Court, or his own attorney of the move. As a consequence, he failed to receive the notice of the date and time of the hearing before the Immigration Court.
 
For the first time on appeal from the denial of the motion to reconsider the respondent, through counsel, alleges that he never received a copy of the Notice to Appear from his prior attorney and implies that he was personally unaware of the change of address requirement. This argument was not raised before the Immigration Judge, and we do not see that it was addressed in the respondent’s affidavit or in the complaint against former counsel. Similarly, counsel argues in her brief that the respondent was under “pressure” at the time of the Special Registration when he was served with the Notice to Appear. Again, this is the first time this argument is being raised and there is no showing how that affects his service of the Notice to Appear and the warnings contained therein. Moreover, counsel argues that the facts in this case are similar to those in Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 180 (BIA 2001), where we held that entry of an in absentia removal order is inappropriate where the record reflects that the alien did not receive, or could not be charged with receiving, the Notice to Appear that was served by certified mail at an address obtained from documents filed with the Service several years earlier. However, this case is notably distinguishable from G-Y-R-. The respondent personally was served with the Notice to Appear. He was put on notice that he would be informed by the Immigration Court of his future hearing date and that he must inform the DHS and the Immigration Court of any change of address. The current allegation by counsel, whose statements are not evidence, that the respondent never received a copy of the NTA or notice of the warnings and directions regarding the change of address requirement was never raised in an affidavit by respondent before the Immigration Judge or submitted in an affidavit on appeal with the request to remand.
 
*3 Finally, we find that the instant case is also factually distinguishable from the recent decision in Ghounem v. Aschcroft, 378 F.3d 740 (8th Cir. 2004). In Ghounem, an Immigration Judge denied the alien’s motion to reopen removal proceedings to rescind an in absentia order. The alien had received the Notice to Appear with notice that the hearing would be set at a future time and date by the Immigration Court. In the motion to reopen the alien argued that neither he, nor his attorney of record before DHS, received notice of the hearing before the Immigration Court. The notice of hearing had been sent by regular mail. The alien conceded that the notice of hearing had been sent to the correct address of record, but denied that he or his wife had actually received it. The United States Court of Appeals for the Eighth Circuit found the Immigration Judge abused his discretion by applying the evidentiary standards set forth in Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995), to deny the motion to reopen by finding a rebuttable presumption of the delivery of the notice of hearing when the notice was sent by regular mail rather than certified mail. The court followed the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit’s decision in Salta v. INS, 314 F.3d 1076, 1079 (9th Cir. 2002), which found that regular mail delivery does not have the same strong presumption of effective delivery as certified mail. The court found that an affidavit from the alien swearing that he did not receive notice of the hearing “is enough to rebut the presumption of delivery created by INS records that it sent Ghounem notice by regular mail.” Ghounem v. Ashcroft, supra, at 745. The court remanded with instructions to conduct an evidentiary hearing as to whether the alien is permitted to reopen his case.
 
In the instant case there is no issue as to whether the presumption of delivery of regular mail applies. The respondent admitted that he moved and failed to inform the authorities and his then attorney before DHS of this change of address. The record contains the envelope which was sent to the last known address of record, and is returned to the Immigration Court as “attempted not known.” Thus, the issue of notice, as it applies in this case, is whether the respondent properly informed the DHS and Immigration Court of his change of address as required in the Notice to Appear. He admittedly did not file a change of address notification and, therefore, is constructively charged with notice of his hearing. Indeed, the statute even provides that written notice is not required when an alien fails to provide a change of address. Sections 239(a)(1)(F)(ii) and (2)(B) of the Act.
 
We recognize the sympathetic factors in this case-- the respondent’s marriage to a United States citizen, the alleged hardship to his wife and the financial hardship to his family abroad if he is removed. However, we do not find that based on the record before us that the Immigration Judge erred in denying the motion to reconsider, or that a remand is required for a new evidentiary hearing.
 
*4 ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2418677 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ARTHUR GOLDMAN
File: AXX XX9 153 - Newark
October 12, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Angela M. Bean, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This is the fourth time that this case has been before the Board. On April 6, 1999, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision, in which the judge denied his motion to reopen deportation proceedings, which were conducted in absentia. On September 8, 2003, we denied a motion to reopen our April 6, 1999, decision. On February 3, 2004, we again declined to reopen the respondent’s case. The respondent filed yet another motion to reopen on April 7, 2004, which is untimely as not filed within 90 days of our final administrative order of April 6, 1999. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
An order of deportation issued following proceedings conducted in absentia could be rescinded only upon a motion to reopen which demonstrated that the alien failed to appear because of exceptional circumstances. The Board lacks jurisdiction to consider such a motion to reopen in the first instance. Matter of Gonzalez-Lopez, 20 I&N Dec. 644 (BIA 1993). The Board has jurisdiction to consider an appeal, when a motion to reopen an in absentia order is denied by an Immigration Judge. Id. at 646. We considered, and denied, such an appeal in this case. The respondent now, for the third time, seeks to have the Board reopen its decision.
 
Even if the untimeliness of the motion were excused, it would be denied. The respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from the attorney, Edwin Peralta-Millan, who represented him at the time of his January 18, 1995, in absentia hearing. Then, according to the respondent, three more attorneys, Natasha Samus, Douglas Lehrman, and Marc Van Der Hout, inadequately pursued claims of ineffective assistance of counsel against Peralta-Millan.
 
Both the Immigration Judge, and this Board, have already evaluated the actions of attorney Peralta-Millan and found that they did not constitute ineffective assistance of counsel. In his motion to reopen submitted to the Immigration Judge, the respondent asserted that, on the morning of the January 18, 1995, deportation hearing, he had agreed to pick up Peralta-Millan. The attorney was late, and, the respondent argued, he was therefore late for his deportation hearing. The Immigration Judge, in denying the untimely motion to reopen submitted by the respondent, found that the respondent was aware of the date, time, and place of his scheduled hearing, and he “failed to establish exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear” (I.J. July 9, 1997). We affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge.
 
*2 In our September 8, 2003, decision, we considered the respondent’s claim that Peralta-Millan had caused him to be late. We found that the respondent had failed to establish ineffective assistance of counsel on the part of Peralta-Millan.
 
The pending motion argues, yet again, that the respondent failed to appear for his January 18, 1995, deportation hearing because of exceptional circumstances, namely, the actions of attorney Peralta-Millan (Respondent’s Mot., at 1, 3). We again find that the respondent failed to establish such exceptional circumstances. He asserts that his attorney insisted on receiving a ride to the migration court, and counsel’s lateness caused the respondent to be late for the immigration hearing (Respondent’s Mot., at 6, 10-13; Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 10). Again, we find this excuse inadequate to reopen the in absentia order. The respondent was aware of the time and place for the scheduled hearing, and knew how long it took to travel to the courthouse (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶¶ 7, 10). Although counsel may have asked the respondent for a ride, there is no assertion that the attorney told him not to attend the deportation hearing. When the attorney was late for the requested ride, it was the respondent’s decision to wait rather than proceed to the deportation hearing (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶ 10). Compare Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 472 (BIA 1996) (respondent demonstrated exceptional circumstances for failing to appear at hearing, where attorney’s employee told him incorrectly that he should not appear at immigration hearing because the case had been postponed). In Matter of Grijalva, supra, at 474, the respondent was “blatantly misled regarding his need to appear at the scheduled hearing.” The respondent did not show that he was similarly misled by Peralta-Millan. The respondent therefore failed to show exceptional circumstances for his failure to appear at the deportation hearing, and there is no basis upon which to reopen these proceedings. We specifically find no reason to reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), as the respondent requests (Respondent’s Mot., at 26-28).
 
The pending motion is therefore denied, and the record is returned to the Immigration Court without further action.

2004 WL 2418613 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOIN UDDIN SHEIKH
File: A47 265 603 - Newark
October 6, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Marcia S. Kasdan, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Charles Parker, Jr.
Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of crime of moral turpitude
Sec. 237(a)(2)(A)(iii), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i)º - Convicted of aggravated felony
 
  
APPLICATION: Removability
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s decision, dated April 12, 2004, finding the respondent removable: as a alien convicted of a crime of moral turpitude within 5 years of admission for which a sentence of 1 year or more may be imposed as defined in section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(2)(A)(i); and, as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony as defined in section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(43)(A). The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that his conviction on September 27, 2002, for endangering the welfare of children under New Jersey Statutes Annotated 2C:24-4a is neither an aggravated felony nor a crime involving moral turpitude. Rather, he claims it is a petty offense. Furthermore, the respondent claims that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his criminal proceeding.
 
An aggravated felony, as defined under the Act, includes sexual abuse of a minor. See section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act. In this case, the respondent was convicted of endangering the welfare of children under N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:24-4a.1 The Indictment specifically reflects that the respondent engaged in sexual conduct which would impair or debauch the morals of A.L., born February 16, 1989, a child less than 16 years of old. Thus, the respondent’s conviction constitutes sexual abuse of a minor and, thus an aggravated felony under section 101(a)(43)(A) of the Act. See Matter of Rodriguez-Rodriguez, 22 I&N Dec. 991 (BIA 1999) (indecent exposure in the presence of a child by one intent on sexual arousal is clearly sexual abuse of a minor). Consequently, the respondent’s classification of the conviction as a petty offense is without merit, especially since the respondent was sentenced to community supervision for life.
 
The respondent also claims that his conviction is not a crime involving moral turpitude which renders him removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act. Section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act specifically provides that an alien convicted of a crime of moral turpitude within 5 years of admission for which a sentence of 1 year or more may be imposed is deportable. In this case, the respondent’s conviction for endangering the welfare of children under N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:24-4a is a crime involving moral turpitude. See Matter of Imber, 16 I&N Dec. 256 (BIA 1977) (sexual misconduct with a female under age 16 is a crime involving moral turpitude). Furthermore, the respondent was admitted to the United States on March 4, 2000, and he was convicted of endangering the welfare of children on September 27, 2002. Thus, he was convicted of a crime involving moral turpitude within 5 years of his admission. Moreover, the respondent’s conviction was for a “crime of the third degree” under N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:24-4a. A person convicted of a crime of the third degree may be sentenced to imprisonment between 3 to 5 years. See N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:43-6a(3). Therefore, the respondent’s conviction renders him removable under section 237(a)(2)(A)(i) of the Act.
 
*2 Additionally, the respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance of counsel during his criminal proceedings. Thus, the respondent is attempting to collaterally attack his criminal conviction. However, the Board cannot entertain a collateral attack on a judgement of conviction, unless that judgement is void on its face. See Matter of Rodriguez-Carillo, 22 I&N Dec. 1031, 1034 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Madrigal, 21 I&N Dec. 323, 327 (BIA 1996). Accordingly, the respondent’s argument is without merit.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

N.J. Stat. Ann. 2C:24-4a specifically states that “¿aºny person having a legal duty for the care of a child or who has assumed responsibility for the care of a child who engages in sexual conduct which would impair of debauch the morals of the child, or who causes the child harm that would make the child an abused or neglected child as defined in R.S. 9:6-1, R.S. 9:6-3 and P.L. 1974, c. 119, s. 1 (C.9:6-8.21 is guilty of a crime of the second degree. Any other person who engages in conduct or who causes harm as described in this subsection to a child under the age of 16 is guilty of a crime of the third degree.”

    0  Reconsideration Granted by IN RE: LAURA ELENA ZAMBRANO VALENZUELA ZOILA NALLELI ARANDA-ZAMBRANO YESICA LIZET ARANDA-ZAMBRANO, BIA, March 28, 2005

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LAURA ELENA ZAMBRANO VALENZUELA ZOILA NALLELI ARANDA ZAMBRANO YESICA LIZET ARANDA ZAMBRANO
Files: AXX XX5 356 - Los Angeles
AXX XX5 357
AXX XX5 358
October 6, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Christopher J. Todd, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURAM. The respondents have filed a motion to reopen our decision of March 1, 2004. The respondents, through counsel Eric A. Dumars, filed the motion to reopen on May 28, 2004, asserting that they were ineffectively represented at their hearing before the Immigration Judge. The lead respondent was unable to identify the attorney who represented them in immigration court. Further, her affidavit did not detail the agreement with the attorney or articulate with any specificity what information and evidence she provided him which was not submitted to the Immigration Judge. Finally, since the lead respondent did not know the identity of their attorney, she was unable to contact him or her or to file a disciplinary complaint. In short, there is essentially no compliance with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592 (9th Cir. 2004) (the absence of an affidavit outlining the agreement with counsel will not be excused where the facts underlying the petitioner’s claim are not plain on the face of the administrative record).
 
On September 8, 2004, the respondent, through yet another counsel, Christopher J. Todd, filed a motion to hold in abeyance our decision on the May 28, 2004, motion to reopen filed by attorney Dumars until he is able to comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. Counsel Todd argues that he is unable to perfect an ineffective assistance of counsel claim until he obtains a copy of the transcript identifying the attorneys who represented the respondents during the course of their proceedings and the nature of the testimony presented.
 
As a general rule, motions should be accompanied by all of the necessary supporting evidence at the time of the original filing. Further, we are not inclined to delay the adjudication of the respondents’ motion since the record shows that, on May 16, 2003, a transcript was mailed directly to the respondents, who appeared pro se on appeal. Therefore, the information needed to establish a Lozada claim was already in their control. The transcript shows that the lead respondent was repeatedly questioned at her hearing about whether there were any other hardships that her family would face if they were removed to Mexico, and she made no mention of the domestic abuse theory of relief that is raised in the motion (Tr. at 22, 30 AXX XXX XXX-35).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motions are denied.

IN RE: LORENA MENDIZABAL

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Garry L. Davis, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sabrina Hall
Deputy District Counsel
*1 On June 11, 2002, the Board remanded this case to an Immigration Judge so the respondent could apply for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(c) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1182(c). On January 24, 2003, an Immigration Judge determined that, having failed to submit her 212(c) application timely, the respondent had abandoned her pursuit for relief. The respondent was ordered deported to Guatemala.
 
The respondent then filed a motion to reopen with the Immigration Judge, which was denied on March 6, 2003. The basis of the motion to reopen, filed by former counsel, was that the respondent filed the 212(c) application with the DHS, but failed to file a copy with the immigration court. The Board affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision on May 5, 2004. The respondent filed a motion to reopen with the Board on August 2, 2004. She argues that former counsel is to blame for the late-filed application, and asserts that she received ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent submits a letter from former counsel, in which counsel assured her that the 212(c) application had been filed “as required by the Immigration Court.”
 
The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the respondent’s motion. The basis for the DHS opposition is that it does not have the respondent’s file, and therefore opposes the motion. The DHS sought an extension of time to supplement its answer, but despite the passage of more than a month since its August 16, 2004, filing, has not done so.
 
The respondent’s motion will be granted, and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge in order that the respondent may be afforded another opportunity to apply for section 212(c) relief.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with this opinion.

2004 WL 2418675 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TOURE DAOUDA
File: A77 779 185 - Denver
October 5, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gerald Burns, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; cancellation of removal
 
*1 The respondent has filed a motion to reopen before the Board of Immigration Appeals (“Board”). We have jurisdiction over this matter pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The respondent argued therein that the ineffective representation of former counsel resulted in a determination by the Board and Immigration Judge that the respondent was not entitled to cancellation of removal under section 240A of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b. We note that the Board previously affirmed the Immigration Judge’s determination that the respondent failed to satisfy the exceptional and extremely unusual hardship requirement for cancellation of removal. The motion is denied.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In Lozada, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639.
 
It is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. Id. at 640. The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair. See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998).
 
In the instant case, the respondent has submitted an affidavit and filed a complaint with the bar association against his former attorney. There is no indication, however, that former counsel was advised of the allegation, and given an opportunity to respond. Former counsel did, however, learn of the complaint from the bar association, and he wrote a response letter that is contained in the record. Therein, former counsel indicated that the respondent did not comply with Matter of Lozada, supra in that he did not receive a copy of the respondent’s allegation. Despite the respondent’s failure to directly notify his former counsel of the allegations contained in his motion to reopen due to ineffective assistance of counsel, we will find that the Matter of Lozada, supra procedural requirements are met.
 
*2 The issue remaining for the Board to decide is whether the respondent’s counsel represented the respondent in an egregious manner that it rendered the hearing unfair, and the outcome would have been different but for the negligence of counsel. See Matter of B-B-, supra. Herein, the Immigration Judge noted several items of proof which should have been provided by the respondent which would have been helpful in deciding the issue of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship to the qualifying relatives (See I.J. at 8, 9). We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent and his counsel were lax by not presenting those items at the hearing.
 
In support of the motion, the respondent has provided to the Board an affidavit, the birth certificates of his three United States citizen children, proof that he has legal custody over his oldest child, Nancy Toure, and recent background evidence concerning conditions in the Ivory Coast1. Regarding the custody issue of Nancy Toure, we recognize that the Immigration Judge found significant that the respondent did not introduce proof that he had legal custody over her. However, the respondent testified at the hearing that he had legal custody over his daughter. It was also indicated that the child lives with her mother during the school year, and spends vacations with the respondent (Tr. at 26). We further note that the respondent testified that his children would remain in the United States, and not return with him to the Ivory Coast (Tr. at 30). In addition, we note that the respondent’s affidavit and motion does not provide any additional evidence regarding how his children would be adversely impacted whether personally or financially by the respondent’s removal. Consequently, even now considering that the respondent is the legal custodian of his daughter and helps in her support, we cannot conclude that the respondent satisfied his burden of proving that he is prima facie eligible for a grant of cancellation of removal. It follows that the respondent has failed to prove in his motion that he was prejudiced by the representation of former counsel. See Matter of B-B-, supra.
 
Accordingly, the following order is entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

1

We note that background evidence on conditions in the respondent’s country was contained in the record when the proceedings were before the Immigration Judge.

2004 WL 2418715 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMONA OVIEDO-ROSARIO
Files: A72 831 992 - New York
September 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Howard M. Rosengarten, Esquire
 
APPLICATION:. Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 1, 2004, in which she denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The respondent had previously been ordered removed in absentia for her failure to appear for the hearing on September 21, 2000. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
On appeal the respondent contends that the Immigration Judg erred in requiring the respondent to demonstrate exceptional circumstances in order to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent further asserts that she should be afforded an opportunity to apply for adjustment of status because she was never advised of the consequences for failing to appear. In her motion to reopen, the respondent also claimed that she failed to appear for her hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
A review of the record reflects that the respondent filed her motion to reopen on February 19, 2004. Therefore the respondent failed to file her motion to reopen within 180 days after issuance of the final order, as is required under section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act. See Matter of A-A-, 22 I&N Dec. 140 (BIA 1998) and Matter of Lei, 22 I&N Dec. 113 (BIA 1998) (a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel does not constitute an exception to the 180-day time limit under section 242B(c)(3)(A) of the Act).1 However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Second Circuit has determined that the deadline for motions to reopen deportation proceedings may be equitably tolled to accommodate claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 130 (2nd Cir. 2000). An alien must demonstrate not only that the alien’s constitutional right to due process has been violated by the conduct of counsel, but that the alien has exercised due diligence in pursuing the case during the period that the alien seeks to toll. Id. The Immigration Judge ordered the respondent removed in absentia on September 21, 2000. The respondent indicated in her motion that an individual acting as an attorney told her that she need not appear for her hearing and that he would represent her at the hearing. Furthermore, after a few months, when the respondent returned to this individual’s office to inquire about the status of her case, the respondent discovered that he had moved and had left no forwarding address. The respondent then waited over three years to file a motion to reopen these proceedings based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that the respondent failed to exercise due diligence required for equitable tolling. Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish exceptional circumstances for her failure to appear at the hearing on September 21, 2000.
 
*2 Finally, the respondent also seeks reopening to apply for adjustment of status. An alien is not required to rescind his or her deportation order under section 242B of the Act if he or she is pursuing an application for new relief, and provided there is no evidence that the respondent received the required warnings under section 242B(e) of the Act. See Matter of M-S-, 22 I&N Dec. 349 (BIA 1998).2 Rather, the motion to reopen is only subject to the regulatory requirements set forth at 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3).3 Under these standards, the respondent’s motion is untimely, as it was filed more than 90 days after the Immigration Judge’s decision. She is accordingly precluded from having her motion considered under the general time limitations for a motion to reopen.4 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
Footnotes

1

We note that Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings pursuant to section 242B of the Act. However, as section 240(b)(5)(C) of the Act sets forth identical language to that contained in section 242B(c)(3), we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of A-A-, supra, and Matter of Lei, supra, by analogy.

2

We note that Matter of M-S-, supra, arose in the context of deportation proceedings and involved the interpretation of sections 242B(e)(1) and (5) of the Act, relating to the limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a deportation hearing. However, as section 240(b)(7) of the Act sets forth a similar limitation on discretionary relief for failing to appear at a removal hearing, we can apply the reasoning set forth in Matter of M-S-, supra, by analogy.

3

Pursuant to 8 C.F.R.§§ 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3), a motion to reopen for the purpose of acting on an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and all supporting documentation.

4

We therefore decline to address the issue of prima facie eligibility for relief from removal, or whether the respondent would deserve a favorable exercise of discretion.

2004 WL 2418592 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JINGBIN ZHANG
File: AXX XX0 583 - New York City
September 30, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Evelyn Marsh, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent appeals the Immigration Judge’s December 12, 2003, decision denying her motion to reopen proceedings and rescind the in absentia order of deportation entered on May 8, 1997. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
We reject the respondent’s argument that she did not receive proper notice of her hearing because she did not actually receive the Notice of Hearing. The Notice of Hearing was sent by certified mail to the respondent’s address of record, as required by section 242B(a)(2) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252b(a)(2) (repealed). The fact that the Notice was returned by the United States Postal Service as “unclaimed” does not render service inadequate. Fuentes-Argueta v. INS, 101 F.3d 867 (2d Cir. 1996); Matter of M-D-, 23 I&N Dec. 540 (BIA 2002); Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). The respondent now admits that the address she provided to the Immigration Court was not in fact her address, but rather was the address of her former lawyer (Exh. 7). We have held that an alien can be charged with receiving proper notice when notice “reaches the correct address but does not reach the alien through some failure in the internal workings of the household.” Matter of G-Y-R-, 23 I&N Dec. 181, 189 (BIA 2001). The situation alleged by the respondent is similar to but not as benign as the example given in Matter of G-Y-R-, supra. The respondent deceived the court by providing an incorrect address in order to obtain a change of venue, and will be charged with receiving proper notice of her hearing when the Notice of Hearing was mailed to that address. Accordingly, the Immigration Judge did not err by entering an in absentia order of deportation.
 
With respect to her ineffective assistance of counsel claim, the respondent has not shown that she acted with the requisite due diligence during the period of time she now seeks to equitably toll in order to render timely her motion to reopen. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000). She submits that she asked her former lawyer about the status of her case “many times” and did not learn of the in absentia order until September 20, 2003 (Exh. 7). In her brief on appeal, the respondent asserts that the last time she saw her former lawyer was on June 4, 1997, and that the next time she went to his office it was closed and no forwarding address was posted. While we cannot consider assertions made in a brief as they are not evidence of record, we acknowledge the respondent’s assertions because even if they had been properly set forth in an affidavit they would not establish that the respondent acted with due diligence. The fact that her lawyer essentially packed up and disappeared should have put her on notice that his representation in her case was deficient, and at least warranted an inquiry to the Immigration Court. The respondent does not assert that she made any inquiry into the status of her case after learning that she had been abandoned by her lawyer. Under these circumstances, the respondent has not demonstrated that she is entitled to equitable tolling of the time for filing a motion to reopen.
 
*2 For these reasons, the appeal will be dismissed.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2375139 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JUN ZHANG
File: AXX XX6 996 - Los Angeles
September 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary J. Yerman, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On March 24, 2004, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. On August 26, 2004, the respondent attempted to file a motion to reopen, but the motion was rejected by the Board’s Clerk’s Office because the respondent did not include the required fee or a fee waiver request form. The respondent then filed the motion on September 10, 2004. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. With certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before June 23, 2004. The respondent contends that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel. However, we considered this issue in our previous decision, and the ineffective assistance of counsel that the respondent complains of in the instant motion relates to an earlier failure of the respondent to appear at his scheduled removal hearing on October 9, 2002. Therefore, the reliance upon equitable tolling is misplaced as the respondent has not raised it as an excuse for the insist untimely motion. The respondent’s request for sua sponte reopening will be denied. “As a general rule, we invoke our sua sponte authority sparingly, treating it not as a general remedy for any hardships created by enforcement of the time and number limits in the motions relations, but as an extraordinary remedy reserved for truly exceptional situations.” See Matter of G-D-, 22 I&N Dec. 1132 (BIA 1999); see also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373, 380 n.9 (A.G. 2002); Matter of J-J-,21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997). The respondent has not established a truly exceptional case for such a an extraordinary remedy.
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 2375137 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EMMANUEL SEGUN
File: AXX XX3 042 - Baltimore
September 28, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda A. Dominguez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is appealing the Immigration Judge’s February 4, 2004, decision denying the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings so that his in absentia order of removal may be rescinded. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) agrees with the Immigration Judge’s decision. See DHS’s Appeal Brief.
 
We affirm and adopt the Immigration Judge’s decision and with the following comments the appeal will be dismissed. Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the ¿I ºmmigration ¿Jºudge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent argues that he did not appear at his hearing scheduled for November 19, 2003, because he was informed by his attorney that a continuance had been requested, and that it was not necessary for him to appear at the hearing. The respondent avers that he learned of the denial of the continuance request only after the entry of the in absentia order of removal. The respondent also complains that a subsequent motion to reopen was improperly filed. The respondent thus argues that he did not appear at the removal hearing because of his prior counsel’s alleged ineffectiveness, yet he has not complied with the requirements we set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, Lozada v. INS, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), for assessing whether an alien has established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Before we determine if an attorney’s representation was ineffective and affected the outcome of his case we require that an alien first provide (1) an affidavit detailing the agreement between the movant and counsel; (2) a showing that the allegations of ineffective assistance were communicated to counsel and that he or she was given an opportunity to respond to them; and (3) a statement as to whether a complaint was filed against counsel with the appropriate disciplinary authorities and if not why not. The respondent has complied with none of these requirements.
 
In denying the respondent’s motion to reopen and rescind, the Immigration Judge noted that the respondent had been properly notified concerning the date, time, and place of the hearing in question, that no continuance request was granted, and the respondent failed to provide an affidavit of explanation for his failure to appear with the motion.
 
*2 As we find that the respondent has failed to establish that he did not receive notice of his hearing, and that he has not established exceptional circumtances for his failure to appear, we find that the Immigration Judge properly denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his proceedings.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

IN RE: ANITA PADILLA SANTIAGO

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Linnette Tano Clark, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge finding the respondent subject to removal as charged and denying the respondent’s application for cancellation ofremoval. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
The respondent argues on appeal that she was provided ineffective assistance of counsel because her former counsel failed to adequately present her case. In support of the respondent’s argument, she has submitted evidence that she has filed a grievance with the state bar disciplinary committee.
 
We find that in the absence of evidence of a determination by state disciplinary authorities that the respondent’s former counsel’s representation was faulty the respondent’s argument lacks merit. The record indicates that the Immigration Judge addressed the arguments that the respondent claims were not properly presented by counsel, namely that her husband may lose some medical coverage through her and her husband may suffer future work-related medical problems.1 I.J. at 9. In sum, the respondent has failed to demonstrate that she was prejudiced by her former counsel.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

We note that hardship to the respondent is not an element considered by an Immigration Judge regarding an application for cancellation of removal. Only hardship to qualifying family members may be considered. 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(D). Even though hardship to the respondent is not an element for cancellation of removal, we note that the Immigration Judge discussed the respondent’s diabetic condition, I.J. at 4.

*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. The record reflects that the respondent appeared before an Immigration Judge, and requested the opportunity to voluntarily depart the United States. His request was granted. On appeal, the respondent argued that his attorney did not advise him of the consequences of his actions. The respondent has failed to comply with the requirements for establishing ineffective assistance of counsel. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). He also presented no evidence, and we cannot detect any from the transcript, that the respondent lacked the mental capacity to understand the nature of the removal proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.

See section 240B(d) of the Act.

IN RE: TSAGHIK MKRTCHYAN

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander Morales, Esquire
 
REISSUED DECISION
 
*1 We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on November 13, 2002. On July 22, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). We will reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and reissue our previous decision.
 
The respondent alleges that she received assistance from an individual, Ron Martinez, who worked under the supervision of attorney Walter Burrier. The respondent claims that when she received a copy of the Board’s November 13, 2002, decision, she consulted Martinez, who failed to tell her about the deadline for filing a petition for review with the Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals, and instead told her to wait to take action until her son’s visa petition was approved. Martinez responded to the charges by saying that the respondent appeared pro se before the Board, and therefore he cannot be blamed for her failure to file a court action. However, Martinez does not directly address the respondent’s assertion that she consulted him after receiving the Board’s decision. The respondent has met the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I & N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, and we will treat the motion as unopposed. The Board’s decision will be reissued and treated as if entered on today’s date. See Firmansjah v. Ashcroft, 347 F.3d 625 (7th Cir. 2003)(Board has authority to reissue decision).
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decision dated November 13, 2002, attached hereto, is hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.

2004 WL 2375064 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KET FUNG TJHIA
File: AXX XX8 562 - Newark
September 21, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
H. Raymond Fasano, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
In a decision dated April 13, 2004, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has moved to “reconsider/reopen.” The motion is one to reopen, not reconsider. See Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991) (discussing the difference between motions to reopen and reconsider). The motion will be denied.
 
First, in the motion to reopen the respondent claims that his previous counsel rendered ineffective assistance. The respondent alleges that his former counsel did not adequately explain to him the ramifications of refusing to abide by the voluntary departure order, that former counsel never asked the respondent why he feared returning to Indonesia, and when he became a Pentecostal Christian.
 
Matter of Lozada 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
It is crucial that the respondent’s former counsel be given “the opportunity to respond to the allegation¿sº of ineffectiveness,” due to the charges made by the respondent. See Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 374 (3rd Cir. Jan. 5, 2003) (A requirement of Lozada includes filing a motion and giving the allegedly ineffective counsel an opportunity to respond). See also Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 132 (3d Cir. 2001)(citing Lozada, 19 I. & N. Dec. at 639).
 
The respondent did file a bar complaint against former counsel and apparently notified him of that complaint. However, there is no evidence in the motion that former counsel was notified of the instant Lozada motion and given an opportunity to respond. As we stated in Matter of Lozada, at 639, “before allegations of ineffectiveness are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion” (emphasis added). We noted that the high standard which we enunciated was necessary if the Board is to have a basis for assessing the substantial number of claims of ineffective assistance of counsel that come before us. Id. See also Asaba v. Ashcroft, 377 F.3d 9, 12 (1st Cir. 2004) (“Even if Asaba had presented evidence that he notified Ozulumba, he failed to meet Lozada’s requirement that counsel be provided an opportunity to respond before filing the motion to reopen); Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 599 (9th Cir.2004)(affirming the Board’s decision where petitioner did not prove that he gave former counsel “notice of the ineffective assistance allegations or an adequate opportunity to respond”). See Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 496 (5th Cir.2000); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 The respondent has also failed to establish that he in fact received ineffective assistance of counsel. The record reflects that former counsel introduced documentation in support of the respondent’s applications including country conditions in Indonesia, and conducted direct examination of the respondent. Former counsel did bring out the fact that the respondent a Pentecostal Christian (Tr. at 20). Concerning the respondent’s allegation on the subject of voluntary departure, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge orally explained the consequences to the respondent if he refused to voluntarily depart from the United States. (Tr. at 45-46). The respondent was also provided a written warning to the same effect. To advance a successful claim for ineffective assistance of counsel, an alien must demonstrate prejudice, i.e., he “must show that he was prevented from reasonably presenting his case.” Uspango v. Ashcroft, 289 F.3d 226, 231 (3d Cir. 2002). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. Matter of Assaad, supra. This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred. Id. The degree of negligence that must be established for ineffectiveness is that counsel’s representation was so egregious that it rendered the hearing unfair: See Matter of B-B-, 22 I&N Dec. 309 (BIA 1998). The respondent has made no such showing. See also Xu Yong Lu v. Ashcroft, 259 F.3d 127, 131 (3d Cir.2001). The respondent is dissatisfied with former counsel’s representation because he lost his case, but he has not shown that the hearing was unfair or that he was “prevented from reasonably presenting his case.”
 
The respondent also moves to reopen based upon a change in country conditions. The documentation that the respondent has attached to his motion does not differ materially from the documentation that was submitted during the removal proceeding. Moreover, most of the documentation pre-dates the respondent’s removal hearing which concluded on February 5, 2003, and thus can not be considered new evidence. On review, we do not find that the respondent has established changed country conditions within the meaning of 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3)(ii). A motion to reopen under 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) will not be granted unless the movant establishes a prima facie case of eligibility for the underlying relief sought. See INS v. Abudu, 485 U.S. 94 (1988).
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

IN RE: SALGADO, MARIA CRUZ

*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated October 02, 2003. We agree with the Immigration Judge that the respondent is not eligible for cancellation of removal because she has not established the requisite continuous physical presence. The respondent testified that she arrived in the United States after April 1992; the Notice to Appear was served on the respondent in February 2002. Because of the inability to establish continuous physical presence, the respondent is not eligible for cancellation of removal. See section 240A(b)(1)(A) of the Act. On appeal, the respondent has raised the issue of ineffective assistance of counsel below. However, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and no claim has been established. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 2375045 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AMINA MOUJTAHID
File: AXX XX8 080 - Seattle
September 21, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Robert Pauw, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Eric Bakken
Assistant District Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed an Immigration Judge’s October 22, 2002, decision denying her request for a continuance pending her appeal of the Department of Homeland Security’s (DHS) denial of a visa petition filed on her behalf by her United States citizen husband. While the appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision was pending, the respondent submitted a motion to remand in order to pursue her application for adjustment of status.
 
A decision whether to continue deportation proceedings to a later date is committed to the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge. Matter of W-F-, 21 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1996); Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29, 1240.6. A continuance may be granted, in the Immigration Judge’s discretion, if good cause is shown. 8 C.F.R. § 1240.6. A decision to deny a continuance will not be overturned on appeal unless it appears that the respondent was deprived of a full and fair hearing. Matter of Perez-Andrade, supra; Matter of Namio, 14 I&N Dec. 412 (BIA 1973). As the respondent requested, in essence, an indefinite continuance we find no error on the part of the Immigration Judge in denying the same. On appeal the respondent suggests that the attorney she retained to pursue her visa petition provided her with ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent has not satisfied the requirements for making such a claim as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). The Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to generally be reasonable, but has held that “they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). However, the respondent has not satisfied any of the noted requirements, nor has she shown that their purpose has been otherwise met. As such, a remand on that basis is not warranted.
 
The respondent submitted a motion to remand in July 2003 generally asserting that she and her husband have filed a new visa petition, and that she is, therefore, eligible for adjustment of status. The DHS opposes the respondent’s motion, noting that the respondent has filed this visa petition while an appeal of the denial of her first visa petition remains pending and that both visa petitions were filed by the same petitioner. Here the factors enumerated in Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002) are not present. For example, the DHS does not base its opposition on our holding in Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992), but notes that the respondent admittedly had sexual relations with her ex-husband while married to the petitioner. We also observe that while the respondent asserts a new visa petition has been filed on her behalf, it is by the same petitioner, based on the same basis for adjustment; their marriage. To follow the logic of the respondent’s motion, she need only repeatedly file visa petitions by the same petitioner and on the same basis to warrant recurrent reopening of her claim. However, as the respondent has not shown that the current visa petition is by a new petitioner and/or on a new basis, she has not demonstrated that it is new, nor has she shown that she is prima facie eligible for adjustment of status. Matter of Ige, 20 I&N Dec. 880 (BIA 1994); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991). As such, a remand is not warranted.
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed and the respondent’s motion to remand is denied.

2004 WL 2375034 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SIGFREDO ANTONIO GALLEGOS IVETTE MITCHELLE HONORES-WALAQUEZ JOSE ANTONIO GALLEGOS-HONORES
Files: AXX XX6 184 - Dallas
AXX XX6 185
AXX XX6 186
September 20, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
David Swaim, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Thomas R. Murphy
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents filed a motion to reconsider with the Board. The respondents argued in their motion that the Board incorrectly found in its order dated July 20, 2004 that they failed to establish that they were the victims of ineffective assistance from their former counsel.
 
Upon review of the record, we note that various submissions that were submitted with the respondents’ motion to reopen were not included at the time of the Board’s prior decision. These documents are now present, and have been reviewed. These documents reveal that the respondents’ former counsel was notified of the allegations against him, and his response to the allegations is included in the record. We therefore conclude that the respondents complied with the requirement that they lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) and Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
The respondents’ further argued in their motion that unless ordered by a court, Texas law prohibits them from disclosing whether a disciplinary action has been filed against their former counsel, and they are not required to do so under Matter of Lozada, supra.. Notwithstanding the respondents’ assertions, we note that the Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals recognizes as valid the procedural requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, supra. See Goonsuwan v. Ashcroft, 252 F.3d 383, 389 (5th Cir. 2001); Lara v. Trominski, 216 F.3d 487, 497 (5th Cir. 2000). We therefore cannot excuse the respondents from the requirement that they must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
In addition, the respondents failed to show that they were prejudiced by their counsel’s failure to file a brief. As we indicated in our earlier decision, this case arises within the jurisdiction of the Fifth Circuit Court of Appeals, which has held that an alien seeking to prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must show not only that counsel was ineffective, but that he suffered “substantial prejudice” as a result of counsel’s errors. Miranda- Lores v. INS, 17 F.3d 84, 85 (5th Cir. 1994); Ogbemudia v. INS, 988 F.2d 595, 598 (5th Cir. 1993). We repeat, here the respondents fail to show substantial prejudice stemming from their attorneys’ alleged failings. The Immigration Judge denied the respondents asylum and withholding of removal because he determined that the respondents had shown neither past persecution, nor a well-founded fear of future persecution, in their native Peru. In their prior and instant motion, the respondents fail to address how the Immigration Judge erred in coming to this conclusion, or why they would have been granted relief, notwithstanding the failure of counsel to file a brief. The respondents do not present the brief that they claim would have caused the Board to reverse the Immigration Judge’s determination, but instead promise to file such a brief only if the Board issues a new briefing schedule. We therefore find no error in our prior decision, and see no reason to reconsider it.
 
*2 Accordingly, the respondents’ motion to reconsider is denied.
