2004 WL 2375007 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ANURATANA SACRA
File: A31 306 480 - Oakdale
September 17, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Nora E. Norman
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on June 30, 2004, when we affirmed the Immigration Judge’s September 12, 2003, decision denying the respondent’s claim that he is a national of the United States, and denied his motion to remand based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent has now filed a motion to reopen. The motion will be denied.1
 
Through his motion, the respondent reiterates his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel and that he is a national of the United States. In support of these claims, the respondent has proffered photocopies of the same documents submitted during his proceedings and previously considered by the Board and the Immigration Judge. Because the respondent has failed to submit material, previously undiscoverable evidence in support of his claim of ineffective assistance of counsel or his assertion that he is a national of the United States, he has necessarily failed to substantiate his burden for reopening on these bases. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c).
 
The respondent’s motion makes the new assertion that he is entitled to derivative citizenship, as predicated upon the naturalization of his biological mother in 1985. However, the respondent has failed to make a prima facie showing of derivative citizenship under the section 320 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, as amended by the Child Citizenship Act of 2000 (“CCA”), Pub. L. No. 106-395, as required by regulation for reopening. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(3). In particular, this Board has held that the citizenship provisions as enacted by the CCA donor apply retroactively. See Matter of Rodriguez-Tejedor, 23 I&N Dec. 153 (BIA 2001). As a result, in order to derive United States citizenship under the CCA, a respondent must have been under the age of 18 years at the time the statute was enacted. Id. (Applying the law in effect at the time the last material condition of the statute is met). The record in this case reflects that the respondent, born in 1963, was well over the age of 18 years at the time the CCA was enacted on February 27, 2001. See Matter of Rodriguez-Tejedor, supra.
 
The respondent is clearly statutorily ineligible for derivative citizenship under the CCA and thus a hearing allowing the respondent to present evidence in support of his claim is not warranted. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

Although the respondent captions his motion as a “motion to reopen and reconsider and/or remand,” it is properly classified as a motion to reopen as the respondent alleges new facts to be proven at a hearing. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1) (2004).

2004 WL 2374999 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARIA DELORAS JEAN A.K.A. MARIE DOLORES JEAN A.K.A. MARIE DOLORES DUVERSAINT A.K.A. MARIE DOLORES JEAN-DUVERSAINT
File: AXX XX3 585 - Baltimore
September 17, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas D. Cramer, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Linda A. Dominguez
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reconsideration; reopening
 
*1 This matter was last before us on April 30, 2004, when we affirmed an Immigration Judge’s orders denying the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal, adjustment of status, waiver of inadmissibility, and voluntary departure. The respondent now moves that we reopen proceedings and reconsider our prior decision. The Department of Homeland Security (formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motions. The respondent’s request for oral argument is denied. The motions will be denied.
 
A motion to reconsider requests that the original decision be re-examined in light of additional legal arguments, a change of law, or an argument or aspect of the case that was overlooked. Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991); see also 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b). Upon review of the motion to reconsider, we find no new legal argument or particular aspect of the case which was overlooked and no ground upon which to reconsider our previous decision.
 
Turning to the respondent’s motion to reopen, a motion to reopen filed with the Board must, among other things, state the new facts to be considered at the reopened hearing and be supported by affidavits or other evidentiary materials demonstrating prima facie eligibility for the relief sought. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(1). Statements in a motion are not evidence and thus are not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980). In support of the motion, the respondent submitted evidence claiming her rehabilitation, and evidence reflecting that her removal from the United States would impose on her relatives significant hardship. However, such evidence is essentially cumulative of evidence which was of record at the time of our April 30, 2004, order.
 
The respondent claims that her attorney who represented her during the proceedings before the Immigration Judge failed to raise pertinent objections during the hearing. This, the respondent implies, amounted to ineffective assistance of counsel. However, other than advancing the aforementioned allegation in the motions to reconsider and reopen, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements necessary to establish a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Accordingly, we find it inappropriate to reopen proceedings.
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reconsider is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 2374995 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GLORIA RUIZ SOLANO
File: A70 817 238 - Los Angeles
September 17, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Eudene Eunique, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision denying the respondent’s motions to reopen and reconsider. We first note that the respondent, in the Notice of Appeal, indicates she is appealing the Immigration Judge’s order dated March 27, 2003. There is no order with that date. Instead, the record reflects that the Immigration Judge issued an order on February 14, 2003, denying the respondent’s January 31, 2003, motion to reopen. On February 28, 2003, the respondent then filed a motion to reconsider, which the Immigration Judge denied on March 20, 2003, apparently considering it a motion to reopen. On April 17, 2003, the Immigration Judge issued another order and Summary Decision denying the respondents motion to reconsider. We will consider the respondent’s appeal to encompass all three orders.
 
On appeal, the respondent contends, as she did in her motion to reopen, that she was provided ineffective assistance of counsel in that her former counsel failed to advise the Immigration Judge during the proceedings that the respondent was married to a United States citizen and also did not file for relief.1
 
A motion to reopen for the purpose of submitting an application for relief must be accompanied by the appropriate application for relief and accompanied by all the supporting documentation. 8 C.F.R. §1003.23(b)(3). A motion to reopen will not be granted unless the respondent establishes a prima facie case of eligibility for the underlying relief sought. With her motion, the respondent submitted only her certificate of marriage and evidence of her husband’s citizenship. There is no evidence if a visa petition has been filed on her behalf, in addition, no Application to Adjust Status (Form I-486) has been submitted. Moreover, because the respondent does not have an approved visa petition, she must provide clear and convincing evidence indicating a strong likelihood, that the marriage is bona fide. Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002). The respondent has not provided any document showing joint ownership of property, a lease showing joint tenancy of a common residency, documentation showing commingling of financial resources, or other evidence which might establish the bona fides of his marriage. Id.
 
Regarding cancellation of removal, the respondent likewise has failed to proffer an application for relief, and has failed to show prima facie evidence of her eligibility. Specifically, she has not provided any evidence to show she has been continuously physically present for 10 years’ prior to service of the Notice to Appear. Nor has she articulated how her husband will suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if she is removed. See section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act.
 
*2 Consequently, we find that the respondent failed to establish eligibility to adjust her status or for cancellation of removal, and as such, she has failed to show that she suffered prejudice from her former counsel’s assistance. “Due process challenges to deportation proceedings require a showing of prejudice to succeed.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 527 (9th Cir. 2000). “Prejudice is found when the performance of counsel was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings.” Ortiz v. INS, 179 F.3d 1148, 1153 (9th Cir. 1999) (quoting Barraza Rivera v. INS, 913 F.2d 1443, 1448 (9th Cir. 1990)). We thus conclude that the Immigration Judge did not err in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

FOR THE BOARD

On appeal, the respondent fails to articulate any form of relief for which she might be eligible. In her motions before the Immigration Judge, she maintained that she would be eligible for cancellation of removal and/or adjustment of status.

2004 WL 2374976 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OKU, ELIAS
File: AXX-XX9-598 - San Diego
September 16, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Razi, Mike
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated October 14, 2003. The respondent requests reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent states that he was unable to timely file a petition for judicial review, because his previous attorney mailed the notice of the Board’s decision to an incorrect address. While the period for filing the motion can properly be tolled due to the ineffective assistance of counsel and fraud, the respondent is expected to file the motion within the specified period for motions to reopen after learning about the Board’s decision. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1196 (9th Cir. 2001). In this case, the respondent states that he learned of this Board’s decision on December 1, 2003. However, he did not file this motion to reopen until April 13, 2004. As the respondent failed to exercise diligence in filing the motion within 90 days of learning about our previous order dismissing the appeal, and as the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion to reopen, we decline to exercise our sua sponte authority to reopen proceedings. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2374967 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KOKSAL YILMAZ
File: A70 851 394 - Philadelphia
September 16, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Maria Isabel A.N. Thomas, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ira L. Mazer
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 241(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - failed to comply with conditions of status
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a male native and citizen of Turkey. This case was last before us on September 26, 2002, when we denied the respondent’s second motion to reopen proceedings. In our September 26, 2002, decision, we found that the respondent’s motion was barred by the numerical limitations delineated in 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). With respect to the current motion to reopen, we find that such motion is both untimely and numerically barred. Consequently, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be denied.
 
On March 22, 2004, the respondent filed a third motion to reopen, in which he alleges ineffective assistance of counsel and in which he again seeks to remand proceedings in order to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent’s current motion to reopen must be denied as both untimely filed and numerically barred, as it does not fall within any of the enumerated exceptions to the time and number restrictions on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of the Board’s decision and parties are limited to one motion to reopen).
 
The respondent avers, in his third motion to reopen, that his former counsel rendered ineffective assistance because he failed to properly file a previous motion to reopen by the September 30, 1996 filing date, and because he failed to attach (to the first motion to reopen) an application for adjustment of status pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) and 1003.23(b)(3). The respondent alleges that this constitutes ineffective assistance of counsel and alleges that the failure to properly file his motion to reopen prejudiced his case. Thus, the respondent argues that the time limit for his motion should be subject to equitable tolling and that we should grant his motion to reopen
 
We note that this case arises within the jurisdiction of the United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit. The Third Circuit has indicated that it has not decided whether to adopt the remedy of equitable tolling, with regard to filing deadlines delineated in the Act, pertaining to motions to reopen which are based on attorney malfeasance. See Bejar v. Ashcroft, 324 F.3d 127, 131 (FN 1) (3rd Cir. 2003). We note that, even if this approach were adopted, in order to apply the doctrine of equitable tolling to the respondent’s motion to reopen, the respondent would need to show that he acted with “reasonable diligence through the period that he seeks to have tolled.” See generally Iavorski v. U.S. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 134 (2d Cir. 2000). We observe that the respondent was put on notice of his prior counsel’s actions, including the date on which his previous motions to reopen were filed and the fact that an application for adjustment of status was not filed with his first motion to reopen, when we rendered our November 10, 1999, decision, and again when we rendered our September 26, 2002, decision. Yet, the respondent did not file a motion to reopen proceedings, alleging ineffective assistance of counsel, until March 22, 2004. Thus, even if we presume that the respondent received ineffective assistance of counsel, and if we presume that equitable tolling is available, we do not find that the respondent acted with reasonable diligence during the period that he wishes to have tolled.
 
*2 In sum, we find that the respondent’s motion is untimely and number-barred, and the respondent has failed to establish that equitable tolling (if available) should be applied to the deadline at issue. Hence, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2374967 (BIA)
End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2004 WL 2374322 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: FEI LIN
File: AXX XX0 808 - New York
September 14, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Thomas V. Massucci, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Virna A. Wright
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(i)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1.003.2 to reconsider its June 10, 2004, order dismissing the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying an earlier motion to reopen proceedings. The Department for Homeland Security (“DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion. The motion will be granted.
 
In support of his motion, the respondent has submitted a copy of the New York Departmental Disciplinary Committee Rules and Procedures describing its notice to attorneys of complaints against them and the opportunity provided to the respondent attorneys to respond to the allegations. This material supplements evidence in the file consisting, in relevant part, of an affidavit by the respondent. describing the whole of his immigration proceedings and his interaction with his previous attorney specifically, including the actions and representations that counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard, and a cover letter for a complaint against his prior attorney as well as a certified mail receipt reflecting a mailing to the New York Departmental Disciplinary Committee. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (confirming the holding of Matter of Lozada, infra); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) (discussing requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel); see also Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 128-29 (2nd Cir. 2000).
 
As such, the respondent appears to have substantially complied with the threshold requirements pursuant to Matter of Lozada, supra. Accordingly, the motion is granted and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion and for the entry of a new decision.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted.

2004 WL 2374304 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ROMEL EDMUNDO RODRIGUEZ
File: AXX XX0 688 - Phoenix
September 8, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Brent Landis
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
Sec. 212(a)(2)(A)(i)(l), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. §1182(a)(2)(A)(i)(I)º - Crime involving moral turpitude
 
  
APPLICATION: Remand
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated March 3, 2004. The Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his removal proceedings, based upon the Immigration Judge’s finding that the respondent had failed to comply with the requirements for establishing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim. On appeal, the respondent argues that he has substantially complied with the requirements for establishing an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, as set forth in the Board’s decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and requests that the record be remanded to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s applications for a waiver of inadmissibility and adjustment of status under sections 212(h) and 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. The respondent’s appeal will be sustained and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings.
 
A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, supra. In Matter of Lozada, supra, we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. However, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held that an alien need not fulfill all the procedural requirements of Maner of Lozada, supra, to assert claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when the record reflects a clear and obvious case of such. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (holding that attorney’s failure to timely file a suspension of deportation application was negligence); see also Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331 (9th Cir. 2000); Varela v. INS, 204 F.3d 1237 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
*2 In this matter, we find that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. The respondent has submitted an affidavit indicating that he provided affidavits and other documents necessary to complete his applications for relief to his former attorney, who he trusted would file the application with the Immigration Court in a timely manner and failed to do so. Additionally, the respondent submitted an affidavit from his current attorney, which indicates that he mailed a copy of the respondent’s motion to reopen proceedings to his prior attorney, in an effort to provide him notice of the allegations made against him. The respondent also submitted a copy of the complaint filed with the State Bar of California detailing the ineffectiveness of his prior attorney’s representation and a copy of a letter from the State Bar of California indicating receipt of the complaint. Based upon the respondent’s submissions, we find that the respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra.
 
In addition to the three procedural requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, it is also required that the claimant establish that he suffered prejudice as a result of his attorney’s ineffectiveness. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003) (stating that a due process violation requires a showing of prejudice, which can be made if counsel’s performance “was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of the proceedings”). The term “prejudice,” refers to actual prejudice. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). This means that it is likely that an alien would have prevailed at the hearing or on appeal had the negligent representation not occurred, Id. In his motion to reopen, the respondent maintains that the ineffective assistance of his prior counsel precluded him from filing his application for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act in a timely manner. In support of his motion, the respondent submitted an approved immediate relative visa petition (Form I-130) and an application for adjustment of status (Form I-485, Form I-485A). In addition, the respondent has submitted an application for a waiver of inadmissibility under section 212(h) of the Act to waive his inadmissibility for a crime involving moral turpitude and an affidavit from his wife alleging the hardship she and her daughters would face if the respondent is removed from the United States. Based upon the evidence in the record, we find that the respondent has established prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525-26 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding a “clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” where counsel “failed, without any reason, to timely file ¿anº application” for suspension where petitioner was prima facie eligible for relief).
 
*3 Inasmuch as the respondent has complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra, and established prima facie eligibility for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, we remand the record to the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s applications for a waiver of inadmissibility and adjustment of status under sections 212(h) and 245 of the Act. Accordingly, the respondent’s appeal is sustained and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

IN RE: NERITAN NMI FYRAJ IRIS FYRAJ

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
John G. Chupa, Esquire
 
REISSUED DECISIONS
 
*1 We dismissed the respondents’ appeals in separate decisions on July 14, 2003. On April 26, 2004, the respondents filed a motion to reopen, seeking to have the Board reissue its previous decisions.1 We denied the motion on July 9, 2004. The respondents filed a motion to reconsider on July 19, 2004. We will grant the motion to reconsider, reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and reissue our July 14, 2003, decisions. We note that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposed reopening, but has not provided a position concerning the motion to reconsider.
 
The respondents alleged in their motion to reopen that their former attorney failed to inform them of our July 14, 2003, decision, so that they could file a petition for review with the Sixth Circuit Court of Appeals. They asserted that they learned of our decision in March, 2004, and the motion to reopen was filed the next month. In the affidavit supporting the motion, the lead respondent stated that he had been unable to contact former counsel with the claims, because counsel was “in hiding.” The lead respondent also stated that he was preparing to file a grievance against former counsel. In our July 9, 2004, decision, we found that the respondents had failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 191 & N Dec. 637 (BIA1988). Specifically, we found that the respondents had failed to file a disciplinary complaint against counsel.
 
In the motion to reconsider, the respondents argue that they, in fact, filed a complaint against former counsel with the Michigan Supreme Court Attorney Grievance Commission prior to our July 9, 2004, decision denying the motion to reopen. A review of the record supports this assertion. The respondents submitted a document containing evidence of a grievance prior to the Board’s July 9, 2004, decision, which was not associated with the record prior to our decision. With that pleading, the respondents also submitted another affidavit of the lead respondent, which more fully detailed his attempts to locate former counsel and notify him of the claims. Upon review of the respondents’ submissions, we find that we should reconsider our July 9, 2004, decision. We find that the respondents complied with Matter of Assaad, supra, and Matter of Lozada, supra, for making a claim against counsel. Therefore, although the motion to reopen was filed out of time, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2), we will reopen proceedings under our sua sponte authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a), and reissue our July 14, 2003, decisions. The Board’s decisions will be reissued and treated as if entered on today’s date. See Firmansjah v. Ashcroft, 347 F.3d 625 (7th Cir. 2003)(Board has authority to reissue decision).
 
*2 ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reconsider is granted, and our July 9, 2004, decision is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondents’ motion to reissue is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The Board’s decisions dated July 14, 2903, attached hereto, are hereby reissued and shall be treated as entered as of today’s date.

2004 WL 2374283 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GUILLERMO ROBLES CUEVAS
File: AXX XX1 468 - Miami
September 7, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Daphne M. Query, Esquire
*1 In an order entered on April 28, we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has, through counsel, filed a motion to reopen, which is not opposed by the Department of Homeland Security. The motion will be granted.
 
The respondent, who seeks to adjust his status, has provided evidence that he divorced his previous wife on April 1, 2003; and married a United States citizen on January 18, 2004. The respondent’s current wife has filed a visa petition on his behalf. An alien who enters into a marriage while in removal proceedings is presumed to have done so to evade the immigration laws of this country, and the burden is on him to demonstrate the bona fides of that marriage. Pursuant to Matter of Velarde-Pacheco, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), the respondent must demonstrate the following in order to receive consideration of his motion: (1) the motion is timely filed; (2) the motion is not numerically barred by the regulations; (3) the motion is not barred by Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996), or on any other procedural grounds; (4) clear and convincing evidence is presented indicating a strong likelihood that the marriage is bona fide; and (5) the Department does not oppose the motion or bases its opposition solely on Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992).
 
We find sufficient compliance, although barely so, with Matter of Velarde-Pacheco, supra, to warrant reopening and remanding this case, although we emphasize that our remand does not establish the bona fides of the reported marriage. It will be the respondenl’s burden to persuade the Department, in the context of a visa petition, and the Immigration Judge, in the context of adjustment, that his marriage is bona fide and not entered into in order to evade the immigration laws of the United States. See 8 C.F.R. § 1245.1(c)(9)(v).
 
During the forthcoming hearing, the respondent should be given the opportunity to explain why he failed to inform the Board, prior to our order, of the change in his marital status and a change in the number and identity of his family members who were claiming derivative status based on his circumstances, if he, on April 1, 2003, divorced Carmen R. Pinilla Velandia, whose alien number is AXX XXX XXX and who joined him as a derivative claimant during the proceedings. If the Immigration Judge determines that this matter raises an issue of the respondent’s worthiness for the relief he seeks, then it should be specifically addressed.
 
We find nothing in the evidence submitted with the motion to revisit our determination regarding the respondent’s previous allegation that he was prejudiced by ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 Should the respondent find that his removal is imminent, he may file an emergency request for a stay with the Board.
 
ORDER: The motion is granted, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

2004 WL 2374310 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: YUN KYEONG KWON SUNG
File: AXX XX7 504 - Las Vegas
September 3, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ubiwe K. Eriye, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening; cancellation of removal; voluntary departure
 
*1 This matter was last before the Board on June 30, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal and denied her motion to remand. On August 2, 2004, the respondent filed the present motion to reopen in which she seeks to apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), and voluntary departure under section 240B(b) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(b). In support of the motion, the respondent contends that her right to a full and fair hearing was violated because neither the Immigration Judge nor her former attorney informed her that she was eligible to apply for cancellation of removal or voluntary departure. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS”), formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service, has filed no response to the motion. The motion will be granted, the removal proceedings will be reopened, and the record will be remanded for further proceedings.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1240.11 (a)(2), Immigration Judges have an affirmative duty to inform an alien in removal proceedings of his or her “apparent eligibility” to apply for any form of relief under the Act—including cancellation of removal and voluntary departure—and to afford the alien an opportunity to submit applications for relief during removal hearing. The United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in whose jurisdiction this proceeding arises, holds that this regulatory requirement is “mandatory” and that an Immigration Judge’s failure to inform an alien of her apparent eligibility to apply for relief is a denial of due process that invalidates the underlying proceeding. United States v. Muro-Inclan, 249 F.3d 1180, 1183-84 (9th Cir. 2001); United States v. Arrieta, 224 F.3d 1076, 1079 (9th Cir. 2000). The precedents of this Board are not to the contrary. Matter of Cordova, 22 I&N Dec. 966, 970-71 (BIA 1999); Matter of Ulloa, 22 I&N Dec. 725, 726 (BIA 1999).
 
Although we express no opinion as to whether the respondent can ultimately sustain her burden of demonstrating eligibility for cancellation of removal or voluntary departure, we conclude at the outset that she was “apparently eligible” for both forms of relief at the time of her prior removal hearing. See sections 240A(b)(1) and 240B(b)(1) of the Act. Specifically, the present record contains no evidence to refute the respondent’s factual assertions as to the length of her physical presence in the United States and, although criminal proceedings are pending against her, she has not to our knowledge been convicted of any crime. The Immigration Judge made no factual determinations with respect to any matter bearing upon the respondent’s moral character, and we find nothing in the record that would necessarily preclude her from demonstrating good moral character during the relevant periods, although the outcome of her pending criminal proceedings could obviously have an impact on this question. Moreover, the respondent is married to a lawful permanent resident of the United States whose hardship is relevant to a determination of eligibility for cancellation of removal, although hardship to the respondent’s son does not appear to be relevant because, as a 22-year-old man, he is no longer a “child” for purposes of the Act. See section 101 (b)(1) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101 (b)(1) (defining a “child”as “an unmarried person under twenty-one years of age…”).
 
*2 Because the respondent was apparently eligible for cancellation of removal and voluntary departure at the time of the Immigration Judge’s decision, the Immigration Judge had a duty to inform her of the availability of these forms of relief and to give her an opportunity to apply for them. Having reviewed the record of proceedings in detail, including the transcript of the respondent’s various removal hearings, we find no indication that the Immigration Judge provided such advisals. The Immigration Judge noted on several occasions that no requests for relief had been submitted to him by the respondent, other than the request for adjustment of status that was the subject of the respondent’s prior proceedings (Tr. at 112, 113), and the Immigration Judge also received a negative reply from the respondent’s counsel when he asked whether the respondent was seeking any relief other than adjustment of status (Tr. at 114). However, the Immigration Judge apparently did not make any independent determination as to the respondent’s eligibility for forms of relief not specifically requested, such as cancellation of removal and voluntary departure, and he plainly did not inform her or her counsel on the record of the existence of such alternative forms of relief. Hence, under applicable Ninth Circuit precedent, the respondent’s due process right to a full and fair hearing was violated in this instance regardless of whether the respondent’s former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel. Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen will be granted and the record will be remanded so that the respondent’s right to seek relief from removal may be preserved.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The removal proceedings are reopened and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing decision.

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  Review Granted, Cause Remanded by Ghahremani v. Gonzales, 9th Cir.(Guam), August 17, 2007
2004 WL 2418659 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PARVIZ GHAHREMANI
File: AXX XX5 604 - Los Angeles
September 1, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Parmjeet Kaur Randhawa, Esquire
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
*1 The respondent moves the Board to reopen the deportation proceedings. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
During the deportation hearing the respondent was represented by his first counsel Kazbek Soobsokov, Esquire. The Immigration Judge granted the respondent withholding of deportation to Iran, but denied his application for 212(c) relief or protection under the Convention Against Torture. The respondent obtained second counsel, Nadia Farah, Esquire, to represent him on appeal. The respondent did not raise any issue of ineffective assistance of his first counsel on direct appeal. On July 16, 2003, the Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent; through second counsel, moved to reconsider, and on February 5, 2004, the Board denied the motion. On May 28, 2004, the respondent, through present counsel, filed the instant motion to reopen due to the alleged ineffective assistance of his first counsel.
 
The respondent’s motion to reopen is untimely. With certain exceptions not relevant here, a motion to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the administratively final order. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before October 14, 2003. The respondent contends that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled due to ineffective assistance of counsel. Individuals seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that they have acted with due diligence. Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1225 (9th Cir. 2002) (acknowledging that party claiming that equitable tolling applies to reopening deadline must show that he acted with reasonable diligence); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176, 1184 (9th Cir. 2001) (en banc); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999); Beffa v. Bank of West, 152 F.3d 1174, 1176-77 (9th Cir. 1998)(“¿fºederal courts apply the doctrine of equitable tolling only sparingly, especially in cases in which plaintiffs fail to exercise due diligence”); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976, 984 (BIA 1997).
 
We find that the respondent’s appeal deadline should not be equitable tolled. In Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9th Cir. 2003), the court found equitable tolling when on October 11, 2000, the alien’s former counsel sent him a letter informing him that he had lost his appeal and had to leave the country. The letter also told the alien that he would probably not prevail should he choose to appeal the decision to the Ninth Circuit. Immediately thereafter, the alien retained new counsel for advice. The court found that the alien had acted with due diligence in discovering the error. The court emphasized that the alien “promptly retained new counsel,” and “¿lºess than one month elapsed from the denial of the appeal to the date that the alien learned of his former counsel’s allegedly fraudulent and ineffective activities. Id at 899. In the instant case the respondent retained new counsel to represent him on appeal yet the issue was never raised on appeal. The respondent, through second counsel, also filed a motion to reconsider, but never filed a motion alleging ineffective assistance of his first counsel. The respondent has manifestly failed to establish due diligence by waiting over seven months to file the motion after the expiration of the deadline for filing a motion to reopen. We also find that the respondent waived the issue by failing to raise the claim of ineffective assistance of counsel when he had retained second counsel on direct appeal. Matter of Edwards, 20 I&N Dec. 191, 196, n.4 (BIA 1990).
 
*2 ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 2418660 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOHAMMED, AMIRA
File: AXX-XX2-318 - ARLINGTON
September 1, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Hadeed Jr., Michael
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reconsider our decision dated June 15, 2004. In our June 15, 2004, decision we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen. The present motion will be denied.
 
Because the respondent submits new evidence, we will consider her motion as a motion to reopen and reconsider.
 
The motion to reopen exceeds the numerical limitations for motions to reopen and will be denied. Our prior order in these proceedings, in which the Board denied a motion to reopen filed by the respondent, was entered on June 15, 2004. The regulations provide that motions to reopen shall be limited to one motion to reopen, with certain exceptions not pertinent here. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The motion to reopen is therefore barred by numerical limitations.
 
The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on October 7, 2003. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2)(with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before January 5, 2004. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until July 12, 2004. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
We find no basis to reconsider our June 15, 2004, decision. Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), was decided on March 6, 2002. The respondent, through counsel, did not file her previous motion to reopen until December 12, 2003. The respondent does not allege ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
On our own motion, we correct one error in our June 15, 2004, decision. The regulation we cited should have been 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(1)(iii)(B) (evidence to establish eligibility for the bona fide marriage exemption). This regulation lists the type of evidence necessary to qualify for the exemption from the general prohibition against approval of a visa petition based on an alien’s marriage during proceedings, and supports our findings in our June 15, 2004, decision.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: CRUZ MOYA, LUIS ALBERTO
File: AXX XX4 800 - LOS ANGELES
August 31, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before this Board on March 29, 2004, when we returned the record to the Immigration Judge in order to prepare a separate oral or written decision. On April 23, 2004, the Immigration Court administratively returned the record to this Board for consideration of the respondent’s original appeal with the Oral Decision of the Immigration Judge contained in the record of proceedings. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent also raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim on appeal. The respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002)(Lozada requirements need not always be rigidly applied); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331, 1335 (9th Cir. 2000), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000) (application of Lozada requirements generally reasonable unless facts relating to claim of ineffective assistance of counsel are apparent in the record). Finally, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 2418644 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMIREZ SANCHEZ, WILLIAM
File: AXX-XX1-594 - LOS ANGELES
August 31, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
XAVIER ROSAS
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). Contrary to the respondent’s assertions on appeal, the Immigration Judge’s decision reflects that the potential hardships to the respondent’s United States wife of several months were considered (I.J. at 3, 7-8, 10). Based on the evidence presented at the hearing, the record fully supports the Immigration Judge’s determination that hardships reaching the exceptional and extremely unusual level were not shown with respect to either his wife or his four-year-old United States citizen child.
 
To the extent that the respondent is now making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we find that, even assuming the respondent had substantially complied with the steps required by Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), he has not demonstrated any prejudice flowing from the ineffective representation. See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000) (finding that the Lozada requirements need not be rigidly enforced where their objective is achieved); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097, 1100 (9th Cir. 1999) (there must be a showing of prejudice resulting from the ineffective assistance); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) (same).
 
The hardships that the respondent asserts on appeal with respect to each of his qualifying relatives are those common to lawful status family members who are separated from a relative who has no lawful status as a result of removal proceedings. Even if they had been presented at the hearing, full consideration of hardships of this level would not affect the outcome of these proceedings. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the alien is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the alien fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the alien shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 2418643 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SALVADOR ALCAZAR-VARGAS A.K.A. SALVADOR ALCAZAR
File: A73 920 541 - San Francisco
August 31, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ricardo Cavallera, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Marci L. Ellsworth
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; voluntary departure; adjustment of status; remand
 
The respondent appeals from the Immigration Judge’s May 5, 2003, decision finding him removable as charged and ineligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(b), and voluntary departure. The respondent also requests a remand to apply for adjustment of status. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
On appeal, the respondent argues that the record contains insufficient evidence to establish that he was convicted of an aggravated felony and, therefore, the Immigration Judge erred in finding the respondent ineligible for cancellation of removal. In addition, the respondent appears to assert that the Immigration Judge’s reliance on that evidence violates the respondent’s right to due process. The respondent’s arguments are without merit.
 
On his application for cancellation of removal, the respondent indicated that he had been convicted of a firearms offense for which he received 2 years of probation (Exh. 3 at 5). During the course of the proceedings before the Immigration Judge, the respondent, through counsel, informed the court that the respondent was convicted of the offense of possessing a firearm in violation of Title 18 U.S.C. section 922(g)(5), which is an aggravated felony pursuant to section 101(a)(43)(E)(ii) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(E)(ii) (Tr. at 23, 50-53, 55-60).
 
The record contains sufficient evidence to support the Immigration Judge’s conclusion that the respondent has been convicted of an aggravated felony and, as a result, is ineligible for cancellation for removal. See section 240(A)(b)(1)(C) of the Act. The Immigration Judge properly relied on the respondent’s admissions concerning his conviction and afforded the respondent every opportunity to meet his burden of proving eligibility for relief from removal. Furthermore, the respondent was represented by counsel and received a full and fair hearing on the merits of his claim before the Immigration Judge.1 Thus, we find no due process violation or prejudice. As the conviction issue is dispositive, we decline to address the issue of whether the respondent has sustained his burden of proving the requisite level of hardship.
 
In addition, the Immigration Judge properly denied voluntary departure finding that, as an alien convicted of an aggravated felony within the proscribed period, the respondent failed to establish the requisite good moral character (I.J. at 8-9). See sections 240B(b)(1)(B), 101(f)(8) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229c(b)(1)(B), 1101(f)(8). On whole, the record supports the Immigration Judge’s resolution.
 
*2 On appeal, the respondent also requests a remand asserting that, as the beneficiary of an approved visa petition, he is eligible to adjust his status. Although the respondent has submitted a copy of an approved visa petition, he has not submitted a copy of a properly executed adjustment application and supporting documentation. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c) (2004); see also Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464, 471 (BIA 1992). Consequently, the respondent has failed to establish prima facie eligibility for adjustment and, therefore, we decline to remand the case. See Matter of S-V-, 20 I&N Dec. 1306, 1307 (BIA 2000); Matter of Coelho, supra. Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed and the motion to remand is denied.

*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated May 11, 2004. On June 19, 2003, the respondent submitted both an appeal and a motion to reopen. We failed to address his motion. We dismissed his appeal in an order summarily affirming the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen his removal proceedings conducted in absentia. The respondent now seeks reopening for our consideration of the motion he previously submitted, as well as a new claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. He contends that his former counsel failed to present sufficient evidence and argument which resulted in both the Immigration Judge’s denial of his motion to reopen and our dismissal of his appeal. The respondent has presented evidence that he has complied with the requirements set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), but he has not clearly demonstrated ineffective assistance of counsel. However, we find that reopening and remand of the record is warranted in this case so that the Immigration Judge may consider the respondent’s evidence that he gave his correct address to the Department of Homeland Security as reflected on the Notice to Appear and on the Form I-213 dated December 5, 2002, but someone who completed his bond papers listed an incorrect address on Form I-830 and caused the notice of the hearing to be misdirected. We will remand the record for the Immigration Judge to consider the respondent’s claim that, being unaware of the error, he had no reason to think that he needed to file a change of address with the court.
 
Accordingly, the motion is granted, the proceedings are reopened, and the record is remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

IN RE: HUGO WILFREDO PINTO ISMODES MARIA ANTONIRTA PANTIGOSO
Files: A95 229 969 - Miami
A95 229 970

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:

ON BEHALF OF DHS:

Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on July 23, 2003. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before October 21, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until April 26, 2004. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.1 The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has filed an opposition to the motion, based on the untimely filing and on the merits.
 
The respondents assert that they received ineffective assistance by prior counsel. They state that prior counsel did not adequately explain the relief of voluntary departure to them. However, the record reveals that the Immigration Judge provided counsel an opportunity to explain the relief to the respondents, and that the male respondent insisted that he did not want to pursue voluntary departure (Tr. at 19, 39).2 We also note that the respondents were represented by a different attorney at their initial hearing before the Immigration Judge on June 27, 2002, when the issue of voluntary departure and other forms of relief were discussed as well (Tr. at 1-10). The respondents have failed to establish that they received ineffective assistance of counsel in this case.
 
The respondents also allege improprieties by the Immigration Judge in this case. However, we see no reason to overturn our prior order in this case, in which we affirmed without opinion the decision of the Immigration Judge below.
 
Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied. The request for a stay is also denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SAGRARIO PEREZ-CASTELLANOS
File: AXX XX5 835 - Los Angeles
August 27, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Natalia Nekrasova, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The motion to reopen has been filed out of time. The final order in these proceedings, in which we dismissed the respondent’s appeal of an Immigration Judge’s decision denying suspension of deportation, was entered by the Board on September 13, 2002. We granted the respondent’s motion seeking adjustment of status, based on marriage to a United States citizen, on May 7, 2003. An Immigration Judge found the respondent ineligible for adjustment of status on May 30, 2003. On December 16, 2003, we affirmed the decision of the Immigration Judge, and determined that we had earlier erred in granting reopening, given that the respondent was ineligible for adjustment of status under Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996), for failing to depart under an order of voluntary departure. We denied a motion to reconsider this decision, as untimely filed, on February 25, 2004, and this motion was filed on May 28, 2004. The pending motion was filed late. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be granted.
 
In her motion to reopen, the respondent again seeks to have proceedings reopened in order to pursue an application for adjustment of status, based on marriage to a United States citizen. In Matter of Velarde, 23 I&N Dec. 253 (BIA 2002), the Board held that a properly filed motion to reopen for adjustment of status based on a marriage entered into after the commencement of proceedings may be granted in the exercise of discretion, notwithstanding the pendency of a visa petition filed on the alien’s behalf, where: (1) the motion to reopen is timely filed; (2) the motion is not numerically barred by the regulations; (3) the motion is not barred by Matter of Shaar, supra, or on any other procedural grounds; (4) clear and convincing evidence is presented indicating a strong likelihood that the marriage is bona fide; and (5) the DHS does not oppose the motion or bases its opposition solely on Matter of Arthur, 20 I&N Dec. 475 (BIA 1992).
 
As noted, we found in our December 16, 2003, decision that reopening is barred by Matter of Shaar, supra. Pursuant to former section 242B(e)(2) of the Act, an alien who fails to depart following a grant of voluntary departure is statutorily barred from applying for certain forms of discretionary relief, including adjustment of status. However, the respondent argues that she was unaware that she had to depart under the Board’s September 13, 2002, order, because former counsel led her to believe that filing a motion to reopen would prevent the respondent from overstaying the voluntary departure period (Respondent’s Mot., at 2-3; Exh. 3; Respondent’s Affidavit).
 
*2 Similarly, the respondent argues that the motions deadline should be tolled, based on former counsel’s assurance that our December 16, 2003, decision would be reconsidered, and counsel’s failure to inform her until March 2004, that such efforts had been unsuccessful and the respondent would have to depart the United States.
 
The respondent has complied with the procedural requirements of Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003) and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Under the circumstances, we agree that the motions deadline should be excused. See Singh v. Ashcroft, 367 F.3d 1182 (9th Cir. 2004)(motions deadline subject to equitable tolling where lawyer’s actions caused late filing, and respondent thereafter acted with due diligence). Accordingly, the record is remanded in order to afford the respondent an opportunity to present her application for adjustment of status to the Immigration Judge.

IN RE: ADUL RERMOGOSAKUL
File: A20 600 480 - Baltimore

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Jennifer S. Goldmeier,

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated December 4, 2003.1 The record reflects that the respondent, through counsel, conceded proper service of the Notice to Appear (NTA), admitted the allegations in the NTA, conceded removability, waived all forms of relief from removal (Tr. at 21-23) and when asked by the Immigration Judge whether he understood and agreed with the plea, the respondent answered in the affirmative (Tr. at 25). The respondent, through counsel, also waived appeal (Tr. at 28).
 
On appeal, the respondent maintains that he was denied a fair hearing due to ineffective assistance of counsel. However, we note that he has not complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent did not present an affidavit detailing how he was aggrieved by his former counsel. The respondent did not demonstrate that is former counsel was informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond. The respondent did not establish that he filed complaints with appropriate disciplinary authorities regarding his former counsel’s ethical or legal violations. Nor did the respondent explain why he has not filed such complaints. Id.; Matter of Rivera, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Moreover, the respondent has failed to establish how he has been prejudiced. Figeroa v. INS, 886 F.2d 76 (4th Cir. 1989) (“To prevail on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel at a deportation proceeding, an alien must show not only ineffective representation, but also prejudice to him which occurred as a result of that ineffectiveness”). In this regard, we note that the respondent is removable as one who has been convicted of an aggravated felony (Government Exh. C) and he has failed to specify any relief for which he would be eligible.
 
Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.

*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated January 26, 2004. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before April 26, 2004. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until April 28, 2004. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time. We note that even if the motion had been timely filed, reopening would be denied because the new evidence offered by the respondent would not change the result in this case. See Matter of Coehlo, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). This is because the respondent was denied voluntary departure for lack of good moral character based on the length of his incarceration, not the fact of his conviction. See sections 240B(b)(1)(B) and 101(f)(7) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. §§ 1229c(b)(1)(B) and 1101(f)(7);. Insofar as the respondent may seek reopening on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, we note that he has failed to comply in any measure with the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518 (9th Cir. 2000) (compliance with Lozada generally required). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JAVIER MENDEZ-PACHECO
File: AXX XX6 057 - Los Angeles
August 26, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Luisito L. Lopez, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Julia C. Chimarusti,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated November 18, 2003, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen in absentia proceedings. The appeal is dismissed.
 
The respondent invokes the Ninth Circuit’s equitable tolling policy for aliens who have filed otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). The Ninth Circuit has stated that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra at 897. In the present case, the respondent alleges that he was provided with ineffective assistance because he was told, by a person holding himself out as an attorney, that his immigration hearing would be taken care of and then that person failed to inform the respondent of the Immigration Judge’s January 23, 2002, in absentia decision. The respondent, in his motion to reopen before the Immigration Judge, indicates he first learned of his in absentia order when he received the “bag and baggage” letter on July 14, 2002. The record belies this contention, however.
 
The record reflects that the respondent was mailed a copy of the January 23, 2002, in absentia order. The order was mailed to the address given by the respondent and to which prior notices were mailed and admittedly received by the respondent. The record contains no indication that the in absentia order was returned by the United States Postal Service. Therefore, we find that the respondent should have filed his motion to reopen not later than 180 days from the date the in absentia order was mailed to the respondent, which would be July 23, 2002. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b)(4)(ii). Moreover, even accepting the respondent’s rendition of facts that he only learned of his in absentia order on July 14, 2002, he would have needed to file his motion to reopen 180 days from that date, or January 13, 2003. Consequently, his May 13, 2003, motion was untimely. Although the respondent maintains that he consulted his current counsel on July 18, 2002, there has been no explanation for why a motion was not filed until May 13, 2003. Therefore, even applying the doctrine of equitable tolling, we find the respondent’s motion untimely because he failed to file his motion not later than 180 days from the time he became aware of his in absentia removal order. See Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, supra (Ninety-day period for filing motion to reopen deportation proceedings would be equitably tolled during time alien was unaware his final order of deportation had become effective).
 
*2 Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2374865 (BIA)
End of Document

2004 WL 2374821 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AGUSTIN ROSAS MARCELO
File: AXX XX4 473 - Los Angeles
August 25, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Sergei Shevchenko, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sumeet Lall
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. A final order of removal was entered against the respondent by an Immigration Judge on March 24, 2003. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal without opinion on January 13, 2004. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on June 23, 2004, claiming that an attorney provided ineffective assistance by not filing a brief with the Board prior to our final order. The pending motion to reopen was filed late. A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed not later than 90 days after the date of that decision. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion, which will be denied.
 
There is no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here. An individual seeking to rely upon “equitable tolling” of a filing deadline must show that he has acted with due diligence. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 899 (9 th Cir.2003); Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1225 (9th Cir. 2002) (acknowledging that party claiming that equitable tolling applies to reopening deadline must show that he acted with reasonable diligence); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board will exercise its sua sponte authority over an untimely motion only in an “exceptional situation”). See also Matter of Jean, 23 I&N Dec. 373 (A.G. 2002). Such due diligence is not shown here. Although the respondent claims that he had an agreement with an attorney to file the brief, the respondent appeared pro se before the Board, and our decision was mailed directly to him. The respondent does not say when he consulted with current counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, supra (in that case, court found that to the extent that equitable tolling applies, based on actions of prior counsel, limitations period begins when respondent meets with new counsel and learns of fraud).
 
In any event, the respondent failed to comply with our procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003); Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 638 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); Siong v. INS, 2004 WL 1637039 (9th Cir. July 23, 2004). Most critically, there is no convincing evidence that the respondent gave the attorney the opportunity to respond to the allegations of ineffectiveness. Id. The respondent claims that he “contacted his former attorney to request a statement regarding his failure to submit brief” (Respondent’s Response, at p. 3). This claim is insufficient to indicate that the attorney was informed of the claims made against him. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004)(notification to counsel is “required” for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and complaint letter to California State Bar with notation “cc” to opposing counsel does not satisfy the requirement). Consequently, there is no statement or affidavit from counsel, giving his version of events. Id. (citing Matter of Lozada, supra, court said that notice to challenged counsel provides a mechanism by which counsel may respond, giving his version of events, and discouraging baseless allegations).
 
*2 The motion to reopen is, therefore, denied.
