2004 WL 2374817 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BALWINDER KAUR
File: A72 401 161 - Houston
August 25, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Frank Guerra, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reconsideration
 
This case was last before us on February 13, 2004, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen for further consideration of her application for suspension of deportation. On March 11, 2004, the respondent timely filed a motion to reconsider. The motion will be granted and the case will be remanded.
 
A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1) (2004); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991) (holding “¿tºhat when we reconsider a decision, we are in effect placing ourselves back in time and considering the case as though a decision in the case on the record before us had never been entered …The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard”). The respondent’s motion states two reasons for reconsideration. The first is that our prior decision is legally defective insofar as it finds that the motion did not demonstrate that the respondent and her children would suffer extreme hardship and that we erred in not considering the likelihood of persecution in India for purposes of hardship (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 3). The second is that due to ineffective assistance of counsel the respondent was prevented from presenting relevant evidence of hardship in her motion to reopen regarding the medical history of her and her eldest son (Respondent’s Motion to Reconsider at 3-5). We agree with the respondent that our prior decision appears to be in error regarding whether her motion to reopen presented sufficient evidence to demonstrate extreme hardship. Accordingly, her motion to reconsider will be granted.
 
Upon reconsideration, we find that the respondent’s motion to reopen presented enough new evidence to establish a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits. See Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996) (holding that reopening may be had where the new facts alleged, together with the facts already of record, indicate a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits, so as to make it worthwhile to develop the issues at a hearing). When the respondent filed her motion to reopen, 6 years had passed since the Immigration Judge’s decision and the fact-finding proceeding upon which we based our October 15, 2003, vacatur of the Immigration Judge’s decision for failure to establish extreme hardship.1 The respondent’s motion to reopen explained that her eldest son, Akash, was almost 14 years old, had entered middle school, was becoming accustomed to an American way of life, and could not speak, read or write the official Indian language of Hindi (Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 2-3). In addition, the motion stated that since the Immigration Judge’s decision, Akash’s condition of acute asthma had become severe enough to require hospitalization for almost 1 week (Respondent’s Motion Reopen at 2-3 and Exh. A (letter from Akash)). We find that these facts indicate a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits and that we erred in denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for failure to demonstrate extreme hardship.
 
*2 We note that the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposed the respondent’s motion to reopen on the bases that the respondent failed to depart voluntarily and that the respondent did not demonstrate that her son would not be able to receive suitable healthcare in India (DHS Opposition to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 3-5, 8-10). The DHS also argued that the respondent’s motion should be denied on discretionary grounds (DHS Opposition to Respondent’s Motion to Reopen at 5-8). With regards to the voluntary departure issue, we find that the record does not include the oral and written warnings required regarding the consequences of a failure to voluntarily depart. See section 240B(d) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d) (1994). Thus, the respondent is not barred from having proceedings reopened. Cf. Matter of Shaar, 21 I&N Dec. 541 (BIA 1996). With regards to the suitable healthcare argument, this is one of the many issues that would more appropriately be developed at a hearing. Finally, with regards to discretion, we find no reason to deny the respondent’s motion on this basis. The DHS’s discretion argument is based on actions and conduct taken by the respondent’s husband; not the respondent. The DHS has not presented any adverse factors against the respondent which would warrant denying her motion in the exercise of discretion.
 
Accordingly, we find that our February 13, 2004, decision, denying the respondent’s motion to reopen for failure to demonstrate extreme hardship was in error and that decision will be vacated. Upon reconsideration, we find that the facts alleged by the respondent in her motion to reopen indicate a reasonable likelihood of success on the merits, so as to make it worthwhile to develop the issues at a hearing. Matter of L-O-G-, supra. On remand, both parties should be given an opportunity to present evidence regarding whether the respondent and her children would suffer extreme hardship if removed to India and any other forms of relief for which the respondent may now be eligible.
 
Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reconsider is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Our decision dated February 13, 2004, is vacated.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to reopen is granted and the record will be remanded to the Immigration Judge for further proceedings consistent with the foregoing opinion.

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MARCEL AUGUSTO RIOS CLAUDIA PATRICIA GARCIA JULIAN DAVID RIOS
Files: AXX XX7 626 - Orlando
AXX XX7 627
AXX XX7 628
August 24, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Vitalia D. Shafer, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
This matter was last before the Board on October 31, 2003, at which time we dismissed the respondents’ appeal of the Immigration Judge’s July 29, 2001, decision for failing to timely file a brief or statement in support of the appeal. The respondents have filed a timely motion to reopen on January 29, 2004, alleging ineffective assistance by their former counsel and requesting that this matter be reopened. The Department of Homeland Security (the DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the motion. The Board will deny the motion.
 
The Board has found that certain criteria must be met in order to grant a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, the motion must be supported by an affidavit of the respondents setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make, counsel must be informed of the allegations against him and provided an opportunity to respond, and the motion must reflect whether a complaint has been filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637, 639 (BIA 1988). The documentary evidence presented by the respondents meets the requirements set forth in Lozada.
 
While the respondents have complied with the established requirements to assert a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, the United States Court of Appeals for the Eleventh Circuit, the jurisdiction in which this matter arose, has held that, in addition to meeting the procedural requirements, an alien must also show prejudice. Although there is a rebuttable presumption of prejudice where alien’s counsel fails to file an appeal brief, any such presumption of prejudice is rebutted where the alien fails to show that an appeal brief could have changed the outcome of the appeal proceedings before the Board. See Dakane v. United States Attorney General, 371 F.3d 771 (11th Cir. 2004).
 
The Board finds that the respondents’ allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel are unpersuasive. While the respondents argue that there was ineffective assistance of counsel because their previous counsel failed to submit properly translated evidence of the risk to the medical profession in Columbia, the respondents do not suggest that the document could have affected the Immigration Judge’s decision, particularly in light of the Immigration Judge’s comprehensive analysis of the objective reports of country conditions. Moreover, the respondents have not indicated what arguments they would have presented on appeal that could have changed the outcome of the appeal proceedings before the Board. Accordingly, as the respondents were not shown to have experienced actual prejudice, either during the course of the proceeding before the Immigration Judge or by the previous counsel’s failure to file an appeal brief, there can be no ineffective assistance of counsel.
 
*2 The respondents’ motion to reopen will be denied.
 
ORDER: The motion for reopen is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **

Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LUIS GALVEZ PINEDA JR. MARIA EVELYN ROQUE PINEDA JOHANNA R. PINEDA ROBINSON R. PINEDA DARWIN R. PINEDA AMIEL PINEDA
Files: AXX XX8 577 - Denver
AXX XX8 578
AXX XX8 579
AXX XX8 580
AXX XX8 581
AXX XX8 582
August 24, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Dani Lisa Page
Deputy Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents filed a motion to reopen on June 22, 2004. We dismissed the respondents’ appeal on December 9, 2002. The motion has been filed out of time and will be denied. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion.
 
The respondents claim in their motion that prior counsel, who has been suspended from the Colorado bar, was incompetent, because he failed to file a brief with this Board, which caused us to dismiss the appeal for failure to file a brief. There is, however, no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here.
 
Equitable tolling of the motions deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf. See Riley v. INS, 310 F.3d 1253, 1257-58 (10th Cir. 2002). The pending motion was filed over a year late. The respondents claim that they were unaware of former counsel’s ineffective assistance until they consulted with the attorney who signed the pending motion, but failed to enter his appearance with the Board. Yet the attorney who signed the pending motion began representing the respondents on October 15, 2003, according to a letter he wrote to the Colorado Supreme Court Attorney Regulation Counsel. Even so, this motion was not submitted for 8 more months. As Riley v. INS, supra, at 1258, requires, we have considered that the respondents have complied with the procedural requirements for making a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). However, the respondents have not shown due diligence in presenting their claims in the pending motion, where the motion was not filed for many months after new counsel was consulted. Riley v. INS, supra, at 1258. The respondents state that they did not become aware of former counsel’s ineffective assistance of counsel until current counsel “received a copy of the administrative record” (Respondents’ Mot., at p. 12). However, the respondents do not state when this occurred. Exhibits submitted with the motion indicate that counsel who signed the pending motion submitted a brief to the Tenth Circuit Court of Appeals on February 6, 2004, after receiving the administrative record. Id.; Respondent’s Mot. at Exh. 4. The pending motion was not filed for another four months. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

FOR THE BOARD

End of Document

© 2020 Thomson Reuters. No claim to original U.S. Government Works.




2004 WL 2374950 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: RAMI SAMI AWAD AL AID
File: AXX XX4 007 - Los Angeles
August 24, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nassim Arzani, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Tracy Nguyen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(7)(A)(i)(I), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(7)(A)(i)(I) º - Immigrant - no valid immigrant visa or entry document
 
This matter was last before the Board on November 20, 2003, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our August 20, 2002, decision denying his motion to reopen. The respondent has now filed a second motion to reopen pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The motion seeks reopening based on ineffective assistance of counsel. The respondent, a native and citizen of Jordan who is married to a United States citizen, wishes to apply for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255. In the alternative, the respondent requests reinstatement of the voluntary departure period granted by the Immigration Judge at the respondent’s hearing on July 11, 2001. The Department of Homeland Security (the DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion. The motion will be denied.
 
As an initial matter, an alien may file only one motion to reopen removal proceedings, and the motion must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The instant motion, filed on July 9, 2004, represents the respondent’s second attempt to reopen his proceedings. It was not filed within 90 days of the final administrative order. The respondent requests that the filing deadline be equitably tolled because he received ineffective assistance of counsel regarding his appeal before the Board. The respondent states in an affidavit that he never received the Board’s decision regarding his appeal, and therefore did not know that he needed to leave the United States within 30 days of the date of the order, which was issued May 6, 2002, in order to comply with the terms of his voluntary departure. See generally section 240B of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c. Rather, the respondent claims that he remained in the United States on the advice of his attorney, applied for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, and was detained by the DHS at his adjustment interview on June 21, 2004.
 
The doctrine of equitable tolling may, in certain circumstances, override the rules regarding motions to reopen in cases where the respondent has been defrauded by an individual purporting to provide legal representation. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1224 (9th Cir. 2002). In order to receive the benefit of equitable tolling, a respondent who alleges ineffective assistance must first comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). The motion to reopen should be supported by an affidavit attesting to the relevant facts, including a statement setting forth the details of the agreement that was entered into with former counsel. See id. at 639. The respondent should also notify former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance so that he or she may respond, and, if the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities on the part of former counsel, the respondent should file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. Id. In this case, the motion is supported by an affidavit from the respondent, a short declaration from the respondent’s former counsel stating that he never received a decision from the Board regarding the respondent’s case, and a complaint form for the State Bar of California that had not yet been submitted when the motion was filed (Respondent’s Exhs. B, C, H). We find that the respondent has substantially complied with Matter of Lozada. See Rodriguez-Lariz, supra, at 1226-27 (stating that the factors are not rigidly applied).
 
*2 We do not, however, find that the evidence of record supports the respondent’s allegation that neither he nor his former attorney received notice of the Board’s decision regarding his appeal. Our order summarily affirming the decision of the Immigration Judge was issued May 6, 2002. The respondent’s former counsel submitted the fast motion to reopen shortly thereafter, on June 3, 2002. The Board denied the motion to reopen on August 20, 2002. The respondent, through former counsel, then filed a motion to reconsider on November 20, 2003. The Board considered the merits of the motion despite its untimeliness. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). Finding no factual or legal error in the prior order, the Board denied the motion to reconsider on March 24, 2004. The instant motion asserts that the respondent knew nothing of the Board’s decisions regarding his case. Given the lengthy procedural history above, this assertion simply is not credible. It stands to reason that the attorney received notice of the Board’s decision regarding the respondent’s appeal because he otherwise would not have known to file the motion to reopen. It is also reasonable to assume the attorney consulted with his client regarding the decision, as the motion would have required more work by the attorney for which he presumably would have been compensated. Moreover, the respondent has not sufficiently rebutted the presumption of effective mail service. See Matter of Grijalva, 21 I&N Dec. 27 (BIA 1995). Under section 239(c) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229a(c), service by mail is sufficient if there is proof of attempted delivery to the last address provided by the alien. We therefore cannot give credence to the respondent’s assertion that he did not know he needed to depart the United States, and we conclude that his case does not merit equitable tolling.
 
We further note that the respondent’s request for remand to the Immigration Judge for consideration of his application for adjustment of status under section 245(i) of the Act is not appropriate because the respondent is an arriving alien; therefore, jurisdiction over the application lies not with the Immigration Judge, but with the DHS. 8 C.F.R. § 1245.2(a)(1). We are also unable to grant the respondent’s request to reinstate the voluntary departure period. The Board does not have authority to reinstate voluntary departure in this instance. See 8 C.F.R. § 1240.29(h) (requiring removal proceedings to be reopened for purpose other than solely making application for voluntary departure). Accordingly, we will enter the following order.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s motion is denied.

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MAURICE UDDOH
File: AXX XX6 810 - Baltimore
August 24, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
James J. Orlow, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The instant motion is untimely and number-barred and will be denied. The final order in these proceedings was entered by the Board on November 26, 2002. Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), only one motion to reopen may be filed and in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before February 24, 2003. The record reflects, however, that the Board did not receive the motion until April 14, 2004, more than a year late. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time.
 
Counsel for the respondent urges that we reopen proceedings in the exercise of our sua sponte reopening authority pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a). We will not do so. New counsel for the respondent alleges ineffective assistance by prior counsel in failing to point out that the charging document (Order to Show Cause, Form I-221) contained the incorrect ground of deportability. The Form I-221 states that on November 17, 1986, the respondent entered the United States without inspection under section 241(a)(1)(B) of the INA (1995). However, new counsel has pointed out that the respondent on November 17, 1986, entered the United States with an F-1 nonimmigrant student visa, as indicated by the stamp in his passport and the confirmation by official records. Thus, it appears that the proper ground of deportability would have been as a visa overstay, under section 241(a)(1)(C) of the INA (1995). However, counsel has failed to establish that the respondent was not deportable when the Immigration Judge issued the in absentia order of deportation in this case on September 5, 1996.
 
The respondent has failed to meet the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 U&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988) in order to establish a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Furthermore, he has failed to show prejudice in his case. Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553, 556, 561-62 (BIA 2003). The respondent has failed to show that he was not deportable. The respondent has failed to show that reopening would be warranted in this case. See Matter of Beckford, 22 I&N Dec. 1216 (BIA 2000) (alien failed to show substantial likelihood that the result in the case would be changed if proceedings were reopened); see generally Matter of L-O-G-, 21 I&N Dec. 413 (BIA 1996). The respondent hired several attorneys over the 17 years he has been in the United States and the 8 years he has been in immigration proceedings, yet he has failed to adjust his status or depart the United States. The respondent has failed to exercise due diligence in pursuing his claims to relief.
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374790 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: AZMOND ALI
File: AXX XX6 937 - New York
August 24, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kai W. De Graaf, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
James D. Paoli
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent’s motion to reopen has been filed out of time and will be denied. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on September 1, 1992. More than 11 years later, on July 30, 2004, the respondent has filed a motion to reopen. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
A motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Contrary to the respondent’s argument, Respondent’s Mot., at pp. 18-19, the time limitation does apply to his case. The time limitations on motions was added to the regulations on July 1, 1996. See 61 FR 18900-01 (July 1, 1996). For cases, like the respondent’s, that already had a final administrative decision on that date, the new regulations allowed until September 30, 1996, for any motion to reopen to be filed. The pending motion was therefore filed over 7 years late. We note that in arguing that his motion should be considered, the respondent cites to regulations applicable to in absentia decisions (Respondent’s Mot., at pp. 20-21). As the respondent’s case was not held in absentia, these references are inapplicable to the respondent’s case.
 
Underlying the motion is a claim of ineffective assistance of the respondent’s former attorney, who has been suspended from the practice of law in New York. The respondent claims that former counsel provided ineffective assistance before the Immigration Judge. The respondent also claims that he was unaware that former counsel had filed an appeal with the Board, or that this appeal was resolved in 1992. However, we cannot excuse the lateness of the motion in this case. In Iavorski v. INS, 124, 127, 134-35 (2d Cir. 2000), the court suggested that the filing deadline for motions with the Board may be tolled based on deceptive actions of counsel. However, such tolling is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence on his own behalf. See Tho Dinh Tran v. Alphonse Hotel Corp., 281 F.3d 23, 36-37 (2d Cir. 2002); Matter of J-J-, 21 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA 1997)(Board will exercise its sua sponte authority over an untimely motion only in an “exceptional situation”). Here, the respondent fails to establish he exercised diligence in pursuing his rights. The respondent states that former counsel told him he would “take care of it” after the Immigration Judge issued an order of deportation in 1987. However, the respondent’s affidavit is vague as to what steps he took in the 17-year period preceding the filing of this motion, to find out about the status of the deportation order against him. The respondent suggests that he contacted former counsel, but was either told that the case was ongoing, or was unable to speak to former counsel (Respondent’s Affidavit, at ¶¶ 10, 12). The respondent’s affidavit suggests that he made no effort after 1996 to learn of the status of his case. Id. at 12. This does not evidence that the respondent exercised diligence in learning about the status of the deportation order pending against him. As the DHS aptly argues, “… this respondent completely ignored the ramification of his prior hearing for 17 years.… The respondent repeatedly claims that he was unable to get in touch with his prior attorney, but never explains why he waited 17 years to procure new counsel to follow up on his case.” See Iavorski v. INS, supra, at 134 (when respondent unable to reach his attorney to inquire about his case, he should have known that ineffective assistance of counsel had occurred and taken action earlier). Given the respondent’s lack of diligence, there is no basis for tolling of the motions deadline, and the motion is therefore denied.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374924 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: BETANCOURT, TIBIRDO T.
File: AXX-XX1-381 - BOSTON
August 23, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Curley, Kathleen M.
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 27, 2003. The respondent submitted a timely motion to reopen based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and a claim for relief from removal based on a visa petition filed by his mother and a pending visa petition filed by his United States citizen wife. The Immigration Judge did not render a decision on the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). He found that the visa petition filed by the respondent’s mother was no longer pending, and that reopening was not warranted on that basis. He determined that the respondent failed to demonstrate the bona fides of his marriage to establish that he is eligible for relief based on the pending visa petition filed by his wife. We note that the respondent’s marriage to his United States citizen wife took place 4 years before the initiation of removal proceedings. There is no requirement to demonstrate the bona fides of that marriage to support the visa petition. We will remand the record to the Immigration Judge for further consideration of the respondent’s claim of ineffective assistance of counsel, and his claim of eligibility for relief based on the pending visa petition filed by his wife. Accordingly, the record is remanded to the Immigration Court for further proceedings.

2004 WL 2374908 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: SERAFIN CAMPOS-SORIA ROSA CUAMBA RODRIGUEZ
Files: AXX XX5 306 - San Francisco
AXX XX5 320
August 23, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Ilyce Shugall, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Larry J. Gallagher
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - Entered without inspection (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Suspension of deportation; fair hearing
 
The respondents now appeal from an Immigration Judge’s June 6, 2003, decision denying their requests for suspension of deportation under section 244(a) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, & U.S.C. § 1254(a), and from the Immigration Judge’s July 2, 2003, decision denying their motion to reconsider that decision. For the reasons set forth in the Immigration Judge’s decisions, we will adopt and affirm the Immigration Judge’s denial of the respondents’ requests for suspension of deportation. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”).
 
On appeal, the respondents reiterate the arguments put forth during the deportation bearings and in the motion to reconsider. It is undisputed, however, that the respondent Serafin Campos-Soria (AXX XXX XXX) entered the United States on September 1, 1992, and was served with an Order to Show Cause on June 14, 1994. It is further undisputed that the respondent Rosa Cuamba Rodriguez (AXX XXX XXX) entered the United States on October 1, 1992, and was served with an Order to Show Cause May 14, 1994. We find, therefore, that the Immigration Judge correctly concluded that the respondents are ineligible for relief under section 244(a) of the Act because they failed to establish that they had been physically present in the United States for a continuous period of not less than 7 years immediately preceding the issuance of the Orders to Show Cause. See section 244(a)(1) of the Act. See also Matter of Nolasco, 22 I&N Dec. 632, 641 (BIA 1999) (discussing the application of the “stop-time” rule under section 309(c)(5) of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, the “IIRIRA”). We emphasize that the Immigration Judge appropriately applied the law as it existed on the hearing date, and rendered the correct decision. Compare, Guadalupe-Cruz v. INS, 240 F.3d 1209, opinion corrected by 250 F.3d 1271 (9th Cir. 2001). In addition, if the respondents had succeeded in advancing this hearing to a date prior to April 1, 1997, they would not have accrued the necessary continuous physical presence by that date.
 
*2 While their appeal remained pending before the Board, the respondent filed a motion to remand based on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. They specifically allege that their former attorney failed to act with due diligence to proceed with the hearings prior to the April 1, 1997, effective date for section 309 of the IIRIRA, thus precluding their eligibility for suspension of deportation. Although we find that the respondents have complied with the procedural requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel as set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA1988), we cannot find that they suffered prejudice as a result of the attorney’s conduct. Even if the attorney had filed the applications for suspension of deportation on behalf of the respondents prior April 1, 1997, the respondents would not have accrued the requisite 7 years of continuous physical presence required under section 244(a)(1) of the Act at that time. See, generally, Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); cf. Guadalupe-Cruz v. INS, 240 F.3d 1209 (9th Cir. 2001). We will deny the motion.
 
In sum, we agree with the Immigration Judge’s findings and conclusion, and we find no reason to disturb the Immigration Judge’s decision. Inasmuch as the dispositive facts of this case are undisputed, and the respondents suffered no prejudice as a result of their former attorney’s handling of the case, we reject the respondent’s contentions with regard to the fairness of the proceedings. Accordingly, the following orders will be entered.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and in accordance with our decision in Matter of Chouliaris, 16 I&N Dec. 168 (BIA 1977), the respondents are permitted to depart from the United States voluntarily within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director; and in the event of failure so to depart, the respondents shall be deported as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.

FOR THE BOARD

2004 WL 2374935 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ZAHIDUL HOQUE CHOWDHURY LOVELY ZAHID CHOWDHURY RUSLAT ZAHID CHOWDHURY FARRUBA ZAHID CHOWDHURY
Files: A73 566 598 - New York
A73 566 599
A73 566 601
A73 566 602
August 20, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Richard VanVeldhuisen
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondents1 filed a motion to reopen on April 23, 2004. We dismissed the respondents’ appeal on March 21, 2003. The motion has been filed out of time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the pending motion, which will be denied.
 
The respondents claim in their motion that prior counsel was incompetent, because prior counsel failed to inform them of the Board’s prior decision so they could file a timely motion to reopen. There is, however, no basis for abrogating the motions deadline here. Any equitable tolling of the motions deadline is unavailable when a party fails to exercise due diligence. See Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124, 127, 134-35. (2nd Cir. 2000). The pending motion was filed almost a year late. The respondents do not say when they learned of the Board’s prior order, and therefore fail to establish that the motion could not have been filed earlier.
 
In any event, for claims of ineffective assistance of counsel, Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Finally, an alien must show that he was prejudiced by the ineffective assistance of counsel. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003); Esposito v. INS, 987 F.2d 108, 110 (2d Cir. 1993). The respondents have not satisfied these requirements. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

FOR THE BOARD

To the extent that Nubyra Zahid Chowdhury, A 73 566 600, wishes to be included in this order, this motion as to her would be numerically barred as well as untimely. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2).

2004 WL 2374936 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VITALIY A. EFIMENKO
File: AXX XX3 367 - Newark
August 20, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Alexander J. Segal, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent appeals from a May 14, 2004, decision by the Immigration Judge to deny his motion to reopen his deportation proceedings. The motion was denied as untimely. The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel, who failed to file an appeal with this Board after the respondent was ordered deported, and that the filing deadline should be equitably tolled. The appeal will be dismissed.
 
The Immigration Judge correctly determined that the motion was untimely. A motion to reopen before an Immigration Judge must be filed within 90 days of the final administrative order of removal, deportation, or exclusion, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.23(b). The respondent was ordered deported on November 29, 1996, but did not file a motion to reopen until April 23, 2004, after being detained by the Department of Homeland Security (the DHS, formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service). Moreover, the respondent’s motion did not conform with the requirements under Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), for a motion to reopen based on ineffective assistance of counsel. Such motions must be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent attesting to the relevant facts, including a statement setting forth the details of the agreement that was entered into with former counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. The respondent should also notify former counsel of the allegations of ineffective assistance so that he or she may respond, and, if the case involved a violation of ethical or legal responsibilities on the part of former counsel, the respondent should file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authorities, Id. In this case, the motion to reopen submitted to the Immigration Judge included only an affidavit by the respondent and correspondence from the respondent’s former attorney regarding his appeal, which is insufficient. We note that on appeal the respondent has attached new evidence that he filed a complaint with the Departmental Disciplinary Committee in New York and sent correspondence of some kind to his former attorney; however, it appears that the mailings occurred after the Immigration Judge ruled on the respondent’s motion (Respondent’s Exhs. A-C). We note that the Board does not consider new evidence on appeal. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.1(d)(3).
 
*2 Finally, we conclude that the respondent is not eligible for equitable tolling. The United States Court of Appeals for the Third Circuit, the circuit in which in this matter arises, has not addressed the issue of whether the filing deadline for a motion to reopen may be equitably tolled. The respondent correctly notes that other circuit courts of appeals have applied the doctrine of equitable tolling to the regulatory time limitation for motions to reopen and have treated actions by the agent of a party as the proper subject of equitable tolling in favor of that same party. See, e.g., Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001); Iavorski v. INS, 232 F.3d 124 (2d Cir. 2000). In order to receive the benefit of equitable tolling, however, an alien must demonstrate not only that his constitutional right to due process was violated by the conduct of counsel, but also that he has exercised due diligence during the period he wishes to toll. See Iavorski v. INS, supra, at 135. While we understand that the respondent’s former counsel allegedly failed to file a Notice of Appeal with the Board after the respondent was ordered deported in 1996, the respondent has not adequately explained why he waited over 7 years to file his motion to reopen. Because the respondent has failed to demonstrate due diligence in pursuing his claim, we do not find that his case warrants equitable tolling. For the foregoing reasons, the appeal is dismissed.

2004 WL 2374901 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JOSE DIMAS ARCE MARCELA OSORIO ARCE
File: AXX XX9 966 - Los Angeles
AXX XX9 967
August 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Juan A. Laguna, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled (both respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondents move the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated November 24, 2003. The respondents request that we apply the principle of equitable tolling and consider their motion despite the fact that it was filed more than 90 days after our final removal order. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2); Lopez v. INS, 184 F.3d 1097 (9th Cir. 1999) (concluding that the statute of limitations for reopening order of deportation should be tolled when alien has been the victim of fraud). Even assuming that the motion was timely under the doctrine of equitable tolling, the respondents have failed to establish a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel. A claim of ineffective assistance of counsel must satisfy the requirements set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), wherein we held that such motions require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The respondent has not contacted former counsel, informed him of the allegations leveled against him, and given him an opportunity to respond. In Matter of Lozada, at 639, we stated that “before allegations of ineffectiveness are presented to the Board, former counsel must be informed of the allegations and allowed the opportunity to respond. Any subsequent response from counsel, or report of counsel’s failure or refusal to respond should be submitted with the motion” (emphasis added). We noted that the high standard which we enunciated was necessary if the Board is to have a basis for assessing the substantial number of claims of ineffective assistance of counsel that come before us. Id. It is crucial that former counsel be given “the opportunity to respond to the allegation¿sº of ineffectiveness,” due to the charges made by the respondent. See Reyes v. Ashcroft, 358 F.3d 592, 598-99 (9th Cir. 2004)(notification to counsel is “required” for an ineffective assistance of counsel claim, and complaint letter to California State Bar with notation “cc” to opposing counsel does not satisfy the requirement). For example, in the male respondent’s affidavit he writes that he indicated to the immigration consultant that his son Omar was under medical supervision because of renal problems, but his son’s condition was never addressed during the hearing. The respondent maintains that he emphasized this fact on several occasions. However, when the male respondent testified during the removal hearing he indicated that Omar was in good health (Tr. at 15). While the respondents have raised this issue in their ineffective assistance of counsel claim, we have no statement or affidavit from counsel, giving his version of events and thus no way to assess the validity of the claim. See also Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 3369 (3rd Cir. 2003) (A requirement of Lozada includes filing a motion and giving the allegedly ineffective counsel an opportunity to respond). The Ninth Circuit Court of Appeals has indicated that rigid adherence to the Lozada requirements is not always required, see Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218, 1227 (9th Cir.2002) (Lozada “factors are not rigidly applied, especially when the record shows a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance”). However, in this case the need for notification and response from former counsel is critical to our meaningful assessment of this claim. Reyes v. Ashcroft, supra. Accordingly, the motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 2374902 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISHTIAQ HUSSAIN
File: AXX XX0 244 - San Francisco
August 19, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Martin Resendez Guajardo, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Katherine Zill
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. On June 9, 2004, the respondent filed a timely motion to reconsider the Board’s May 10, 2004, decision finding that he had waived his right to appeal. The respondent contends that this Board erroneously found that he had made no argument on appeal that his waiver of appeal was not knowing and intelligent as he raised this specific argument in his Notice of Appeal. The respondent also contends that he was deprived of the right to file a brief on appeal as he was not provided with a transcript of the proceedings. Furthermore, the respondent avers that the Immigration Judge erred in not informing him that he might be eligible for relief in the form of asylum (Motion at 4-5).
 
When deciding a motion to reconsider, the Board considers the case as though the decision in the case on the record before us had never been entered. If the applicant was eligible for relief at the time of the original decision, then in reconsidering the decision, we treat his status as that which it had been at the time of the initial decision. “The very nature of a motion to reconsider is that the original decision was defective in some regard.” Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399, 402 (BIA 1991).
 
The respondent correctly notes that the Board erred in stating that he had not contended that his waiver was not knowing and intelligent. However, the Board correctly noted that the respondent was represented by counsel when he elected to waive appeal, contrary to his contentions otherwise (Tr. at 5). We find no merit to the respondent’s argument that he had not retained the attorney and that the record does not indicate whether she had been “fully familiar” with his case (Reply to DHS Opposition). When he waived appeal, the respondent appeared with the same counsel who had represented him at the prior hearing. The fact that the respondent contracted for services with another attorney who then utilized the representative who ultimately appeared on his behalf is not relevant to our inquiry. It is the respondent’s prerogative whom he chooses to serve as his representative, if any. The representative at issue submitted a Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney on behalf of the respondent, which the respondent never contested, and appeared twice on his behalf (Tr. at 1, 5). At no time during the proceedings below did the respondent indicate that this woman was not his representative. As such, the record corroborates the Board’s finding that the respondent appeared with counsel, through whom he waived appeal (Tr. at 5-6). Given these facts, the Board did not err in finding that the respondent had waived appeal. See Matter of Rodriguez-Diaz, 22 I&N Dec. 1320 (BIA 2000). Moreover, while the respondent suggests that his counsel was not adequately prepared, he has not complied with the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637(BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
*2 We find no merit to the respondent’s argument that he was “denied” the right to file a brief because he was not provided with a transcript of the proceedings (Motion at 3). The Board does not normally prepare a transcript when a party has waived appeal as the Immigration Judge’s decision is final. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.3(a)(1); Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). While the respondent also asserts error in that he was not afforded an interpreter, we note that he informed the Immigration Judge that he speaks English (Tr. at 2). Moreover, the respondent’s counsel indicated that she had conferred with him before waiving appeal on his behalf (Tr. at 5). Again, if the respondent seeks to assert that his counsel took action contrary to their agreement or that she, in fact, had not adequately conferred with him, he has not met the requirements for a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Matter of Lozada, supra. Finally, we find no merit to the respondent’s allegation that the Immigration Judge erred in not noting that as a particular political party was no longer in power that “there was an inference that ¿the respondentº may fear returning” to his native Pakistan (Motion at 5). This argument is highly attenuated. An Immigration Judge is under no obligation to present a respondent’s claim on his behalf, particularly where, as here, a respondent is represented. The respondent through counsel informed the Immigration Judge that he was not seeking any relief, and he wished to have an order entered for his return to Pakistan (Tr. at 5). As such, there was no “inference” that could be drawn that the respondent feared harm if removed from the United States. In sum, we find no basis upon which reopening is warranted.
 
Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2374869 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JACQUES GARABET MAKSOUDIAN
File: A94 211 943 - Los Angeles
August 18, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Gary Silbiger, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(l)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The respondent is an unmarried male native and citizen of Lebanon. In a decision dated March 30, 1999, an Immigration Judge denied the respondent’s application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), and granted him the privilege of voluntary departure. The respondent filed a timely appeal. The Board summarily affirmed the Immigration Judge’s decision on September 24, 2002, and granted the respondent 30 days in which to voluntarily depart. The respondent filed a petition for review of that decision with the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. On March 17, 2004, the Ninth Circuit dismissed his petition for review of the Board’s September 24, 2002, dismissal of his appeal of the Immigration Judge’s decision. A petition for rehearing was denied by the Ninth Circuit on July 12, 2004.
 
While the respondent’s petition for review was pending, he filed, on December 23, 2002, a timely motion to reopen based upon counsel’s assertion that the respondent was gathering additional evidence regarding the exceptional and extremely unusual requirement for his previously denied cancellation of removal application. The Board denied the motion on July 30, 2003, for failure to support the motion with an affidavit or other evidentiary material as required by regulation.
 
On August 29, 2003, the respondent filed a timely motion to reopen and reconsider in which he alleged that he had additional new and material evidence regarding his eligibility for cancellation of removal. On October 27, 2003, the Board denied the motion to reconsider for failure to allege any factual or legal error in the Board’s prior decision as required for reconsideration and denied the motion to reopen for again failing to support the motion with evidence as required by regulation and also for exceeding the numerical limitations for motions to reopen as dictated by regulation.
 
On August 9, 2004, the respondent filed his third motion to reopen in which he seeks to have an opportunity to apply for adjustment of status under section 245 of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1255, based upon an approved visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen mother and to again apply for cancellation of removal based upon his claim that he received ineffective assistance of counsel from Barry Fagan, the attorney who represented him at his removal hearing.1 On August 11, 2004, the respondent filed a supplement to his motion to reopen in which he raised arguments regarding his voluntary departure period and the tolling of motions to reopen based upon claims of ineffective assistance of counsel. On August 12, 2004, the respondent filed, via facsimile transmission, a second supplement to his motion to reopen in which he provided his application for adjustment of status — one of the forms of relief sought in his August 9, 2004, motion — as well as an addendum and a supplement to that application. The Department of Homeland Security (DHS) has filed no response to the respondent’s three filings regarding his most recent motion to reopen. The motion to reopen will be denied.
 
*2 We first address that portion of the respondent’s motion that deals with his request for reopening so that he can apply for adjustment of status. In order to have proceedings reopened so that he can apply for adjustment of status, the respondent must show that the motion is timely filed, he has a visa immediately available to him, that he is not statutorily barred for failing to voluntarily depart within 30 days of the Board’s September 24, 2002, decision, and that he is not otherwise barred or ineligible for adjustment of status. The respondent currently has a visa immediately available to him due to his mother’s naturalization on January 30, 2004, and the resulting conversion of his second preference visa to a first preference visa.2 See Dep’t. of State Visa Bulletin, Vol. VIII, No. 72 (Aug. 2004). The respondent contends that his voluntary departure period, as calculated under Ninth Circuit law, expires on August 18, 2004.3 Under Ninth Circuit law, the respondent is thus not statutorily barred by section 240(d) of the Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229c(d), from having proceedings reopened where he did not depart the United States within 30 days of the Board’s September 24, 2002, order.
 
However, the respondent is barred by the time and numerical limitations in the motions regulation from having proceedings reopened because he is seeking reopening in order to apply for adjustment of status in his third motion to reopen which was filed well after the expiration of the 90-day period for the timely filing of motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). The respondent’s motion, as it relates to his request for adjustment of status, does not fall within any of the exceptions to the time and numerical bars for motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(3). The respondent states that the motion should be granted, even though it is untimely and his third motion, because he is now eligible for adjustment of status and is claiming that he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel. As the respondent did not become eligible to apply for adjustment of status until his mother became a naturalized United States citizen on January 30, 2004, he cannot claim that any alleged ineffectiveness of the attorney who represented him only at his removal hearings, which ended on March 30, 1999, affected his ability to timely file a motion to reopen for adjustment of status within 90 days of the Board’s September 24, 2002, decision. Moreover, it is present counsel’s filing of two previous, and clearly defective, motions to reopen that subjects the respondent to the numerical bars regarding the instant motion to reopen. The respondent does not cite to any exception to the time and numerical limitations on motions to reopen which are based upon eligibility for adjustment of status and we find none in the regulations. Thus, the respondent’s motion to reopen for adjustment of status must be denied.
 
*3 We next turn to that portion of the respondent’s motion that seeks reopening so that he can again apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. The respondent contends that he has new evidence showing that he met the continuous physical presence requirement and cites to a variety of documents which he provides as exhibits D-G with his motion. The respondent, however, fails to explain how his 1983-1984 high school records, college records for 1986-1988, bank letter attesting to an account opened in October of 1987 to the October 16, 1997, date of the letter, and social security records showing employment earnings for 1985-1986 and 1989-2002, constitutes new or previously unavailable or undiscoverable evidence that could not have been presented at his March 30, 1999, hearing. Nor has the respondent demonstrated how this evidence could not have been presented with either of his first two motions to reopen. The respondent also asserts that he has new evidence that his mother would face exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if he is deported. He points to the death of his father, his mother’s subsequent diagnosis of depression, and her continued health problems for which she needs assistance. Counsel’s statements that the respondent’s brother and sister cannot provide their mother with the emotional and economic assistance that is provided by the respondent is insufficient to show that proceedings should be reopened on this basis. Furthermore, the respondent does not explain why his mother, a United States citizen, cannot obtain local, state, or federal assistance for the elderly or disabled or why such aid could not be used in conjunction with any aid provided by the respondent’s brother, who lives with the respondent and their mother, and by the respondent’s sister, who lives within 1 hour of their mother.
 
The crux of the respondent’s present motion is his allegation that he received ineffective assistance from his former attorney and thus should have proceedings reopened. The respondent argues that he received ineffective assistance from his former counsel in that former counsel did not meet with him sufficiently to be aware of the relevant facts of the case, did not obtain the documentation needed to meet the eligibility requirements for cancellation of removal, did not interview and present crucial witnesses, and did not request an interpreter for the respondent’s lawful permanent resident father, who attempted to testify on his son’s behalf at the hearing. He asserts that his former counsel’s actions fell well below the standard of competency.
 
The respondent has not fully complied with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), as there is no copy of any agreement regarding his representation by former counsel and it is unclear from the document submitted by the respondent whether he filed a complaint with a state bar, and if so, which state bar. See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The “complaint,” which he submitted as exhibit Q to his motion, consists of 1-page of an apparently multi-page document which is unaccompanied by any attachments showing to which state bar the complaint was made. Nor does the respondent provide any evidence showing that the “complaint” was filed with the appropriate disciplinary authorities. In addition, the letter from present counsel informing former counsel of the complaint lodged against him is dated August 4, 2004, only 4 days before the motion was sent to the Board by overnight courier. See Resp. Mot. at Exh. S. Thus, former counsel was not given a meaningful opportunity to respond to the complaint.4 Without former counsel’s statement as to how he prepared for the respondent’s hearing or how often he met with the respondent, we have an incomplete and one-sided version of the events that transpired. Moreover, the respondent fails to explain why he waited until less than 2 weeks before his voluntary departure date to file the instant motions and supplements thus depriving former counsel and the DHS from having a meaningful opportunity to respond to his numerous arguments.
 
*4 Furthermore, even if we were to find that the respondent has met this first hurdle of establishing substantial compliance with Lozada, we do not find that the respondent’s situation falls within the Ninth Circuit’s equitable tolling policy for aliens who have filed with the Board otherwise untimely motions in which they raise allegations of ineffective assistance of counsel. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889 (9th Cir. 2003); Socop-Gonzalez v. INS, 272 F.3d 1176 (9th Cir. 2001). The Ninth Circuit has stated that equitable tolling will be applied where the alien is prevented from timely filing a motion by deception, fraud, or error so long as the alien acted with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d at 897. See also Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, supra, and cases cited therein. The facts of this case show that all of the alleged ineffectiveness of former counsel could have, and should have, been known prior to the issuance of the Board’s decision on September 24, 2002. Present counsel has been representing the respondent since the filing of the Notice of Appeal on April 27, 1999. Thus, present counsel received a copy of the Immigration Judge’s decision and the transcript of proceedings. The respondent and present counsel were therefore well aware of what evidence was, and was not, submitted to the Immigration Judge as well as what witnesses were, and were not, available to testify on the respondent’s behalf. Even applying equitable tolling to his case, the clock began running at the latest on September 24, 2002, when the Board dismissed his appeal. However, the respondent did not file this motion to reopen until August 9, 2004, almost 2 years after our decision dismissing his appeal and almost 5 months after the Ninth Circuit denied his petition for review of that dismissal. Counsel chose to limit his arguments on appeal to the Board on the Immigration Judge’s alleged failure to order an interpreter and on the Immigration Judge’s alleged error in finding that the respondent’s evidence was insufficient to meet the continuous physical presence and exceptional and extremely unusual hardship requirements. This was a tactical decision by counsel who then chose to wait until the respondent’s request for rehearing on the denial of his petition for review was denied by the Ninth Circuit before filing the instant motion to reopen. The respondent could have filed a timely motion to remand during the pendency of his appeal or a timely motion to reopen setting forth the facts of his case but did not do so. We do not find that the respondent acted diligently in this case.
 
Accordingly, the following orders shall be entered:
 
ORDER: The motion to reopen is denied.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD

The respondent’s mother became a naturalized United States citizen on January 30, 2004. Her naturalization automatically converted his approved petition from a second preference (2B) family petition to a first preference family petition. The respondent would not be eligible for a second preference (2B) visa at this time because visas are available only for petitions with priority dates of July 1, 1995, or early. 2004 WL 2374885 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ABEL ANTONIO MEJIA-CRUZ
File: AXX XX5 641 - Las Vegas
August 18, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM: The appeal is dismissed. The summary of the Immigration Judge’s oral decision, dated October 28, 2003, noted that the Immigration Judge granted the respondent 4 months voluntary departure. We also note that in the transcript of proceedings, the respondent, represented by counsel, agreed to 4 month period of voluntary departure. One of the regulatory conditions for being granted 120 days voluntary departure in removal proceedings is that the alien “waives appeal of all issues ….” 8 C.F.R. § 1240.26(b)(1)(i)(D). On November 24, 2003, the respondent filed a Notice of Appeal (Form EOIR-26). Upon review, we find that this case is not properly before us. The Immigration Judge’s decision became administratively final upon the respondent’s waiver of the right to appeal, and the Board lacks jurisdiction over this case. See Matter of Shih, 20 I&N Dec. 697 (BIA 1993). The respondent has argued that the decision to waive appeal was not a knowing and intelligent one, because, although his attorney discussed it with him, he did not understand it. Absent valid assertion of ineffective assistance of counsel, per Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), we are forced to conclude that the respondent, having selected his attorney, is bound by his attorney’s actions, especially where he has discussed them with the respondent. Accordingly, as there is nothing now pending before the Board,
 
IT IS ORDERED that the record be returned to the Immigration Court without further Board action.

IN RE: MALDONADO AGUILAR, ROSARIO

*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent has appealed from the Immigration Judge’s decision dated June 05, 2003. We have reviewed the record and we agree that the respondent does not qualify for cancellation of removal because he failed to demonstrate that his United States citizen children would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship as a result of his return to Mexico. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b)(1)(D); Matter of Andazola, 23 I&N Dec. 319 (BIA 2002); Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 63 (2001). We do not find that the Immigration Judge’s denial of a continuance was improper when the respondent’s case had been pending for 5 months and the respondent’s attorney failed to provide an adequate explanation for his absence at the scheduled hearing. See Matter of Perez-Andrade, 19 I&N Dec. 433 (BIA 1987)(the decision to grant or deny a continuance for good cause is within the sound discretion of the Immigration Judge and will not be overturned on appeal unless it is shown that the respondent was denied a full and fair hearing); 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6. We will not grant remand on the basis of ineffective assistance of counsel, as we are not persuaded that the respondent would have prevailed in this matter but for the allegedly ineffective representation of former counsel. See Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). The respondent has presented no arguments which persuade us that this decision should be disturbed. Accordingly, the appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the alien fails to so depart, the alien shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. 2004 WL 2374638 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: KANTA K. MIRCHANDANI
File: AXX XX2 758 - Los Angeles
August 16, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Kevin Tracy, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Sumeet Lall
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. The respondent is a female native and citizen of India. This case was last before us on July 20, 1998, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reconsider our denial of her appeal of an Immigration Judge’s denial of her motion to reopen. In our July 20, 1998, decision, we found that the respondent failed to establish that she was eligible for adjustment of status as she failed to provide a copy of the Approval Notice of the visa petition filed on her behalf. We pointed out that the document attached to her motion to reopen, which the respondent alleged was an Approval Notice of the visa petition, was in fact an Approval Notice of an application for voluntary departure under the Family Unity Program. Thus, the respondent’s motion to reconsider was denied.
 
On April 6, 2004, the respondent filed a second motion to reopen in which she alleges ineffective assistance of counsel and in which she again seeks to apply for adjustment of status. The respondent’s motion to reopen must be denied as both untimely filed and number-barred, as it does not fall within any of the enumerated exceptions to the time and number restrictions on motions to reopen. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (motions to reopen must be filed within 90 days of the date of the Board’s decision and parties are limited to one motion to reopen). We note that the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, in which jurisdiction the instant case arises, recognizes equitable tolling of deadlines and numerical limits on motions to reopen during periods when a petitioner is prevented from filing because of deception, fraud, or error, as long as the petitioner acts with due diligence in discovering the deception, fraud, or error. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 897 (9th Cir. 2003). We find that equitable tolling has not been shown to be applicable in this case as the respondent has not shown that she acted with due diligence after we explicitly enumerated the error at issue in our July 20, 1998, decision. The respondent did not file a motion to reopen until almost 6 years after we issued that decision, and she has not established or claimed that she was unaware of the fact that her last motion had been denied in 1998.
 
Accordingly, the respondent’s motion to reopen is denied.

2004 WL 2374626 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: JORGE HERNANDEZ-CORTEZ
File: AXX XX8 444 - Los Angeles
August 13, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL AND MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Antonio Z. Sandoval, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Monica M. Little
Assistant District Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 212(a)(6)(A)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1182(a)(6)(A)(i)º - Present without being admitted or paroled
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal; remand
 
The respondent appeals from an Immigration Judge’s June 16, 2003, decision pretermitting his application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b), on grounds of abandonment. During the pendency of the appeal, moreover, the respondent filed a motion to remand in which he asserts that his failure to file his cancellation of removal application was due to the ineffective assistance of his former counsel. The appeal will be dismissed and the motion will be denied.
 
It is not disputed that the respondent failed to file his application for cancellation of removal by the deadline set by the Immigration Judge, nor is there any doubt as to whether the respondent was given adequate notice of that deadline. The Immigration Judge has explicit regulatory authority to set a deadline for the filing of applications for relief, and an applicant’s failure to abide by that deadline constitutes an abandonment of the application. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.31(c) (2003). Because the respondent received ample notice of the filing deadline and was given sufficient time to complete his application, we agree with the Immigration Judge that he did not show good cause for a continuance. 8 C.F.R. §§ 1003.29 and 1240.6. Therefore, the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
In his motion to remand, the respondent contends that his former attorney provided ineffective assistance of counsel by failing to timely file his application for cancellation of removal. However, the respondent’s motion does not comply with the requirements of Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), and fails to persuade us that his failure to file the application in a timely manner was due to former counsel’s ineffectiveness. Among other things, Matter of Lozada requires that an alien claiming ineffective assistance of counsel submit an affidavit specifying the nature and scope of former counsel’s representation and either file a complaint against the allegedly-ineffective attorney or provide an adequate explanation for failing to file such a complaint. In this instance the respondent has submitted a sworn declaration in which he states that the precise scope of former counsel’s representation was “never clear” to him and that he was not filing a bar complaint against his former attorney because “¿iºt was not clearly define ¿sicº if ¿former counselº was going to work on the merits of the Immigration matter…” (R’s Motion to Remand, Exh. C). For the reasons stated in Matter of Rivera-Claros, 21 I&N Dec. 599 (BIA 1996), we find the respondent’s explanation for his failure to file a bar complaint against former counsel to be inadequate. Unanswered questions remain regarding the nature of the attorney-client relationship, and the respondent’s failure to file a bar complaint both aggravates these questions and leaves open the possibility of collusion between the respondent and former counsel. Absent the respondent’s full compliance with the Lozada requirements, the degree of confidence we have in his ineffective assistance of counsel claim is lessened to the point where we decline to grant the motion.
 
*2 Furthermore, the respondent is ultimately responsible to be his own advocate in removal proceedings, and if he was in doubt as to whether former counsel’s representation extended to the handling of his immigration case (which would include the filing of his cancellation of removal application), then he was under an obligation either to obtain clarification on that matter prior to his filing deadlines or to file the application himself as an act of prudence. Instead, he simply let matters rest and allowed his deadlines to pass without action. Thus, the respondent’s failure to file the application in a timely manner was necessarily caused at least as much by his own lack of diligence as by former counsel’s ineffective assistance.
 
The following orders shall be issued.
 
ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s motion to remand is denied.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
