2004 WL 2374641 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: VESEL GJEKA
File: AXX XX0 168 - El Paso
August 12, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Jorge Guttlein, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
David Burke
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
APPLICATION: Reopening, termination
  
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on October 9, 2003, when we denied the respondent’s motion to reopen his deportation proceedings for lack of jurisdiction. The respondent has now filed another motion to reopen and to terminate the proceedings, again contending that a new charging instrument should be issued, as well as alleging that he is eligible for adjustment of status based on an approved immediate relative visa petition filed on his behalf by his United States citizen spouse. The respondent’s motion is dismissed, as moot.
 
The record reflects the respondent departed the United States to Canada, and was detained and arrested by the Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) upon his attempted re-entry to the United States. By voluntarily departing the United States to Canada in June 2002, the respondent executed the Immigration Judge’s outstanding order of deportation. Further, 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(d) specifically prohibits our consideration of a motion to reopen or a motion to reconsider, after the respondent departs from the United States. Consequently, in rendering our October 9, 2003, decision, the Board concluded we had no jurisdiction to consider his request and denied the respondent’s motion, as moot. See Matter of Okoh, 20 I&N Dec. 864 (BIA 1994) (the Board has no jurisdiction over an alien’s motion to reconsider an order of deportation after the order has been executed, and the alien has been deported from the United States.)
 
We decline to revisit issues that were raised, addressed, and resolved in our previous order. Furthermore, as noted by counsel, several motions alleging the ineffective assistance of counsel have been filed, considered, and denied, in this case. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d 851 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (setting out the requirements that would support a motion to reopen based on a claim for ineffective assistance of counsel). Moreover, as urged by the DHS in its opposition to the respondent’s motion, ineffective assistance of counsel is not the proximate cause for the respondent’s inability to have his case reopened. It is the respondent’s departure from the United States that renders the respondent ineligible for reopening. The respondent cannot excuse the execution of his order of deportation on a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Pursuant to section 241(a)(5) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1231(a)(5), an alien who attempts to illegally re-enter the United States after having voluntarily left the United States, thereby executing the Immigration Judge’s outstanding order of deportation, is subject to the reinstatement of the prior order of deportation, which is not subject to being reopened or reviewed, and may not apply for any relief under the Act, and shall be removed under the prior order.
 
*2 The United States Circuit Court of Appeals for the Fifth Circuit, the jurisdiction wherein this case arises, has reached the same conclusion that executed orders of deportation underlying a reinstatement order are not subject to review. In Ojeda-Terrazas v. Ashcroft, 290 F.3d 292, 295 (5th Cir. 2002), the Court found that “section 241(a)(5) ¿of the Actº limits that review to the reinstatement order itself; ¿and that theº court cannot ‘reopen or review’ the merits of the ¿underlyingº deportation order.”
 
Therefore, we are without jurisdiction to consider the respondent’s motion. Accordingly, the record shall be returned to the Immigration Judge without further action.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The record is returned to the Immigration Judge without further action.

2004 WL 2374699 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: NUNES, RICARDO EMANUEL
File: AXX XX0 764 - Newark
August 9, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
 
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen and reconsider our decision dated May 13, 2004. Applicable regulations provide that motions to reopen removal proceedings “shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing.…” 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2. The respondent must make a prima facie showing that he is eligible as a matter of law for the relief sought upon reopening. Matter of Rodriguez-Vera, 17 & N Dec. 105 (BIA 1979). A motion to reconsider must state the reasons for reconsideration, be supported by pertinent case precedent and be predicated on a legal or factual defect in the most recent decision. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(b)(1); Matter of Cerna, 20 I&N Dec. 399 (BIA 1991).
 
The respondent’s instant motion is based on (1) our failure to grant his timely-filed motion for an extension in the briefing schedule on his appeal, and (2) a claim that ineffective assistance of counsel in the underlying criminal proceedings led to the imposition of a one-year sentence on a probation violation, and, hence, the respondent’s designation as an aggravated felon under section 101(a)(43)(G) of the Act. At bottom, we assume that the respondent claims that absent the 1-year sentence, he would not have been designated an aggravated felon, that the removal charge would not have been sustained, and that proceedings would have been terminated. As to the first basis for the motion to reopen, the respondent has not made a prima facie showing that he would likely have been granted relief if we had considered his brief on appeal. The respondent has not submitted a copy of the brief or indicated what additional information the appeal brief would have contained beyond the 6-page “Affirmation in Support of the Notice of Appeal” submitted with the appeal on February 11, 2004, which basically raised the ineffective assistance of counsel claim. Notably, the respondent has not indicated that the brief would have included the necessary elements to make a cognizable claim of ineffective assistance of counsel under rules previously articulated by this Board. See Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988) (requiring that the motion be supported by an affidavit by the alien setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel, including relevant representations; that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned be informed of the allegations leveled against him and be given an opportunity to respond: and that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities, and if not, why not). The respondent failed to meet these threshold requirements both in the underlying appeal and in his instant motion. At a minimum, we note that even at this point in these proceedings, the respondent has not submitted an affidavit swearing that his counsel in the criminal proceedings failed to inform him of the immigration consequences of his plea to one of the numerous alleged probation violations. Therefore, even if we were to reopen proceedings, the ineffective assistance of counsel claim would be deficient. In this regard, we reject the respondent’s reliance, in the motion to reopen, on his pending motion in New York state court to set aside his one-year criminal sentence. A pending motion to reduce an underlying criminal sentence falls far short of showing clear evidence of prejudicial ineffective assistance of counsel during the removal proceedings below. See Ponce-Leiva v. Ashcroft, 331 F.3d 369, 377 (3d Cir. 2003).
 
*2 Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2374657 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: LINDA G. MCCALL, BENEFICIARY OF A VISA PETITION FILED BY WILLIAM H. LONG, PETITIONER
File: AXX XX1 767 - California Service Center
August 6, 2004
 
IN VISA PETITION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF PETITIONER: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Robert Raymond,
Associate Legal Advisor
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The appeal is dismissed. The Regional Service Center (RSC) director instructed the petitioner to submit additional evidence in support of the visa petition, detailing the forms of evidence which might be acceptable. The RSC director correctly determined that the petitioner had failed to adequately respond to the request for additional evidence and had consequently failed to establish the claimed relationship between himself and the beneficiary.
 
Specifically, the petitioner failed to provide evidence that his marriage to the beneficiary was registered and/or recorded after the marriage ceremony. See 8 C.F.R. § 204.2(a)(2).
 
On appeal, the respondent apparently alleges ineffective assistance by former counsel. Claims of ineffective assistance of counsel require compliance with Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). In that case, we held that such claims require: (1) that the motion be supported by an affidavit of the allegedly aggrieved respondent setting forth in detail the agreement that was entered into with counsel with respect to the actions to be taken and what representations counsel did or did not make to the respondent in this regard; (2) that counsel whose integrity or competence is being impugned is informed of the allegations leveled against him and given an opportunity to respond; and (3) that the motion reflect whether a complaint has been filed with appropriate disciplinary authorities with respect to any violation of counsel’s ethical or legal responsibilities, and if not, why not. Id. at 639. The Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit, where this case arises, has found the application of the noted requirements to generally be reasonable, but has held that “they need not be rigidly enforced where their purpose is fully served by other means.” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 526 (9th Cir. 2000). Here, however, the respondent has failed to even minimally fulfill any of the above-noted requirements. See Rodriguez-Laris v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002)(substantial compliance with Lozada requirements found adequate). As such, the respondent has failed to show that remand on this basis is warranted.
 
The petitioner may, of course, file a new visa petition on the beneficiary’s behalf that is supported by competent evidence that the beneficiary is entitled to the status sought under the immigration laws.
  
<Signature>
  
FOR THE BOARD
 
2004 WL 2374657 (BIA)
End of Document

KeyCite Yellow Flag - Negative Treatment
    0  On Reconsideration IN RE: ARLIND PRAPANIKU, BIA, November 10, 2004
2004 WL 2374670 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: PRAPANIKU, ARLIND
File: AXX-XX7-471 - LOS ANGELES
August 6, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Angleton, James C., Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated January 2l, 2004. The respondent’s motion is based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. We find that the respondent has largely complied with the procedural requirements for reopening set forth in our decision in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). However, we do not find that the respondent has established that he suffered actual prejudice as a result of his attorney’s allegedly ineffective representation. Id. at 640;Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Even if the facts alleged by the respondent are accepted as true, we do not find that the respondent met his burden of establishing past persecution or a well-founded fear of persecution on account of one of the statutorily protected grounds, or that it is more likely than not that he would be persecuted or subjected to torture upon his return to Albania. See section 101(a)(42)(A) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1101(a)(42)(A); INS v. Elias-Zacarias, 502 U.S. 478 (1992); INS v. Cardoza-Fonseca, 480 U.S. 421 (1987); INS v. Stevic, 467 U.S. 407 (1984); 8 C.F.R. § 1208.16(c)(2); Matter of Y-L-, A-G-, & R-S-R, 23 I&N Dec. 270 (A.G. 2002); Matter of S-V-, 22 I&N Dec. 1306 (BIA 2000). Therefore, we are not persuaded that the respondent would have prevailed on appeal but for former counsel’s allegedly negligent representation. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992)(the party that seeks to reopen bears the “heavy burden” of proving that the result in the case would likely change if proceedings were reopened). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2374708 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: EFRAIN MARIO ALVAREZ-SIGUENCIA
File: A28 579 737 - Boston
August 5, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Cynthia R. Exner, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Leigh Mapplebeck
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
On September 22, 1988, an Immigration Judge ordered the respondent’s deportation when he failed to appear for a scheduled deportation hearing. See generally section 242(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1252(b). On March 21, 1991, this Board dismissed the respondent’s appeal from that decision. The respondent now moves the Board to reopen the proceedings; rescind the Immigration Judge’s in absentia order of deportation; and remand the record to the Immigration Judge to enable him to apply for adjustment of status. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) opposes the motion. With the following comments, we will deny the motion.1
 
The Board entered the final order in these proceedings on March 21, 1991. In our previous decision, we concluded that the respondent failed to establish reasonable cause for his failure to appear at a scheduled hearing, and that his failure to appear resulted in the abandonment of any claim to any form of relief from deportation, or any claim to any other potential benefits under the Act.
 
Pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2) (with certain exceptions not pertinent here), a motion to reopen in any case previously the subject of a final decision by the Board must be filed no later than 90 days after the date of that decision, or on or before September 30, 1996, whichever is later. In the instant case, a motion to reopen would have been due on or before September 30, 1996. We did not receive the motion until June 16, 2004, however. The motion to reopen was therefore filed out of time
 
The respondent now claims to have been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel in these proceedings. We emphasize, however, that in our previous decision we noted that the record reflected the respondent’s awareness of the scheduled hearing date. Compare, Matter of N-K- & V-S-, 21 I&N Dec. 879 (BIA 1997) (holding that an attorney’s failure to notify the alien of his hearing date constituted ineffective assistance of counsel, and was a reasonable cause for the alien’s failure to appear). In his motion, the respondent has put forth neither arguments nor evidence which would persuade us to reopen the proceedings on our own motion at this time. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); Matter of J-J-, 20 I&N Dec. 976 (BIA1997).
 
*2 In sum, the motion will be denied as untimely. Accordingly, the following order will be entered.
 
ORDER: The motion is denied.

2004 WL 2374524 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: GISSELLA URSULA BARRANTES
File: AXX XX4 669 - Los Angeles
August 4, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Aggie Dolinska, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - failed to comply with conditions of status
 
Lodged: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM. This case was last before the Board on March 23, 2004, when we dismissed the respondent’s appeal. The respondent has now filed a timely motion to reopen asserting a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. The motion is denied.
 
The respondent did not show that she received ineffective assistance of counsel from either of her prior attorneys. First, the respondent did not fully comply with the requirements outlined in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Although the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit. within whose jurisdiction this matter arises, has held that the Lozada requirements are “not sacrosanct,” the Ninth Circuit has also held that “the Lozada requirements are generally reasonable, and under ordinary circumstances the BIA does not abuse its discretion when it denies a motion to remand or reopen based on alleged ineffective assistance of counsel where the petitioner fails to meet the requirements of Lozada..” Castillo-Perez v. INS, 212 F.3d 518, 525 (9th Cir. 2000).
 
Here, the respondent submitted a complaint to the State Bar of California and an affidavit regarding each attorney from whom she claims she received ineffective assistance of counsel. However, the respondent’s affidavits do not describe “‘in detail the agreement that was entered into with former counsel with respect to the actions to be taken … and what counsel did or did not represent to the respondent in this regard.” Matter of Lozada, supra, at 639. Rather, the respondent describes very generally her agreement with each attorney. With regard to her first attorney, the respondent could not recall whether she hired him before or after the date that the first Notice of Hearing was mailed to the respondent. In addition, the respondent did not submit evidence that she informed either of her prior attorneys of the accusations leveled against them, much less the provision of an opportunity to respond to those accusations. See id.
 
In the absence of an explanation of the representation agreements between the respondent and her prior attorneys, we cannot determine whether the attorneys failed in their duties to the respondent. In this regard, we note that this case does not involve “a clear and obvious case of ineffective assistance of counsel” so as to excuse the respondent from satisfying the formal requirements of Matter of Lozada, supra. Castillo-Perez, supra, at 525-26. We therefore find that the respondent failed to show that she received ineffective assistance from either of her prior attorneys. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(2), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(2) (1988)º - Entered without inspection
  
APPLICATION: Reopening
 
The case was last before the Board on January 14, 1991, when we continued the case indefinitely to allow the respondent an opportunity to pursue temporary protected status. The Department of Homeland Security (“the DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has submitted a motion requesting that the case be reinstated and the respondent’s appeal adjudicated. The DHS’ motion will be granted and the respondent’s appeal will be dismissed.
 
In our decision, dated January 14, 1991, we notified both parties that if they wished further action on the matter, they needed to submit a written request to reinstate proceedings accompanied by certification of service on the opposing party. The DHS has submitted a written request to reinstate proceedings along with certification of service on the respondent. As such, the DHS has complied with our decision dated January 14, 1991, and we will grant the DHS’ motion to reinstate proceedings. As a result, we will now adjudicate the respondent’s appeal.
 
On appeal, the respondent asserts that he was advised not to attend his hearing by counsel. Thus, the respondent’s argument will be construed as an ineffective assistance of counsel argument. However, the record reflects that the respondent was not represented by counsel at the time of his immigration hearing on March 18, 1988. In fact, a Form G-28 (Notice of Entry of Appearance as Attorney or Representative) was not filed with the Immigration Court until December 9, 1988. Moreover, the respondent has not established a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel pursuant to Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988). Thus, the respondent’s appeal is without merit.
 
ORDER: The DHS’ motion to reinstate proceedings is granted.
 
FURTHER ORDER: The respondent’s appeal is dismissed.

IN RE: ARUN DIXIT JAYANTI DIXIT ADIT DIXIT
Files: AXX XX8 744 - San Francisco
AXX-XX8-745
AXX XX8 746
August 4, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Charles V. Stebley, Esquire
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Kevin R. Lashus
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Notice: Sec. 237(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (AXX XXX XXX, AXX XXX XXX)
Sec. 237(a)(1)(C)(i), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1227(a)(1)(C)(i)º - Nonimmigrant - violated conditions of status (AXX XXX XXX)
 
  
APPLICATION: Cancellation of removal
 
The respondents appeal an Immigration Judge’s April 16, 2003, denial of their application for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(b). The appeal is dismissed.
 
Initially, the respondents argue that they were denied effective assistance of counsel with respect to their application for cancellation of removal. Specifically, they contend that the setting of the merits hearing only 17 days after the Master Calendar hearing deprived them of a reasonable opportunity to prepare for that hearing and prior counsel’s failure to object to that date prevented them from providing the Immigration Judge with adequate documentation in support of their claim. However, upon careful review of the record, we conclude that the respondents have failed to demonstrate that their former counsel’s performance was so inadequate that it may have affected the outcome of proceedings. See Iturribarria v. INS, 321 F.3d 889, 894-97 (9th Cir. 2003). The respondent’s former counsel’s actions in failing to object to the setting of the merits hearing 17 days after the Master Calendar hearing does not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel. See e.g. Magallanes-Damian v. INS, 783 F.2d 931 (9th Cir. 1986)(holding that an attorney’s decision to forego contesting deportability was a tactical decision that did not rise to the level of ineffective assistance of counsel). Indeed, the record reflects that the respondents submitted numerous documents and also testified in detail in support of their claims. In the absence of some other significant shortcoming, we cannot conclude that former counsel’s actions in representing the respondents was so deficient as to negatively impact the outcome of proceedings. In addition, with respect to the documents submitted with the respondents’ appeal brief, because our review is limited to the record as it was constituted before the Immigration Judge, we do not consider the evidence submitted on appeal. Matter of Federenko, 19 I&N Dec. 57, 74 (BIA 1984).
 
Turning to the merits of the respondents’ claims, we note that continuous physical presence and good moral character are not at issue in this case. See section 240A(b)(1) of the Act. Accordingly, the dispositive issue on appeal is whether the respondents have demonstrated that their United States citizen children would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if the respondents are ordered removed to Nepal. See section 240A(b)(1)(D) of the Act.
 
*2 To establish “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship,” an applicant for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act must demonstrate that his or her spouse, parent, or child would suffer hardship that is substantially beyond that which would ordinarily be expected to result from the alien’s deportation, but need not show that such hardship would be “unconscionable.” Matter of Monreal, 23 I&N Dec. 56 (BIA 2001). The Board has held that although many of the factors that were considered in assessing “extreme hardship” for suspension of deportation should also be considered in evaluating “exceptional and extremely unusual hardship,” an applicant for cancellation of removal must demonstrate hardship beyond that which has historically been required in suspension of deportation cases involving the “extreme hardship” standard. Id.; cf. Matter of O-J-O-, 21 I&N Dec. 381 (BIA 1996); Matter of Anderson, 16 I&N Dec. 596 (BIA 1978). In establishing eligibility for cancellation of removal, only hardship to qualifying relatives, not to the applicant himself or herself, may be considered, and hardship factors relating to the applicant may be considered only insofar as they might affect the hardship to a qualifying relative. Matter of Monreal, supra. Moreover, among the factors to be considered in assessing hardship to a cancellation applicant’s children are poor economic conditions and diminished educational opportunities in the country of removal and whether the applicant has family members in that country to assist in his or her adjustment upon return. Matter of Andazola, 23 I&N Dec. 319 (BIA 2002).
 
We concur with the Immigration Judge that the respondents have not demonstrated that any hardship their United States citizen children may suffer would rise to the level of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if they are removed from the United States. The qualifying children were 6 and 5 years old at the time of the hearing. The 6 year old speaks some Napalese as well as English and has been diagnosed with asthma. The younger child, although in good health, apparently is in need of some special assistance in her schooling and has repeated kindergarten. See Matter of Monreal, supra (stating that cancellation may be appropriate where a qualifying child has “very serious health issues” or “special needs in school”); Matter of O-J-O-, supra (noting, with respect to suspension of deportation, that an alien’s health and physical condition, including the existence of any serious medical conditions, are factors to be evaluated in determining whether deportation will result in extreme hardship). However, careful review of the record fails to reveal any significant medical evidence reflecting that the older child’s asthma requires specialized treatment or medication or otherwise is considered to be a serious medical problem which could not be adequately treated in Nepal. Moreover, there is no objective evidence that the younger child manifests a learning disability of such severity as to require a specialized educational setting or other intervention shown to be unavailable in Nepal. When viewed in light of the fact that the respondents have no family ties in this country; both adults are highly educated and have transferable skills, they possess significant financial resources including equity in their home estimated to be $190,000, coupled with the fact that they have numerous close relatives in Nepal including the male respondent’s father, mother and brother who also can assist in their reestablishing themselves, the respondents have failed to demonstrate that removal will result in exceptional and extremely unusual hardship based solely upon either their older child’s asthma or the younger child’s learning difficulties.
 
*3 Furthermore, the Board recognizes that Nepal likely will not provide the respondents’ children with an education equal to that which they might obtain in the United States. Nonetheless, the respondents have failed to show that their children would be deprived of all schooling or of an opportunity to obtain any education. See Matter of Andazola, supra, at 323. In addition, although they have argued that their children would face financial hardship in Nepal, as noted above, the respondents have significant financial resources. Moreover, the Board has previously held that a lowered standard of living or reduced economic opportunities generally are insufficient to establish a finding of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship. See Matter of Andazola, supra (noting a lower standard of living in alien’s home country is insufficient to establish eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b)).
 
We note that a deportable alien “cannot gain favored status by the ¿mereº birth of a citizen child,” see Ramirez-Durazo v. INS, 794 F.2d 491, 489-99 (9th Cir. 1986); however, where hardship to the alien’s citizen’s children is extreme, the United States Court of Appeals for the Ninth Circuit has held in suspension of deportation cases that this alone may warrant suspension of their parent’s deportation. See Cerrillo-Perez v. INS, 809 F.2d 1419, 1422 (9th Cir. 1987). The Ninth Circuit has further held that “the most important single (hardship) factor may be the separation of the alien from family living in the United States.” Contreras-Buenfil v. INS, 712 F.2d 401, (9th Cir. 1983). In fact, where the Board has failed to give this factor “considerable, if not predominant weight,” it has abused its discretion. Gutierrez-Centeno v. INS, 99 F.3d 1529, 1533 (9th Cir. 1996). In this case, the respondents have indicated that their children will accompany them to Nepal and as such, family separation is not at issue.
 
In sum, the evidence of record does not indicate that whatever difficulties the respondents’ children might face in Nepal would rise to the exceptional and extremely unusual standard. See Matter of Andazola, supra (holding that the respondent, an unmarried mother, did not establish eligibility for cancellation where she failed to demonstrate that her 6- and 11-year-old children will suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship upon her removal to Mexico); Matter of Monreal, supra; see also Matter of Pilch, 21 I&N Dec. 627, 631 (BIA 1996) (holding, with respect to suspension of deportation, that cultural adjustment alone does not constitute extreme hardship). Furthermore, whatever financial difficulties the children might face are not likely to be significantly different from those occasioned by deportation generally, particularly to a country with a weaker economy and comparatively fewer employment opportunities. Adjustment to a lower standard of living generally will be insufficient, in and of itself, to support a finding of exceptional and extremely unusual hardship. Matter of Monreal, supra.
 
*4 Therefore, even considering all of the factors presented cumulatively, we find that the respondents have not met their burden of establishing that their United States citizen children would suffer exceptional and extremely unusual hardship if they are removed. The respondents failed to provide evidence that his children would suffer hardship that is substantially different from, or beyond, that which would normally be expected from the deportation of an alien with close family members here. See id.; see also Matter of Andazola, supra. Accordingly, they have not demonstrated eligibility for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act.
 
ORDER: The appeal is dismissed.
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent filed a motion to reopen on April 29, 2004. We dismissed the respondent’s appeal on November 20, 2002. We denied a motion to reopen as untimely on May 30, 2003. We reconsidered this order on January 30, 2004, then again denied the motion to reopen on the merits. The pending motion to reopen was filed more than 90 days after our final order of November 20, 2002. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c)(2). Moreover, the motion exceeds the numerical limitations for motions to reopen. Id. The Department of Homeland Security (the “DHS,” formerly the Immigration and Naturalization Service) has not responded to the pending motion, which will be denied. Although the respondent makes claims against former counsel, who has been suspended from practice before the Board, he does not claim that prior counsel failed to promptly notify him as to the Board’s January 30, 2004, decision. Given that any motion filed after that point would have been late, the respondent fails to explain adequately why he was not able to submit the pending motion until April 29, 2004. This is particularly so given that the respondent has failed to comply with the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003), and Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). Most significantly, the respondent has not provided former counsel with a chance to respond to the charges. The pending motion is, therefore, denied.

2004 WL 2374605 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: TCHAKOUNTE, EBENEZER
File: AXX XX9 504 - Baltimore
August 4, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Patrick Tzeuton, Esquire
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. This case was last before us on June 9, 2004, when we adopted and affirmed the results of the Immigration Judge’s decision dated May 1, 2003. On July 7, 2004, the respondent filed a motion to reopen for adjustment of status. The motion is denied.
 
The regulations provide that motions to reopen removal proceedings “shall not be granted unless it appears to the Board that evidence sought to be offered is material and was not available and could not have been discovered or presented at the former hearing ….” 8 C.F.R. § 3.2. This Board has held that a party who seeks to reopen proceedings to pursue a discretionary grant of relief from deportation bears a “heavy burden” of demonstrating that if his motion to reopen were granted, the new evidence presented would likely change the result in the case. Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992). We find the respondent’s motion must be denied for failure to submit evidence which is material and was not available or could not have been discovered or presented at the respondent’s former hearing. The respondent contends the documents were available to his former counsel but were inadvertently not submitted by his former counsel. However, the respondent’s contentions concerning his former counsel’s alleged ineffective assistance are not substantiated by an affidavit or other evidentiary material. Statements made in a motion are not evidence and are thus not entitled to any evidentiary weight. See INS v. Phinpathya, 464 U.S. 183, 188-89 n.6 (1984); Matter of Ramirez-Sanchez, 17 I&N Dec. 503 (BIA 1980).
 
Moreover, the respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. Accordingly, the motion is denied

IN RE: SEPULVEDA-AGUIRRE, FRANCISCO TORRES-GUERRERO, ROSA ELENA

ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge for the reasons stated therein. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondents raise an ineffective assistance of counsel claim on appeal. The respondents have failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (B1A 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002)(Lozada requirements need not always be rigidly applied); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331,1335 (9th Cir. 2000), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000) (application of Lozada requirements generally reasonable unless facts relating to claim of ineffective assistance of counsel are apparent in the record). Finally, the respondents have not established that they were prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondents are permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondents fail to so depart, the respondents shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the respondents fail to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondents shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief-under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.

2004 WL 2374560 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MOALA, VILIAMI
File: AXX-XX1-979 - DENVER
August 3, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Nwadibia, Rochelle A. Fortier
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated June 13, 2001. Although entitled one for reconsideration, the respondent’s motion is correctly characterized as one to reopen as she seeks to introduce new, previously unavailable evidence. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(c). The motion is time-barred as it was filed on March 31, 2004, far more than 90 days after the Board’s June 13, 2001, decision. Id. The motion is also number-barred as it is the second such motion filed by the respondent. Id. The respondent has submitted an order from a district court in conjunction with her motion which reduces her conviction for theft from a third degree felony to a class A misdemeanor. However, this record does not identify on what basis the respondent’s conviction was reduced. As such, we are unable to ascertain whether the state court action was taken pursuant to a rehabilitative statute or to ameliorate the immigration consequences of the conviction. See Matter of Pickering, 23 I&N Dec. 621 (BIA 2003) (holding that the vacating of an alien’s conviction for possession of a controlled substance for the sole purpose of avoiding the bar to his acquisition of permanent residence did not eliminate the conviction for immigration purposes). As the respondent’s conviction remains final for immigration purposes, the respondent has not shown that she is prima facie eligible for the relief she seeks and we do not find that an exercise of our sua sponte authority to reopen is warranted. 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2(a); see also Matter of Rodriguez-Ruiz, 22 I&N Dec. 1378 (BIA 2000). Finally, the respondent vaguely asserts that she has an unidentified “claim for ineffective assistance of counsel,” but has not complied with any of the requirements for such a claim as enumerated in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988). See also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Therefore, reopening on that basis is not warranted. Accordingly, the motion is denied.

2004 WL 2374526 (BIA)
 
** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: MEDINA-MEDINA, JOSE PRESCILIANO
File: AXX-XX7-858 - SAN FRANCISCO
August 2, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
MOTION
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT:
 
Van Der Hout, Marc, Esq.
 
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Evan R. Franke,
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. The respondent moves the Board pursuant to 8 C.F.R. § 1003.2 to reopen our decision dated September 24, 2002. The respondent’s motion has been untimely filed, but the respondent argues for equitable tolling of the filing deadline based upon a claim of ineffective assistance of counsel. Specifically, he alleges that former counsel failed to inform him that he could present witnesses to establish continuous physical presence in the United States, and that former counsel failed to present evidence of the respondent’s health problems in support of his application for suspension of deportation.
 
Equitable tolling requires a showing of actual prejudice arising from the allegedly ineffective representation of former counsel. See Munoz v. Ashcroft, 339 F.3d 950 (9th Cir. 2003); see also Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003). Given the respondent’s submission of affidavits in lieu of absent witnesses, we find it difficult to believe that the respondent was unaware that he could present witnesses at the hearing. Moreover, we note that the absence of certain witnesses is but one factor cited by the Immigration Judge; the Immigration Judge also cited the absence of corroborating documentation in finding that the respondent had failed to prove continuous physical presence. With respect to the respondent’s health problems, we note that former counsel has denied the respondent’s allegation of deficient or inadequate performance, asserting that the respondent disclosed no health problems during the preparation of his case, and also noting that the respondent failed to mention any health concerns at the hearing even when specifically asked about his ability to pay for medical care (Tr. at 27). The evidence submitted by the respondent on appeal shows that the respondent incurred modest emergency room charges on June 13, 1997, but there is no indication as to the nature of the emergency room visit. Laboratory reports dating from August 1997 only reflect elevated blood glucose and cholesterol levels. A physician’s note from June 1998 indicates that the respondent was not diagnosed with diabetes until December 1997. We note that no complications of diabetes are mentioned in the respondent’s chart prior to the date of the respondent’s hearing, and that the only restrictions placed on the respondent were dietary in nature. In sum,. the evidence presented does not reveal whether the respondent actually suffered from a particularly serious medical condition at the time of the hearing. Based on the foregoing, we are unable to determine that former counsel’s representation was deficient in any respect, and we are not persuaded that the respondent would have prevailed on appeal but for former counsel’s alleged failure to introduce the additional evidence now proffered. See Matter of Coelho, 20 I&N Dec. 464 (BIA 1992)(the party that seeks to reopen bears the “heavy burden” of proving that if proceedings were reopened, the new evidence would likely change the result in the case). Accordingly, the motion is denied.

U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: ISAIAS SANDOVAL CADENA AMALIA VICENTE FRANCO MARIANA SANDOVAL VICENTE
Files: AXX XX8 696 - Portland
AXX XX8 697
AXX XX8 698
August 2, 2004
 
IN DEPORTATION PROCEEDINGS
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENTS:
 
Philip Hornik, Esquire
 
CHARGE:
 
*1 Order: Sec. 241(a)(1)(B), I&N Act ¿8 U.S.C. § 1251(a)(1)(B)º - In the United States in violation of law (all respondents)
  
APPLICATION: Administrative closure; suspension of deportation
  
ORDER:
 
PER CURIAM: The respondents have appealed the decision of an Immigration Judge denying them consideration for suspension of deportation. They also requested on appeal that the Board administratively close these proceedings to allow them an opportunity to seek cancellation of removal in removal proceedings. These proceedings will be administratively closed.
 
Pursuant to section 309(c)(3) of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Pub. L. No.104-208, 110 Stat. 3009-546 (1996), the Attorney General may elect to terminate deportation proceedings, in which there has not been a final administrative decision, and reinstate the proceedings as removal proceedings. See also section 203(a)(1) of the Nicaraguan Adjustment and Central American Relief Act, Pub. L. No. 105-100, 111 Stat. 2193 (1997), amended by Pub. L. No. 105-139, 111 Stat. 2644 (1997). This allows non-lawful permanent resident aliens, who are ineligible for suspension of deportation due to the stop-time provision of section 240A(d)(1) of the Immigration and Nationality Act, 8 U.S.C. § 1229b(d)(1), to be placed in removal proceedings, where they may apply for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) of the Act. However, those individuals with a final administrative decision in their case are not eligible for administrative closure of their cases.
 
It appears from the record that the aliens in the case before us may be eligible for cancellation of removal under section 240A(b) as noted above. Accordingly, the proceedings before the Board in this case are administratively closed.
 
If either party to this case objects to the administrative closure of these proceedings, a written request to reinstate the proceedings may be made to the Board. The Board will take no further action in the case unless a request is received from one of the parties. If removal proceedings are not initiated, the party seeking reinstatement must submit the request directly to the Board at the above address, without fee, but with a certification of service on the opposing party. If properly submitted, the Board shall reinstate the proceedings. The final administrative decision was issued by the Board on August 17, 2000. Accordingly, for the foregoing reasons the respondents’ motions will be denied.
 
*2 The Board has considered the respondents’ argument that the negligence of their former counsel prejudiced them because they would have been able to apply for suspension of deportation prior to the enactment of the stop-time rule had counsel not unduly delayed their case. Notwithstanding, we find that the stop-time provision applies to orders to show cause issued prior to the effective date of the Illegal Immigration Reform and Immigrant Responsibility Act of 1996, Division C of Pub. L. No. 104-208, 110 Stat. 3009 (“IIRIRA”), despite the fact that the respondent may have been the victim of ineffective assistance of counsel prior to the enactment of the stop-time rule. See generally Najjar v. Ashcroft, 257 F.3d 1262 (11th Cir. 2001).

** THIS IS AN UNPUBLISHED DECISION - NOT INTENDED FOR CITATION AS PRECEDENT **
 
U.S. Department of Justice
Executive Office for Immigration Review
Board of Immigration Appeals
IN RE: OROZCO OROZCO, JOSE SANTOS
File: AXX XX0 313 - LOS ANGELES
July 30, 2004
 
IN REMOVAL PROCEEDINGS:
  
APPEAL
 
 
ON BEHALF OF RESPONDENT: Pro se
  
ON BEHALF OF DHS:
 
Ana Partida
Assistant Chief Counsel
 
ORDER:
 
*1 PER CURIAM. We adopt and affirm the decision of the Immigration Judge finding the respondent had not established good cause to have his hearing continued on March 20, 2003. See 8 C.F.R. § 1003.29. See Matter of Burbano, 20 I&N Dec. 872, 874 (BIA 1994) (noting that adoption or affirmance of a decision of an Immigration Judge, in whole or in part, is “simply a statement that the Board’s conclusions upon review of the record coincide with those which the Immigration Judge articulated in his or her decision”). The respondent raises an ineffective assistance of counsel claim on appeal. The respondent has failed to meet the requirements for making an ineffective assistance of counsel claim as set forth in Matter of Lozada, 19 I&N Dec. 637 (BIA 1988), aff’d, 857 F.2d 10 (1st Cir. 1988), which requires that the alien must lay out, in a detailed affidavit, the agreement between the alien and the representative; the alien must give the representative the opportunity to respond to the allegation of ineffectiveness, and the alien must file a complaint with the appropriate disciplinary authority or adequately explain why no filing was made. See Rodriguez-Lariz v. INS, 282 F.3d 1218 (9th Cir. 2002) (Lozada requirements need not always be rigidly applied); Escobar-Grijalva v. INS, 206 F.3d 1331,1335 (9th Cir. 2000), amended by 213 F.3d 1221 (9th Cir. 2000) (application of Lozada requirements generally reasonable unless facts relating to claim of ineffective assistance of counsel are apparent in the record). Finally, the respondent has not established that he was prejudiced by any ineffective assistance of counsel. See Melkonian v. Ashcroft, 320 F.3d 1061 (9th Cir. 2003); Matter of Assaad, 23 I&N Dec. 553 (BIA 2003).
 
FURTHER ORDER: Pursuant to the Immigration Judge’s order and conditioned upon compliance with conditions set forth by the Immigration Judge and the statute, the respondent is permitted to voluntarily depart from the United States, without expense to the Government, within 30 days from the date of this order or any extension beyond that time as may be granted by the district director. See section 240B(b) of the Immigration and Nationality Act; 8 C.F.R. §§ 1240.26(c), (f). In the event the respondent fails to so depart, the respondent shall be removed as provided in the Immigration Judge’s order.
 
*2 NOTICE: If the respondent fails to depart the United States within the time period specified, or any extensions granted by the district director, the respondent shall be subject to a civil penalty of not less than $1,000 and not more than $5,000, and shall be ineligible for a period of 10 years for any further relief under section 240B and sections 240A, 245, 248, and 249 of the Immigration and Nationality Act. See section 240B(d) of the Act.
